كتاب السير والجهاد
أحكام السير والجهاد
فإن حاصرناهم، ندعوهم إلى الإسلام، فإن أسلموا وإلا إلى الجزية. أي ندعو الكفار أولاً. أي وإن لم يسلموا. فإن قبلوا فلهم مالنا وعليهم ما علينا ولا نقاتل من لم تبلغه الدعوة إلى الإسلام. من المضار دون العبادات. فقد حاصرناهم: وفي المستخلص ثبت هنا "فصل في كيفية القتال" فإن حاصرناهم أي إذا حاصرنا مدينة أو حصناً في دار الحرب ندعوهم أولاً إلى الإسلام؛ لما روي عن ابن عباس أنه قال: "ما قاتل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قوماً قط إلا دعاهم إلى الإسلام". رواه أحمد، وقوله: "فإن أسلموا" في بعض النسخ جواب الشرط محذوف أي تم المراد ولا يتعرض لهم بعد الإسلام لقوله عليه: "أمرت أن أقاتل الناس حتى يقولوا لا إله إلا الله، فإذا قالوها عصموا مني دمائهم وأموالهم إلا بحقها"، ولابد أن يؤمنوا به عليه وإنما اكتفي في الحديث بكلمة التوحيد؛ لأنهم كانوا يعتقدون الشرك، فإذا وحدوا علم بذلك أنهم آمنوا بمحمد عليه؛ لأنهم لم يعرفوا ذلك إلا منه عليه. [تبيين الحقائق: 84/4] بخلاف اليهود والنصارى؛ لأنهم يقولون إن محمداً عليه رسول الله إلى العرب دوننا فلا يحكم بإسلامهم ما لم يقراً برسالته ويتبرءوا من دينهم. [عيني، مستخلص] أي تم المراد وكففنا عن قتالهم. وإلا إلى الجزية [أي ندعوهم إلى أداء الجزية لو محلا لها. (ط) عيني] أي وإن لم يسلموا ندعوهم إلى أداء الجزية؛ لما روي أنه عليه كان إذا أمر أميراً على الجيش أو السرية أمره به في حديث فيه طول، رواه أحمد ومسلم والترمذي وصححه، وهذا في حق من تقبل منه الجزية كأهل الكتاب والمجوس أو عبدة الأوثان من العجم، وأما من لا تقبل الجزية منه كالمرتدين وعبدة الأوثان من العرب، فلا ندعوهم إلى أداء الجزية؛ لعدم الفائدة؛ إذ لا يقبل منهم إلا الإسلام فنقاتلهم إلى أن يسلموا ونقاتل من تقبل عنه إلى قبول أدائها؛ لأن بها ينتهي القتال؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿ حَتَّى يُعْطُوا الْجِزْيَةَ عَنْ يَدٍ وَهُمْ صَاغِرُونَ ﴾ (التوبة:29) (عيني، مستخلص) فإن قبلوا فلهم إلخ: أي إن قبلوا أداء الجزية، فلهم ما لنا من عصمة دمائهم وأموالهم ويلزم عليهم ما يلزم علينا؛ لقول علي ه: إنما بذلوا الجزية لتكون دماؤهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأموالنا، ومراده بالبذل القبول، بالإعطاء المذكور في الآية، والمعنى يجب لهم علينا ويجب لنا عليهم إذا تعرضنا لدمائهم وأموالهم أو تعرضوا لدمائنا وأموالنا ما يجب لبعضنا على بعض عند التعرض، فقبول الجزية ليس إلا لزوال هذا التعرض الذي كنا نتعرض قبل قبولها، وليس المراد أنه يجب عليهم من العبادات ما يجب علينا وكذا من الكفارات؛ لأن الكفار لا يخاطبون في العبادات، بل المراد وجوب القصاص والقطع في السرقة وغير ذلك. (مستخلص، فتح) ولا نقاتل إلخ: الدعوة بفتح الدال في الحرب، وكذا في الطعام، وأما في النسب فبكسر الدال، كذا لكافة العرب إلا عدي الرباب، فإنهم يكسرون دعوة الطعام، ويفتحون في النسب، وقيل الفتح في الطعام، والضم في الحرب، والكسر في النسب، أي لا يجوز لنا أن نقاتل من لم تبلغه الدعوة إلى الإسلام؛ لقوله عليه في وصية أمراء الجيش: "ادعهم إلى شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله"؛ ولأنهم بالدعوة يعلمون أنا نقاتلهم للدين لا على سبيل الأموال، ولعلهم يجيبون فيحصل المقصود بلا قتال ومن قاتلهم قبل الدعوة يأثم للنهي عنه ولا يغرم؛ خلافاً للشافعي، والمراد بالنهي قوله عليه لعلي ه: "لا تقاتل حتى تدعوهم إلى الإسلام"، ومطلق الدعوة شامل للحقيقية والحكمية، وهي انتشار الدعوة شرقاً وغرباً فأقيم ظهورها مقامها، وإذا بلغهم الإسلام فلا ينبغي لنا أن نقاتلهم حتى ندعوهم إلى الجزية (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) وندعو ندباً من بلغته لتكون الدعوة مبالغة في الإنذار وهذا مستحب ليس بواجب لما روي عن البراء بن عازب أنه قال: "بعث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم رهطا من الأنصار إلى أبي رافع، فدخل عبد الله بن عتيك بيته ليلاً، وهو نائم". رواه أحمد والبخاري؛ ولأن النبي عليه أغار على بني المصطلق وهم غافلون، والغارة لا تكون بدعوة، وفي "المحيط": تقديم الدعوة إلى الإسلام على القتال كان في ابتداء الإسلام حين لم ينتشر الإسلام ولم يستفض وأما بعد ما انتشر واستفاض، وعرف كل مشرك إلى ماذا يدعى يحل له القتال قبل الدعوة؛ لأنهم ربما يستحصنون بالدعوة فلا يقدر عليهم [رمز الحقائق: 418/1] (مستخلص) نستعين بالله تعالى: أي تطلب العون من الله سبحانه وتعالى عليهم. (ع، معدن) ونحاربهم بنصب المجانيق [جمع منجنيق وهي آلة ترمى بها الأحجار أي نقيمها على حصونهم وأسوار مدائنهم. (ط، ع)] لما روى الترمذي أن النبي نصب المنجنيق على أهل طائف. وقوله: "وحرقهم"، أي ونحاربهم أيضاً بالحرق بالنار؛ لأن النبي عليه أحرق بويرة، بضم الموحدة موضع من بلد بني النضير، وكذا نحاربهم بالغرق بإرسال المياه عليهم، وقطع الأشجار سواء كانت مثمرة أو غير مثمرة، وقد صح أن النبي عليه قطع أشجار بني النضير، وفيه قوله تعالى: ﴿مَا قَطَعْتُمْ مِنْ لِينَةٍ أَوْ تَرَكْتُمُوهَا قَائِمَةً عَلَى أُصُولِهَا فَبِإِذْنِ اللَّهِ ﴾ (الحشر:5) الآية، وكذلك نحاربهم بالرمي بالنبال والحجارة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَعِدُّوا لَهُمْ مَا اسْتَطَعْتُمْ مِنْ قُوَّةٍ (الأنفال: 60) وقد فسر النبي عليه القوة بالرمي؛ لأن في هذه الأشياء إلحاق الكبت والغيظ بهم وتفريق شملهم، وهذا كله بالإجماع إلا في رواية للشافعي وأحمد نفعل بهم ما يفعلونه بنا. (مستخلص، عيني) غرقهم بتسبيب المياه على دورهم وأنفسهم. (عيني) وإفساد زروعهم بإرسال الدواب فيها أو إطلاق النار ونحو ذلك. (عيني) وإن تترسوا واصل بما قبله يعني يجوز رميهم وإن كانوا تترسوا بنا أي جعلوا بعضنا بمنزلة الترس. (معدن)
Book of Campaigns and Jihad. Rulings of Campaigns and Jihad. If we besiege them, we call them to Islam; if they accept [Islam, well and good], otherwise [we call them] to jizya. That is, we call the disbelievers first. That is, if they do not accept Islam. If they accept, they have what we have and upon them what is upon us, and we do not fight those whom the call to Islam has not reached. [This refers to] worldly matters, not worship. When we besiege them: in al-Mustakhlas it is recorded: 'Section on the manner of fighting.' If we besiege them, meaning if we besiege a city or fortress in the abode of war, we first call them to Islam, due to what is narrated from Ibn Abbas that he said: 'The Messenger of Allah never fought a people except that he called them to Islam.' Reported by Ahmad. His statement 'If they accept Islam' – in some copies, the conditional response is omitted, meaning: the desired objective is achieved, and they are not to be harmed after Islam, due to his statement: 'I have been commanded to fight people until they say La ilaha illa Allah, and when they say it, they protect from me their blood and property except by its right.' And it is necessary that they believe in him, but the hadith sufficed with the word of tawhid because they used to believe in shirk, so when they proclaimed tawhid, it was known that they believed in Muhammad, because they only knew that from him. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 4/84] Contrary to Jews and Christians, because they say 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah only to the Arabs, not to us,' so they are not judged to be Muslims unless they affirm his prophethood and disavow their religion. [Ayni, Mustakhlas] That is, the desired objective is achieved and we refrain from fighting them. Otherwise [we call them] to jizya: i.e., we call them to pay jizya if they are eligible for it. (T, Ayni) That is, if they do not accept Islam, we call them to pay jizya, due to the narration that he, when appointing an amir over an army or a raiding party, would instruct him accordingly in a lengthy hadith, reported by Ahmad, Muslim, and al-Tirmidhi who declared it authentic. This applies to those from whom jizya is accepted, such as People of the Book, Magians, or idol worshipers of non-Arab origin. As for those from whom jizya is not accepted, such as apostates and Arab idol worshipers, we do not call them to pay jizya because there is no benefit; only Islam is accepted from them, so we fight them until they accept Islam. And we fight those from whom jizya is accepted until they accept payment of it, because that ends the fighting, due to the verse: 'Until they pay jizya out of hand while they are humbled' (9:29). (Ayni, Mustakhlas) 'If they accept' etc.: That is, if they accept payment of jizya, they have what we have of protection of their blood and property, and what is obligatory upon us becomes obligatory upon them, due to the statement of Ali: 'They only pay jizya so that their blood becomes like our blood and their property like our property.' He meant by 'payment' acceptance, i.e., the giving mentioned in the verse. The meaning is: what is obligatory for us against them becomes obligatory for us regarding them if we attack their blood or property or they attack ours, like what is obligatory for some of us against others when attacked. Accepting jizya is only for the removal of this attack that we were making before accepting it. It does not mean that acts of worship become obligatory upon them as upon us, nor expiations, because disbelievers are not addressed concerning worship. Rather, it means the obligation of retaliation, amputation for theft, etc. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'And we do not fight' etc.: Da'wa with fatha on the dal in war, likewise in food; as for lineage, it is with kasra. Thus for all Arabs except the tribe of Rabab, who break the da'wa of food and open for lineage. It is said: fatha in food, dammah in war, kasra in lineage. That is, it is not permissible for us to fight those whom the call to Islam has not reached, due to his statement in the instruction to commanders: 'Call them to the testimony that there is no god but Allah.' And because by the call they know that we fight for religion, not for plunder, and perhaps they will respond and the objective will be achieved without fighting. Whoever fights them before the call commits a sin due to the prohibition, but does not pay blood money, contrary to al-Shafi'i. The prohibition refers to his statement to Ali: 'Do not fight until you call them to Islam.' The absolute call includes both actual and constructive call, which is the spread of the call east and west, so its prevalence stands in its stead. If Islam has reached them, it is not appropriate for us to fight them until we call them to jizya. (Mustakhlas, Fath, Ayni) And we call – as a recommendation – those whom it has reached, so that the call is an intensification of warning. This is recommended, not obligatory, due to what is narrated from al-Bara' ibn 'Azib: 'The Messenger of Allah sent a group of Ansar to Abu Rafi', and 'Abdullah ibn 'Atik entered his house by night while he was sleeping.' Reported by Ahmad and al-Bukhari. And because the Prophet attacked Banu al-Mustaliq while they were unaware, and a raid is not preceded by a call. In al-Muhit: 'Presenting the call to Islam before fighting was at the beginning of Islam when Islam had not spread and become widespread. As for after it spread and became widespread, and every polytheist knows what he is called to, it is permissible to fight before the call, because they might fortify themselves with the call and then cannot be overcome.' (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/418) (Mustakhlas) 'We seek help from Allah': that is, we seek aid from Allah against them. (Ayni, Madan) 'And we fight them by setting up mangonels' [plural of manjaniq, a device for throwing stones], i.e., we erect them against their fortresses and city walls. (T, Ayni) Because al-Tirmidhi narrated that the Prophet set up a mangonel against the people of Ta'if. His statement: 'and burning them,' i.e., we also fight them by burning with fire, because the Prophet burned the Buwayrah, a place in the land of Banu al-Nadir. Likewise, we fight them by drowning by sending water upon them, cutting down trees whether fruitful or not, and it is authentically established that the Prophet cut down the trees of Banu al-Nadir, and about that is the verse: 'Whatever palm-trees you cut down or left standing on their roots, it was by Allah's permission' (59:5). Likewise, we fight them by shooting with arrows and stones, due to the verse: 'And prepare against them whatever you can of strength' (8:60), and the Prophet interpreted 'strength' as shooting. Because in these things there is infliction of humiliation and rage upon them and dispersal of their unity. All this is by consensus, except in a narration from al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: 'We do to them what they do to us.' (Mustakhlas, Ayni) 'Drowning them': by causing water to flow over their houses and themselves. (Ayni) 'And spoiling their crops': by sending animals into them or setting fire, etc. (Ayni) 'And if they take cover' (tatarrasu): it is connected to what precedes, meaning it is permissible to shoot at them even if they take cover with us, i.e., they make some of us like a shield. (Madan)
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the procedure of jihad. It opens with the obligation to call the enemy to Islam before fighting, citing the hadith of Ibn Abbas. If they accept, they are protected. If they refuse, they are called to jizya. The commentary clarifies that this call is only for those eligible for jizya (e.g., People of the Book, Magians, non-Arab idolaters); Arab idolaters and apostates must accept Islam or be fought. When jizya is accepted, the enemy gains protection of life and property, but only in worldly matters—not in acts of worship, as disbelievers are not obligated in worship. The text then discusses the ruling on fighting those who have not received the da'wa: it is prohibited, and one who fights them sins but does not pay diyah, contra Shafi'i. However, the commentary notes that this was early Islam; after Islam spread widely, the call is no longer obligatory before attack, as it is considered constructive notice. Later, the text addresses permissible means of war: setting up mangonels, burning, drowning, cutting trees, shooting, and spoiling crops—all supported by hadith and Quran. Even if the enemy uses Muslim shields (tatarru), it is permissible to shoot, because repelling the greater harm (the enemy's attack) takes precedence.

Key Terms
جزية (jizya): A tax paid by eligible non-Muslims in exchange for protection and exemption from fighting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT eligible to pay jizya?
A) Jews and Christians
B) Magians (Zoroastrians)
C) Non-Arab idol worshipers
D) Apostates

Key Takeaway
Jizya is accepted only from eligible non-Muslims and grants protection in worldly matters, not in worship.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules
intermediate

Permissible Means of Warfare and the Ruling on Human Shields
A ruling on permissible means of warfare and the exceptional case of human shields.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the permissible means of warfare according to the Hanafi school and apply the ruling on tatarru (human shields).

Core Idea
All means that inflict harm on the enemy are permissible by consensus; even if the enemy uses Muslim shields, targeting is allowed.

Warfare inevitably involves destruction. The Hanafi school, based on prophetic practice, permits a wide range of means: mangonels, burning, drowning, cutting trees, shooting, and spoiling crops. Crucially, even when the enemy uses Muslims as human shields (tatarru), it is permissible to shoot because repelling the greater harm (the enemy attack) takes precedence over the individual lives of the Muslims used as shields.

Guided Path
1
List the permissible means mentioned
Mangonel (manjaniq), burning with fire, drowning by water, cutting trees (fruitful or not), shooting with arrows and stones, spoiling crops.
These are supported by specific hadith and Quranic verses.
2
Identify the evidence
For mangonel: the Prophet set up a mangonel against Ta'if. For burning: he burned Buwayrah. For cutting trees: Quran 59:5. For general strength: Quran 8:60 interpreted as shooting.
Consensus among the companions is claimed, except a narration from Shafi'i and Ahmad that says 'we do to them what they do to us.'
3
Understand the principle
These means cause humiliation, rage, and dispersal of the enemy, which is the purpose of jihad.
There is no restriction to only using same means as the enemy.
4
Apply the ruling on tatarru
If the enemy uses Muslims as human shields, it is permissible to shoot at them, because the greater harm (enemy attack) must be repelled even if it results in Muslim casualties.
This is a principle of prioritizing the public interest.

Source Text

[تبيين الحقائق: 84/4] بخلاف اليهود والنصارى؛ لأنهم يقولون إن محمداً عليه رسول الله إلى العرب دوننا فلا يحكم بإسلامهم ما لم يقراً برسالته ويتبرءوا من دينهم. [عيني، مستخلص] أي تم المراد وكففنا عن قتالهم. وإلا إلى الجزية [أي ندعوهم إلى أداء الجزية لو محلا لها. (ط) عيني] أي وإن لم يسلموا ندعوهم إلى أداء الجزية؛ لما روي أنه عليه كان إذا أمر أميراً على الجيش أو السرية أمره به في حديث فيه طول، رواه أحمد ومسلم والترمذي وصححه، وهذا في حق من تقبل منه الجزية كأهل الكتاب والمجوس أو عبدة الأوثان من العجم، وأما من لا تقبل الجزية منه كالمرتدين وعبدة الأوثان من العرب، فلا ندعوهم إلى أداء الجزية؛ لعدم الفائدة؛ إذ لا يقبل منهم إلا الإسلام فنقاتلهم إلى أن يسلموا ونقاتل من تقبل عنه إلى قبول أدائها؛ لأن بها ينتهي القتال؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿ حَتَّى يُعْطُوا الْجِزْيَةَ عَنْ يَدٍ وَهُمْ صَاغِرُونَ ﴾ (التوبة:29) (عيني، مستخلص) فإن قبلوا فلهم إلخ: أي إن قبلوا أداء الجزية، فلهم ما لنا من عصمة دمائهم وأموالهم ويلزم عليهم ما يلزم علينا؛ لقول علي ه: إنما بذلوا الجزية لتكون دماؤهم كدمائنا وأموالهم كأموالنا، ومراده بالبذل القبول، بالإعطاء المذكور في الآية، والمعنى يجب لهم علينا ويجب لنا عليهم إذا تعرضنا لدمائهم وأموالهم أو تعرضوا لدمائنا وأموالنا ما يجب لبعضنا على بعض عند التعرض، فقبول الجزية ليس إلا لزوال هذا التعرض الذي كنا نتعرض قبل قبولها، وليس المراد أنه يجب عليهم من العبادات ما يجب علينا وكذا من الكفارات؛ لأن الكفار لا يخاطبون في العبادات، بل المراد وجوب القصاص والقطع في السرقة وغير ذلك. (مستخلص، فتح) ولا نقاتل إلخ: الدعوة بفتح الدال في الحرب، وكذا في الطعام، وأما في النسب فبكسر الدال، كذا لكافة العرب إلا عدي الرباب، فإنهم يكسرون دعوة الطعام، ويفتحون في النسب، وقيل الفتح في الطعام، والضم في الحرب، والكسر في النسب، أي لا يجوز لنا أن نقاتل من لم تبلغه الدعوة إلى الإسلام؛ لقوله عليه في وصية أمراء الجيش: "ادعهم إلى شهادة أن لا إله إلا الله"؛ ولأنهم بالدعوة يعلمون أنا نقاتلهم للدين لا على سبيل الأموال، ولعلهم يجيبون فيحصل المقصود بلا قتال ومن قاتلهم قبل الدعوة يأثم للنهي عنه ولا يغرم؛ خلافاً للشافعي، والمراد بالنهي قوله عليه لعلي ه: "لا تقاتل حتى تدعوهم إلى الإسلام"، ومطلق الدعوة شامل للحقيقية والحكمية، وهي انتشار الدعوة شرقاً وغرباً فأقيم ظهورها مقامها، وإذا بلغهم الإسلام فلا ينبغي لنا أن نقاتلهم حتى ندعوهم إلى الجزية (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) وندعو ندباً من بلغته لتكون الدعوة مبالغة في الإنذار وهذا مستحب ليس بواجب لما روي عن البراء بن عازب أنه قال: "بعث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم رهطا من الأنصار إلى أبي رافع، فدخل عبد الله بن عتيك بيته ليلاً، وهو نائم". رواه أحمد والبخاري؛ ولأن النبي عليه أغار على بني المصطلق وهم غافلون، والغارة لا تكون بدعوة، وفي "المحيط": تقديم الدعوة إلى الإسلام على القتال كان في ابتداء الإسلام حين لم ينتشر الإسلام ولم يستفض وأما بعد ما انتشر واستفاض، وعرف كل مشرك إلى ماذا يدعى يحل له القتال قبل الدعوة؛ لأنهم ربما يستحصنون بالدعوة فلا يقدر عليهم [رمز الحقائق: 418/1] (مستخلص) نستعين بالله تعالى: أي تطلب العون من الله سبحانه وتعالى عليهم. (ع، معدن) ونحاربهم بنصب المجانيق [جمع منجنيق وهي آلة ترمى بها الأحجار أي نقيمها على حصونهم وأسوار مدائنهم. (ط، ع)] لما روى الترمذي أن النبي نصب المنجنيق على أهل طائف. وقوله: "وحرقهم"، أي ونحاربهم أيضاً بالحرق بالنار؛ لأن النبي عليه أحرق بويرة، بضم الموحدة موضع من بلد بني النضير، وكذا نحاربهم بالغرق بإرسال المياه عليهم، وقطع الأشجار سواء كانت مثمرة أو غير مثمرة، وقد صح أن النبي عليه قطع أشجار بني النضير، وفيه قوله تعالى: ﴿مَا قَطَعْتُمْ مِنْ لِينَةٍ أَوْ تَرَكْتُمُوهَا قَائِمَةً عَلَى أُصُولِهَا فَبِإِذْنِ اللَّهِ ﴾ (الحشر:5) الآية، وكذلك نحاربهم بالرمي بالنبال والحجارة؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَأَعِدُّوا لَهُمْ مَا اسْتَطَعْتُمْ مِنْ قُوَّةٍ (الأنفال: 60) وقد فسر النبي عليه القوة بالرمي؛ لأن في هذه الأشياء إلحاق الكبت والغيظ بهم وتفريق شملهم، وهذا كله بالإجماع إلا في رواية للشافعي وأحمد نفعل بهم ما يفعلونه بنا. (مستخلص، عيني) غرقهم بتسبيب المياه على دورهم وأنفسهم. (عيني) وإفساد زروعهم بإرسال الدواب فيها أو إطلاق النار ونحو ذلك. (عيني) وإن تترسوا واصل بما قبله يعني يجوز رميهم وإن كانوا تترسوا بنا أي جعلوا بعضنا بمنزلة الترس. (معدن)
Book of Campaigns and Jihad. Rulings of Campaigns and Jihad. If we besiege them, we call them to Islam; if they accept [Islam, well and good], otherwise [we call them] to jizya. That is, we call the disbelievers first. That is, if they do not accept Islam. If they accept, they have what we have and upon them what is upon us, and we do not fight those whom the call to Islam has not reached. [This refers to] worldly matters, not worship. When we besiege them: in al-Mustakhlas it is recorded: 'Section on the manner of fighting.' If we besiege them, meaning if we besiege a city or fortress in the abode of war, we first call them to Islam, due to what is narrated from Ibn Abbas that he said: 'The Messenger of Allah never fought a people except that he called them to Islam.' Reported by Ahmad. His statement 'If they accept Islam' – in some copies, the conditional response is omitted, meaning: the desired objective is achieved, and they are not to be harmed after Islam, due to his statement: 'I have been commanded to fight people until they say La ilaha illa Allah, and when they say it, they protect from me their blood and property except by its right.' And it is necessary that they believe in him, but the hadith sufficed with the word of tawhid because they used to believe in shirk, so when they proclaimed tawhid, it was known that they believed in Muhammad, because they only knew that from him. [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 4/84] Contrary to Jews and Christians, because they say 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah only to the Arabs, not to us,' so they are not judged to be Muslims unless they affirm his prophethood and disavow their religion. [Ayni, Mustakhlas] That is, the desired objective is achieved and we refrain from fighting them. Otherwise [we call them] to jizya: i.e., we call them to pay jizya if they are eligible for it. (T, Ayni) That is, if they do not accept Islam, we call them to pay jizya, due to the narration that he, when appointing an amir over an army or a raiding party, would instruct him accordingly in a lengthy hadith, reported by Ahmad, Muslim, and al-Tirmidhi who declared it authentic. This applies to those from whom jizya is accepted, such as People of the Book, Magians, or idol worshipers of non-Arab origin. As for those from whom jizya is not accepted, such as apostates and Arab idol worshipers, we do not call them to pay jizya because there is no benefit; only Islam is accepted from them, so we fight them until they accept Islam. And we fight those from whom jizya is accepted until they accept payment of it, because that ends the fighting, due to the verse: 'Until they pay jizya out of hand while they are humbled' (9:29). (Ayni, Mustakhlas) 'If they accept' etc.: That is, if they accept payment of jizya, they have what we have of protection of their blood and property, and what is obligatory upon us becomes obligatory upon them, due to the statement of Ali: 'They only pay jizya so that their blood becomes like our blood and their property like our property.' He meant by 'payment' acceptance, i.e., the giving mentioned in the verse. The meaning is: what is obligatory for us against them becomes obligatory for us regarding them if we attack their blood or property or they attack ours, like what is obligatory for some of us against others when attacked. Accepting jizya is only for the removal of this attack that we were making before accepting it. It does not mean that acts of worship become obligatory upon them as upon us, nor expiations, because disbelievers are not addressed concerning worship. Rather, it means the obligation of retaliation, amputation for theft, etc. (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'And we do not fight' etc.: Da'wa with fatha on the dal in war, likewise in food; as for lineage, it is with kasra. Thus for all Arabs except the tribe of Rabab, who break the da'wa of food and open for lineage. It is said: fatha in food, dammah in war, kasra in lineage. That is, it is not permissible for us to fight those whom the call to Islam has not reached, due to his statement in the instruction to commanders: 'Call them to the testimony that there is no god but Allah.' And because by the call they know that we fight for religion, not for plunder, and perhaps they will respond and the objective will be achieved without fighting. Whoever fights them before the call commits a sin due to the prohibition, but does not pay blood money, contrary to al-Shafi'i. The prohibition refers to his statement to Ali: 'Do not fight until you call them to Islam.' The absolute call includes both actual and constructive call, which is the spread of the call east and west, so its prevalence stands in its stead. If Islam has reached them, it is not appropriate for us to fight them until we call them to jizya. (Mustakhlas, Fath, Ayni) And we call – as a recommendation – those whom it has reached, so that the call is an intensification of warning. This is recommended, not obligatory, due to what is narrated from al-Bara' ibn 'Azib: 'The Messenger of Allah sent a group of Ansar to Abu Rafi', and 'Abdullah ibn 'Atik entered his house by night while he was sleeping.' Reported by Ahmad and al-Bukhari. And because the Prophet attacked Banu al-Mustaliq while they were unaware, and a raid is not preceded by a call. In al-Muhit: 'Presenting the call to Islam before fighting was at the beginning of Islam when Islam had not spread and become widespread. As for after it spread and became widespread, and every polytheist knows what he is called to, it is permissible to fight before the call, because they might fortify themselves with the call and then cannot be overcome.' (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/418) (Mustakhlas) 'We seek help from Allah': that is, we seek aid from Allah against them. (Ayni, Madan) 'And we fight them by setting up mangonels' [plural of manjaniq, a device for throwing stones], i.e., we erect them against their fortresses and city walls. (T, Ayni) Because al-Tirmidhi narrated that the Prophet set up a mangonel against the people of Ta'if. His statement: 'and burning them,' i.e., we also fight them by burning with fire, because the Prophet burned the Buwayrah, a place in the land of Banu al-Nadir. Likewise, we fight them by drowning by sending water upon them, cutting down trees whether fruitful or not, and it is authentically established that the Prophet cut down the trees of Banu al-Nadir, and about that is the verse: 'Whatever palm-trees you cut down or left standing on their roots, it was by Allah's permission' (59:5). Likewise, we fight them by shooting with arrows and stones, due to the verse: 'And prepare against them whatever you can of strength' (8:60), and the Prophet interpreted 'strength' as shooting. Because in these things there is infliction of humiliation and rage upon them and dispersal of their unity. All this is by consensus, except in a narration from al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: 'We do to them what they do to us.' (Mustakhlas, Ayni) 'Drowning them': by causing water to flow over their houses and themselves. (Ayni) 'And spoiling their crops': by sending animals into them or setting fire, etc. (Ayni) 'And if they take cover' (tatarrasu): it is connected to what precedes, meaning it is permissible to shoot at them even if they take cover with us, i.e., they make some of us like a shield. (Madan)
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the procedure of jihad. It opens with the obligation to call the enemy to Islam before fighting, citing the hadith of Ibn Abbas. If they accept, they are protected. If they refuse, they are called to jizya. The commentary clarifies that this call is only for those eligible for jizya (e.g., People of the Book, Magians, non-Arab idolaters); Arab idolaters and apostates must accept Islam or be fought. When jizya is accepted, the enemy gains protection of life and property, but only in worldly matters—not in acts of worship, as disbelievers are not obligated in worship. The text then discusses the ruling on fighting those who have not received the da'wa: it is prohibited, and one who fights them sins but does not pay diyah, contra Shafi'i. However, the commentary notes that this was early Islam; after Islam spread widely, the call is no longer obligatory before attack, as it is considered constructive notice. Later, the text addresses permissible means of war: setting up mangonels, burning, drowning, cutting trees, shooting, and spoiling crops—all supported by hadith and Quran. Even if the enemy uses Muslim shields (tatarru), it is permissible to shoot, because repelling the greater harm (the enemy's attack) takes precedence.

Key Terms
منجنيق (manjaniq): A siege engine used to throw stones against fortifications.
تترس (tatarru): When the enemy uses Muslims as human shields during battle.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on using a mangonel (manjaniq) against an enemy fortress?
A) Prohibited because it causes unnecessary destruction.
B) Permissible because the Prophet used it against Ta'if.
C) Permissible only if the enemy uses one first.
D) Makruh (disliked).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits all effective means of warfare, including targeting when the enemy uses Muslim shields, based on prophetic practice and the principle of repelling greater harm.

Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules
intermediate
8 min
Shooting at Enemy Using Muslim Shields
Madhhab dispute on tatarrus (human shields).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tatarrus and explain the madhhab dispute over liability (diyah/kaffarah) for accidentally killed Muslims.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits shooting at enemies using Muslim shields to prevent jihad from being blocked; no compensation is due.

This lesson examines a critical battlefield scenario: enemy forces using Muslim captives or traders as human shields. The Hanafi school permits attacking, while other schools restrict it if Muslim death is certain. Understanding the proofs and principles behind each position is essential for applying fiqh in conflict.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its core argument
Identify the ruling: it is permissible to shoot at the enemy even if they use Muslim shields. The proof is the absolute command to fight jihad, which would be blocked if such shields prevented action.
The Hanafi view prioritizes the general obligation of jihad over the specific harm to the captive.
2
Identify the opposing view and its basis
The three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) and al-Hasan al-Basri hold that if the attacker knows the Muslims will be killed, shooting is prohibited, and diyah/kaffarah become obligatory.
Their basis: killing a Muslim is forbidden, and the command to fight is not absolute when it leads to that.
3
Examine the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response uses two arguments: (1) intention directs the shot to the enemy, since capacity to distinguish is limited; (2) the principle 'al-furud la taqran bi al-gharamat' (obligations are not coupled with penalties) removes liability.
Also, repelling general harm (threat to the community) outweighs specific harm (killing the captive).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis permit the attack without any compensation; other schools forbid the attack if Muslim death is certain and require diyah/kaffarah if it occurs.
This affects how a commander balances battlefield necessity against individual protection.
5
Understand the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi in the school because it is supported by the majority of Hanafi scholars and the principle of necessity (darurah).
The text from Al-Hidayah and its commentaries affirms this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ببعضنا : [ متعلق بـ"تترسوا" أي ببعض المسلمين الذين عندهم من الأسرى أو التجار. (عيني)] أي يجوز رمي الكفار ولو جعلوا بعض المسلمين بمنزلة الترس؛ لأن الأمر بالقتال مطلق، ولو كان الغير مانعاً لانسد الجهاد؛ إذ قلما تخلو مدينة أو حصن عن أسير أو تاجر، ولأن فيه دفع الضرر العام للدفع عن مجتمع الإسلام بإثبات الضرر الخاص، وهو قتل الأسير والتاجر.
ونقصدهم: أي نقصد برمينا الكفار؛ لأنه تعذر التميز فعلاً، ولقد أمكن بالنية؛ إذ الطاعة بحسب الطاقة، فبعد نية الرمي إلى الكفار لو أصاب السهام أسيراً أو تاجراً فلا دية ولا كفارة؛ لأن الفروض لا تقترن بالغرامات، وعند الثلاثة لا يرمون إن تترسوا بهم إذا علم أنهم يتلفون، به فتجب الكفارة والدية عند الإصابة، وبه قال الحسن. (مستخلص، عيني، فتح)
'By some of us' refers to 'tatarru' (using as shields), meaning some of the Muslims who are captives or merchants. It means it is permissible to shoot the disbelievers even if they have made some Muslims like shields, because the command to fight is absolute; and if the other (the Muslim shield) were a hindrance, jihad would be blocked, because rarely is a city or fortress free from a captive or merchant. And because it involves repelling general harm to defend the Muslim community by inflicting specific harm, which is killing the captive and merchant. 'And we intend them': we intend, by our shooting, the disbelievers, because distinction is practically impossible, but it is possible by intention, since obligation is according to capacity. So after intending to shoot the disbelievers, if an arrow hits a captive or merchant, there is no blood money (diyah) nor expiation (kaffarah), because obligations (of war) are not coupled with penalties. According to the three (Imams), they do not shoot if (the enemy) uses them as shields when they know they will be killed, then blood money and expiation become obligatory upon hitting. Al-Hasan also held this view.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute on the ruling of shooting at enemy forces who use Muslim captives or merchants as human shields (tatarru). The Hanafi position, as stated by Imam Abu Hanifa, is that it is permissible to shoot, and no compensation (diyah or kaffarah) is due if a Muslim is accidentally killed, because the command to fight jihad is absolute and the need to repel the general threat to the Muslim community overrides the specific harm. The Hanafi proof is rational: if the presence of a Muslim shield prevented fighting, jihad would be impossible, as such shields are common. Intention suffices to direct the shot at the enemy. The opposing view (the three imams: Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) holds that shooting is not allowed when the attacker knows the Muslims will be killed, and if they are killed, both diyah and kaffarah are obligatory. This difference arises from whether the command to fight is considered absolute or restricted by the prohibition of killing Muslims. The Hanafi school relies on the principle of repelling the greater harm (general to the community) by incurring the lesser harm (specific loss).

Key Terms
تترس (tatarrus): Using someone as a shield; here, the enemy using Muslim captives or traders to protect themselves.
دية (diyah): Blood money paid to the heirs of a person killed unlawfully.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation, typically freeing a slave or fasting, for certain sins including accidental killing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a Muslim captive is accidentally killed when shooting at an enemy using them as a shield?
A) The shooter must pay diyah and perform kaffarah.
B) The shooter must pay diyah but no kaffarah.
C) No diyah or kaffarah is due.
D) The shooter must be qisas.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the obligation of jihad is primary, and accidental killing of Muslim shields in a valid attack does not incur diyah or kaffarah.

The Prohibition of Taking a Mushaf and Women into a Small Raiding Party
Explains the prohibition and conditions for taking a Qur'an and women into enemy territory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taking a Mushaf and women into enemy territory, and distinguish between the case of a small sariyyah and a large army.

Core Idea
A Mushaf and women must not be taken into a small raid where safety is at risk, but are permissible with a large secure army.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains a prohibition that applies specifically to small raiding parties (sariyyah). The concern is that the Mushaf might be desecrated or the woman captured and dishonored. However, if the army is large and safety is assured, the prohibition lifts. We will examine the evidence from the hadith of Ali and the reasoning behind this nuanced ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is taking a Mushaf (copy of the Qur'an) and a woman into a sariyyah. The ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibited close to haram) due to the risk.
The prohibition is specific to small raids, not large armies.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the statement of Ali ibn Abi Talib: 'Do not travel with the Qur'an into the land of the enemy.'
This hadith is the direct textual evidence.
3
Understand the illa (effective cause)
The prohibition is due to fear of harm: the Mushaf may be desecrated, and the woman may be captured and dishonored.
Risk (khawf) is the key factor.
4
Identify the exception
If the army is large (askar 'azim) and safety is assured, it is permissible to take women and Mushafs.
The absence of risk removes the prohibition.
5
Note the extension by qiyas
By analogy, books of hadith and fiqh are included with the Mushaf, as they also contain sacred texts and risk disrespect.
This extension is explicitly mentioned in the commentary.

ونهينا عن إخراج مصحف: لأن في إخراجه من التعريض؛ لأن يناله العدو، فيستخف به، ويلحق بالمصحف كتب الحديث والفقه، وقد نهانا الشارع عن إخراج المصحف كما قال علي: "لا تسافروا بالقرآن في أرض العدو". وقوله: "وامرأة"؛ لأن في إخراجها تعريض المرأة على الضياع والفضائح. وقوله: "في سرية" من سرى يسري بمعنى سار ليلاً، ومنه السرية؛ لأنها تسري في خفية، والجيش الجمع العظيم، وكذا الجند، وأما السرية فنحو أربع مائة يسيرون بالليل ويختفون بالنهار ويقال: عدد السرايا أربع مائة رجل، ولا بأس بإخراج النساء والمصاحف إذا كان عسكر عظيم يؤمن عليه، وقوله : يخاف عليها أي على السرية، وفي أكثر النسخ بتثنية الضمير، ويرجع على المصحف والمرأة وهو أنسب للتعليل. (عيني، فتح، محشي)
And we are prohibited from taking out a Mushaf: because taking it out exposes it to risk; the enemy may obtain it and treat it with disrespect. Included with the Mushaf are books of hadith and fiqh. The Lawgiver has prohibited taking out the Mushaf, as Ali said: 'Do not travel with the Qur'an into the land of the enemy.' And his statement: 'and a woman' because taking her out exposes the woman to loss and disgrace. And his statement: 'in a small raiding party (sariyyah)' from sara yasri meaning to travel by night; hence sariyyah because it moves secretly. A jaysh is a large group, likewise a jund. A sariyyah is about four hundred who travel by night and hide by day; it is said the number of a sariyyah is four hundred men. There is no harm in taking out women and Mushafs if it is a great army where safety is assured. And his statement: 'fearing for it' (yukhafu 'alayha) means for the sariyyah. In most copies, the pronoun is dual, referring to the Mushaf and the woman, which is more suitable for the reasoning.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on the prohibition of taking a Mushaf (copy of the Qur'an) and women into enemy territory during a small raiding party (sariyyah). The reason is the risk of the Mushaf being desecrated or the woman being captured and dishonored. The proof is the statement of Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) prohibiting travel with the Qur'an into enemy land, and the general principle of avoiding harm. The commentary clarifies that the prohibition applies specifically to a small party (about 400 men) where security is not guaranteed. However, if the army is large and safety is assured, it is permitted to take women and Mushafs. The dual pronoun 'alayhima' (fearing for both) in some copies indicates the prohibition covers both the Mushaf and the woman. This ruling extends to books of hadith and fiqh by analogy, as they similarly risk disrespect.

Key Terms
مصحف (mushaf): A physical copy of the Qur'an.
سرية (sariyyah): A small military detachment that travels by night and stays hidden by day, typically about 400 men.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is it permissible to take a Mushaf into enemy territory?
A) Only in a small raiding party (sariyyah).
B) Only when there is no risk of capture.
C) When the army is large and safety is assured.
D) It is never permissible.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits taking a Mushaf and women into a small raiding party due to risk, but permits it in a large secure army.

Prohibition of Ghadr, Ghulul, and Muthlah in Jihad
Explains the prohibition of ghadr, ghulul, and muthlah with proofs and the distinction between treachery and deception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three prohibited acts in jihad and distinguish between treachery (ghadr) and lawful deception (khida').

Core Idea
Treachery breaks treaties; deception wins wars—only without violating covenants.

In the conduct of war, Islam establishes clear ethical boundaries. This lesson examines three specifically prohibited acts: treachery (ghadr), embezzlement of spoils (ghulul), and mutilation (muthlah). It also clarifies a crucial distinction between unlawful treachery and lawful military deception, resolving an apparent conflict with the hadith 'War is deception.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the three prohibited acts
Read the definitions of ghadr (breaking a pact), ghulul (secretly taking spoils before division), and muthlah (mutilating the dead).
These are distinct categories from general battlefield conduct.
2
Locate the primary proof
Find the hadith: 'Do not mutilate, do not commit treachery, and do not kill children.' Recognize its source and authority.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati al-dalala).
3
Understand the distinction between ghadr and khida'
Read the counter-argument based on 'War is deception.' Then follow the Hanafi response: deception is permissible as long as no treaty exists; breaking a treaty is ghadr.
Examples: feigning retreat or attacking by night is khida' if no treaty is violated.
4
Address the counterargument about the 'Urainiyin
The text notes an objection: the Prophet reportedly mutilated the 'Urainiyin. The response is abrogation (naskh) by the later prohibition.
This shows that earlier permissive action is overridden by a later explicit prohibition.
5
Summarize the Hanafi ruling
Ghadr, ghulul, and muthlah are haram. Lawful deception is permitted only within the bounds of existing treaties.
This ruling is from zahir al-riwayah and is agreed upon across schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغدر وغلول أي ونهينا أيضاً عن غدر الغدر نقض العهد وترك الوفاء بما التزم، والغلول: الخيانة في المغنم قبل القسمة، وقوله: ومثلة" اسم من مثل به أي جعله نكالاً وعبرة لغيره كقطع الأعضاء وتسويد الوجه، والنهي هذه قوله عليه: "لا تمثلوا، ولا تغدروا ، ولا تقتلوا وليداً"، رواه أحمد وابن ماجه. فإن قيل : قال علي: "الحرب خدعة"، فيشكل بالغدر ؟ قلنا: الفرق بين الغدر والخداع أنه ما دامت الحرب قائمة لا يحرم الخداع بأن نريهم أنا لا نحاربهم في هذا اليوم حتى يأمنوا ونحاربهم فيه، أو نريهم أنا نذهب إلى صوب آخر حتى يغفلوا فنأتيهم بياتاً ونحو ذلك، بخلاف ما إذا كان بيننا وبينهم عهد على أن لا نحارب في هذا اليوم حتى أمنوا فإنه لا تجوز المحاربة؛ لأن هذا استثمان وعهد، فالمحاربة نقض العهد وليس من خداع الحرب فيكون غدراً، والحاصل أن الخديعة إيصال مكر إلى الخصم من حيث لا يعلم . غير خلف وعد أو نقض عهد، فإن قلت: روي من عن النبي عليا أنه مثل بالعرنيين حتى سمل أعينهم؟ قلنا: إنه ينسخ بالنهي المتأخر قال علي: "لا تمثلوا بخلق الله"،
And 'ghadr' (treachery) and 'ghulul' (embezzlement): meaning we are also prohibited from ghadr, which is breaking a pact and failing to fulfill what was undertaken; and ghulul is betrayal regarding the spoils before division. And his statement: 'and muthlah' is a verbal noun from 'mathala bihi' meaning to make him an example and a warning for others, like cutting off limbs and blackening the face. The prohibition comes from his saying: 'Do not mutilate, do not commit treachery, and do not kill children,' narrated by Ahmad and Ibn Majah. If it is said: Ali said, 'War is deception,' so this conflicts with treachery? We say: The difference between treachery (ghadr) and deception (khida') is that as long as war is ongoing, deception is not prohibited, such as showing them that we will not fight them today so that they feel secure and then we fight them, or showing them we are going in another direction so that they become negligent and then we come to them by night and so on. But if there is a treaty between us and them that we will not fight on that day until they are secure, then fighting is not permissible, because this is a request for protection (istithman) and a covenant; so fighting would be breaking the covenant and not from war deception, thus it would be treachery. In summary, deception is delivering a stratagem to the opponent from where he does not know, without breaking a promise or breaching a covenant. If you say: It is narrated that the Prophet mutilated the 'Urainiyin until he gouged out their eyes? We say: That is abrogated by the later prohibition; Ali said: 'Do not mutilate the creation of Allah.'
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage explains three prohibited acts in jihad: ghadr (treachery: breaking a pact), ghulul (embezzlement: taking spoils before proper division), and muthlah (mutilation: disfiguring the dead). The proof is a hadith: 'Do not mutilate, do not commit treachery, and do not kill children.' The commentary addresses an apparent conflict with the hadith 'War is deception.' The distinction is that deception (khida') is permissible as long as no treaty is broken; it involves trickery within the context of ongoing hostilities, like feigning retreat or attacking by night. However, if a treaty exists, breaking it constitutes ghadr and is prohibited. The objection regarding the Prophet's mutilation of the 'Urainiyin is answered by stating that this was abrogated by the later prohibition, as narrated from Ali. Thus, the ruling is established: these acts are absolutely prohibited in warfare.

Key Terms
غدر (ghadr): Breaking a pact or treaty, failing to fulfill a commitment.
غلول (ghulul): Secretly taking spoils of war before the official distribution.
مثلة (muthlah): Mutilation of the dead, such as cutting off limbs or blackening the face.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the difference between treachery (ghadr) and deception (khida') in war?
A) Deception involves breaking a treaty; treachery does not.
B) Treachery is breaking a treaty; deception is lawful trickery without breaking a treaty.
C) Both are prohibited in war.
D) Deception is only allowed against women and children.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ghadr (treachery), ghulul (embezzlement), and muthlah (mutilation) are absolutely haram in jihad, and lawful deception is permissible only without breaking a treaty.

Protection of Non-Combatants in Jihad: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Defines whom it is forbidden to kill in war and exception for military leaders

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on which non-combatants are protected in jihad and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the principle of harb versus kufr.

Core Idea
In jihad, killing is permitted against combatants, not non-combatants—disbelief alone does not justify killing.

This passage from the Hanafi fiqh tradition addresses a fundamental question of the laws of war: whom is it permissible to kill in jihad? The Hanafi school draws a clear line between combatants and non-combatants, protecting women, children, the elderly, the blind, and the lame unless they actively participate or lead the war effort. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its scriptural basis, and the key dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who permits killing all adult male disbelievers regardless of their capacity to fight.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the categories of people the Hanafi school forbids killing in jihad: women, children, the insane, decrepit old men, the blind, and the lame. Note the exception: if they are strategists or kings, they may be killed.
The exception is derived from the Prophet's killing of Durayd ibn al-Simmah, a blind strategist.
2
Identify the Opponent and Their View
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that all adult male disbelievers may be killed, including the elderly, blind, and lame, because killing is a recompense for kufr (disbelief).
The Shafi'i position is based on the belief that the primary justification for killing in jihad is disbelief itself.
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: kufr is established, so killing is justified as a punishment. The Hanafi response refutes this by distinguishing dar al-taklif (abode of obligation) from dar al-jaza' (abode of recompense).
The Shafi'i argument is that the Quran and general commands to fight the disbelievers imply all disbelievers may be killed.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: The world is a place of obligation, not punishment. Killing is only permitted for harb (actual war), not mere disbelief. If disbelief were the cause, even the completely helpless would be killed, which is not the case.
The Hanafi school cites hadiths explicitly prohibiting killing women and children and non-combatant elderly.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: Under Hanafi law, non-combatants are safe provided they do not fight or lead; under Shafi'i law, all adult male disbelievers may be killed regardless of combat status.
This difference significantly affects the conduct of Muslim armies and the protection of civilians.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير والجهاد

أحكام السير والجهاد

ومثلة وقتل امرأة وغير مكلف وشيخ فان وأعمى ومقعد، إلا أن يكون أحدهم رأي في الحرب، أو ملكاً، وقتل أب مشرك .

أي في أمرها ذا

وعن عمران بن الحصين الله أن النبي عليه كان ينهاهم عن المثلة بعد أمر عرنيين يبين في كل خطبة، وفي " الاختيار": النهي بعد الظفر بهم ولا بأس به قبله؛ لأنه أبلغ في كبتهم وأضر بهم، وهذا حسن. [رمز الحقائق: ٤١٩/١] (مستخلص)

ومثلة : أي وعن مثلة وهي أن يجدع المقتول أو يقطع عضو منه. (عيني)

وقتل امرأة أي ونهينا عن قتل امرأة، فإنه روي عنه عليه: "أنه نهى عن قتل النساء والصبيان"، رواه البخاري ومسلم وآخرون، أي الذين لا يقدرون على القتال، ولا على الصياح عند التقاء الصفين، وقوله: "وغير مكلف"، أي ونهينا عن قتل غير مكلف أي صبي ومجنون؛ لما روينا، وقوله: "وشيخ فان"؛ لقوله عليه: "لا تقتلوا شيخاً فانياً"، أي الذي لا يقدر على القتال أو الصباح أو الإحبال، والحديث رواه أبو داود، وقوله: "أعمى ومقعد"، وكذا الراهب الذي لا يخالط الناس وأهل الكنائس الذين لا يخالطون الناس؛ لعدم تحقق الحرب منهم، والمقعد هو الأعرج. وقيل: هو الذي أقعده الداء عن الحركة، وقيل: المتشنج الأعضاء، والزمن الذي طال مرضه، وقال الشافعي: يقتل الشيخ والمقعد والأعمى؛ لأن القتل عنده جزاء الكفر، وقد تحقق قلنا الدنيا دار التكليف، وليست بدار الجزاء، وإنما وجب في بعض الجنايات؛ لتنتظم مصالح العباد؛ لأن السفهاء لا ينتهون بمجرد الوعيد، فالمبيح للقتل الحرب عندنا، ولا يتحقق منهم، ولو كان الكفر مبيحاً للقتل لوجب أن يقتل يابس الشق والمقطوع يده اليمنى ولم يجب. (مستخلص، فتح عيني) إلا أن يكون إلخ : أي إذا كان أحد المذكورين صاحب تدبير في أمر الحرب أو ملكاً فحينئذ يقتل؛ لأن في قتله كسر شوكتهم، وإزالة ضررهم عن المسلمين، وقد صح أن رسول الله قتل دريد بن الصمة، وكان ابن مائة وعشرين سنة، وقيل: ابن مائة وستين؛ لأنه كان صاحب رأي وهو أعمى رمز الحقائق: ٤١٩/١] والصبي والمجنون إذا قاتلا في الحرب جعلا ذا رأي وقتلا، وكذا إذا كان الصبي ملكاً وقد أحضروه موضع القتال، وكذا الحكم لو كانت المرأة ملكة. (فتح)

أحدهم: أي المذكورين غير الصبي والمجنون؛ لأنه لا رأي لهما فلا يقتلان إلا أن يقاتلا أو يكونا ملكين. (عيني) ذا رأي: أي صاحب رأي وتدبير أو مقاتلاً. (ط، عيني)
The Book of Siyar and Jihad. Rulings of Siyar and Jihad. And [prohibition of] muthla, killing a woman, a non-mukallaf, a decrepit old man, a blind man, and a lame man, unless one of them has an opinion in war or is a king, and killing a polytheist father. Meaning: concerning her matter this. And from 'Imran ibn Husayn (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to forbid them from mutilation after the affair of the 'Urayniyin, which he would mention in every sermon. And in al-Ikhtiyar: prohibition after victory over them, but there is no harm in it before victory because it is more effective in suppressing them and more harmful to them, and this is good. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/419, Mukhtasar) Muthla: meaning also muthla, which is to cut off the nose of the slain or amputate a limb of his. ('Ayni) Killing a woman: meaning we are also forbidden to kill a woman, for it is narrated from him (peace be upon him): 'He forbade killing women and children,' reported by al-Bukhari, Muslim and others, meaning those who cannot fight or shout at the meeting of the two ranks. And his saying 'and a non-mukallaf' meaning we are forbidden to kill a non-mukallaf, i.e., a boy or an insane person, due to what we narrated. And his saying 'and a decrepit old man' due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'Do not kill a decrepit old man,' meaning one who cannot fight or shout or impregnate, and the hadith was reported by Abu Dawud. And his saying 'a blind man and a lame man' and likewise a monk who does not mix with people and the people of churches who do not mix with people, due to the lack of actual war from them. The lame person is the one who is crippled. It is said: he is the one whom illness has incapacitated from movement, and it is said: the one with contracted limbs, and the chronically ill one whose illness has prolonged. Al-Shafi'i said: The old man, the lame, and the blind are killed because killing in his view is the recompense for disbelief, and it is established. We said: The world is the abode of obligation, not the abode of recompense. It is only obligatory in some crimes to maintain the interests of the people, because the foolish are not deterred by mere threat. Therefore, the permissibility of killing is based on war according to us, and it is not realized from them. If disbelief were the permissibility of killing, then it would be necessary to kill the one whose side has dried up and the one whose right hand is cut off, but it is not. (Mukhtasar, Fath al-'Ayni) 'Unless' etc.: meaning if one of the mentioned individuals is a strategist in warfare or a king, then they are killed, because their killing breaks their power and removes their harm from the Muslims. It is authentically reported that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) killed Durayd ibn al-Simmah, who was one hundred and twenty years old, and it is said one hundred and sixty, because he was a strategist while blind. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/419) The boy and the insane person, if they fight in war, they are considered strategists and are killed. Likewise, if the boy is a king and they brought him to the battlefield, the same ruling applies if the woman is a queen. (Fath) 'One of them': meaning the mentioned ones other than the boy and the insane person, because they have no opinion, so they are not killed unless they fight or are kings. ('Ayni) 'Strategist': meaning one with opinion and strategy, or a combatant. (T., 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
The passage begins by listing categories of people whom it is forbidden to kill in jihad: women, children, the insane, the elderly, the blind, and the lame. The prohibition applies as long as they are not combatants. The exception for those who have military expertise or are kings is proven by the Prophet's killing of Durayd ibn al-Simmah, a blind strategist. The text then discusses mutilation (muthla), which is cutting off the nose or limbs of the slain. The Hanafi school prohibits mutilation after victory but allows it before victory as a psychological weapon. The passage also includes an inter-madhhab dispute with al-Shafi'i, who allows killing the elderly, blind, and lame because they are disbelievers. The Hanafi response is that jihad is about war, not disbelief; the world is a place of obligation, not recompense. If disbelief alone permitted killing, then even the helpless would be killed, which is not the case. The Hanafi position is that only those who actually participate in war or are leaders of war can be killed.

Key Terms
مثلة (muthla): Mutilation of the dead enemy, such as cutting off the nose or limb.
شيخ فان (shaykh fan): Decrepit old man who cannot fight, shout, or marry.
مقعد (muq'ad): Lame or crippled person; one who cannot move due to illness or defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it not permissible to kill a blind person who is not fighting?
A) Because the blind are protected under all circumstances
B) Because the blind cannot be warriors
C) Because the permissibility of killing is based on actual combat, not mere disbelief
D) Because the blind are not soldiers

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school protects non-combatants in jihad, permitting killing only those who participate in war or lead it.

When a Muslim Son May Kill His Polytheist Father in Jihad
Explains when a Muslim son may kill his polytheist father.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on initiating killing of a polytheist father and apply the exception for self-defense.

Core Idea
Initiating killing of a polytheist father is forbidden, but self-defense is an exception.

Jihad requires fighting polytheists, but what if the enemy is your own father? The Quran commands kindness to parents, yet war demands action. This lesson resolves the tension: the son must not strike first, but if attacked, he may defend himself, even by killing. We explore the ruling, its proof, and the reasoning behind the exception.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The passage discusses a Muslim son encountering his polytheist father in battle. Determine the default ruling on initiating killing.
The Quranic verse is cited: 'accompany them with kindness'.
2
Locate the exception
Find the condition under which killing becomes permissible. The text mentions 'if the father intends to kill the son and he cannot defend himself except by killing him'.
Self-defense overrides the default prohibition.
3
Understand the evidence
Analyze how the Quranic verse (Luqman 31:15) is used to prohibit initiation, and how self-defense is justified by necessity.
The dalil is zanni al-dalala: it does not explicitly mention war, so analogical reasoning supports the exception.
4
Apply the ruling to other family relations
The passage extends the ruling: killing a disbelieving brother, uncle, or son is not disliked, but a rebellious Muslim brother (baghi) cannot be killed. Understand the principle: lack of isma (sanctity) for harbis.
The key difference is that a Muslim baghi still has isma, so killing him is forbidden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قتل أب: أي ونهينا أيضاً عن قتل أب؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَصَاحِبُهُمَا فِي الدُّنْيَا مَعْرُوفام (لقمان: ١٥) وليست البداءة بالقتل من المعروف. تبيين الحقائق: ٩٠/٤] (عيني) وليأب الابن: أي إذا أدرك الابن المسلم أباه المشرك فليمتنع عن قتله، وينبغي أن ينصرف عنه ويترك؛ ليقتله غير الابن، وإن لم يكن ثمه من يقتله لا يمكنه من الرجوع حتى لا يعود حرباً علينا، ولكنه يلجئه إلى مكان يستمسك به حتى يجيء غيره فيقتله، والمراد بالأب الأصل وإن علا، وبالامتناع عدم القتل ابتداء، فأما إذا قصد الأب قتل. ولم يمكنه دفعه إلا بقتله، فلا بأس بقتله؛ لأن هذا دفع عن نفسه، وله أن يدفع أباه المسلم بالقتل إذا قصد الأب قتله، فالكافر أولى. وكذا له أن يؤثر حياته، ألا ترى أنه لو كان للابن ماء يكفي أحدهما فللابن أن يشربه وإن كان الأب يموت عطشاً، ولهذا يحبس الأب بنفقة ولده دون دينه، ومع هذا لو قتله لا يجب عليه شيء؛ لعدم العاصم، ولا يكره قتل أخيه وخاله وعمه المشركين، بخلاف أخيه الباغي حيث لا يجوز له قتله وكذا يجوز له قتل ابنه الكافر؛ لأنه لا يجب إحياؤه؛ ولهذا لا تجب عليه نفقة ابنه المحارب. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٠/١] (فتح)
Killing a father: meaning we are also forbidden to kill a father, due to Allah's statement: 'But accompany them in the world with kindness' (Luqman 31:15). Initiating killing is not from kindness. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 4/90, 'Ayni) And the son should refuse: meaning when the Muslim son encounters his polytheist father, he should refrain from killing him, and it is better for him to turn away and leave him so that someone else kills him. If there is no one else to kill him, he should not allow him to return to become a combatant against us, but rather he should force him into a place where he is detained until another person comes and kills him. The intended meaning of 'father' is the root ancestor, even if elevated. By 'refraining' is meant not initiating killing. As for when the father intends to kill the son and the son cannot defend himself except by killing him, then there is no harm in killing him because this is self-defense. He may even defend himself from a Muslim father if the father intends to kill him, so a disbeliever is more entitled. Likewise, he may prefer his own life; do you not see that if the son has water sufficient for only one of them, the son may drink it even if the father dies of thirst? For this reason, the father is imprisoned for the son's nafaqah but not for his debt. Despite this, if the son kills him, nothing is obligatory upon him because there is no sanctity (isma). And it is not disliked to kill a disbelieving brother, maternal uncle, or paternal uncle, unlike a rebellious brother (baghi) whose killing is not permissible. Likewise, it is permissible for him to kill his disbelieving son, because his preservation is not obligatory; hence, he is not obligated to provide nafaqah for his warring son. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/420, Fath)
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
The passage addresses a specific situation: a Muslim son encounters his polytheist father in battle. The initial ruling is that the son should not kill his father; he should step aside and let another Muslim do it. If no one else is present, he should detain the father until another Muslim can kill him. The prohibition is based on the Quranic verse commanding kindness to parents; initiating killing is not kindness. However, if the father attacks the son, the son may kill him in self-defense. This is analogous to the ruling with a Muslim father: a son may kill his father in self-defense, and the disbelieving father is more deserving of being killed in self-defense. The text also notes that if the son kills his father in self-defense, no diyah or kaffarah is due because the father has no isma (sanctity) as a harbi. The son may also kill his disbelieving brother, uncle, or son, but not a rebellious Muslim brother (baghi). The principle is that the sanctity (isma) of a harbi is not established, so killing them in war is permissible, but familial ties impose a moral obligation to avoid initiating killing.

Key Terms
الأب الأصل (al-ab al-asl): Father or any male ascendant (grandfather, etc.).
عاصم (asim): Sanctity (isma) that protects life; a harbi has no 'asima.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What should a Muslim son do if he captures his polytheist father in war?
A) Kill him immediately
B) Release him
C) Let another Muslim kill him
D) Imprison him indefinitely

Key Takeaway
A Muslim son must not initiate killing of his polytheist father in war, but may kill in self-defense; no liability arises due to lack of sanctity.

Rulings on Truce with Enemy and Repudiation of Treaty
Explains conditions for truce with payment and repudiation rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on truce with payment, repudiation of treaty, and fighting without repudiation, and identify the conditions for each.

Core Idea
A truce with payment is permissible for benefit; repudiation is required when breaking a treaty early; betrayal nullifies the treaty without notice.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains three critical scenarios in the law of jihad regarding treaties with non-Muslims. The Hanafi school balances the obligation of jihad with practical considerations of benefit and necessity. Understanding these rules is essential for proper application of Islamic international law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on truce with payment
Find the statement 'We make a truce with them' and the condition 'if there is benefit'. Note the ruling is permissibility (jai'z). Also note the treatment of money received.
The text specifies that money is treated like jizyah unless the enemy was besieged.
2
Locate the Quranic proof
The verse 8:61 is cited. Identify the phrase 'fa-jnah laha' and understand that the imperative implies permission, not obligation, due to context.
The imperative form indicates ibahah when benefit exists.
3
Analyze the repudiation rule
'We repudiate if better' – explain the need for notification (nabdh) in the manner of the treaty. If the treaty expires, no repudiation is needed.
The repudiation must match the treaty's publicity: widespread or private.
4
Distinguish fighting without repudiation
The text says 'we fight without repudiation' when the enemy king betrays. Betrayal itself breaks the treaty, so no notice is needed.
The term 'nabdh' here means notification, not annulment.
5
Summarize practical consequences
Apply these rules: truce with payment is ghanimah only if the enemy was besieged; early repudiation requires returning proportional payment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونصالحهم كذا في أكثر النسخ، وفي "المستخلص" زاد قبل هذا فصل في الموادعة، ونصالحهم أي إذا رأى الإمام أن يصالح أهل الحرب على مال يأخذوه أو على مال يدفعه عند خوف الهلاك وكان في ذلك مصلحة للمسلمين فيجوز ذلك الصلح؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ جَنَحُوا لِلسَّلْمِ فَاجْنَحْ لَهَا ﴾ (الأنفال:٦١) أي إن مالوا إلى الصلح، بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن فيه خير؛ لأنه يكون ترك جهاد صورة ومعنى وهو فرض فلا يجوز تركه من غير عذر، وهذا إذا كان لنا حاجة، وإن لم يكن لنا حاجة لم يجز، ثم المال الذي يؤخذ منهم بالصلح يصرف مصارف الجزية، ولا خمس فيه إذا لم ينزلوا بساحتهم بل أرسلوا رسولا إلا إذا أحاط الجيش بهم ثم أخذوا المال فهو غنيمة. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٠/١] (فتح)

وننبذ لو خيراً: أي لو صالحهم الإمام مدة ثم رأى أن نقض الصلح أنفع للمسلمين فينبذ إليهم وقاتلهم؛ لأن النبي عليه نبذ الموادعة التي كانت بينه وبين أهل مكة؛ لأن المصلحة لما تبدلت كان النقض جهاداً، ويكون النبذ على الوجه الذي كان الأمان، فإن كان منتشراً يجب أن يكون النبذ كذلك، وإن كان غير منتشر فالنبذ يكون كذلك، وهو على قياس الإذن بالحجر، وهذا إذا نقض الصلح قبل المدة، وأما إذا مضت المدة يبطل الصلح بمضيها، فلا ينبذ إليهم، وإن كان الصلح على جعل، فنقضه قبل مضي المدة رده عليهم بحصته. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٠/١] (مستخلص)

ونقاتل بلا نبذ النبذ ههنا بمعنى الإعلام، بخلافه في قوله : "وننبذ لو خيراً"، فإنه بمعنى نقض العهد مع إرسال العلم إليهم، وقوله: "لو خان ملكهم"؛ لأن النبذ نقض العهد، وقد انتقض بالخيانة منهم، وقيد بـــ "الملك"، فعم ما لو =
And we make a truce with them: thus in most copies. In al-Mukhtasar, a chapter on al-Muwada'ah is added before this. And we make a truce with them: meaning when the Imam sees to make a truce with the people of war with money they take or with money they pay due to fear of destruction and there is benefit for Muslims in that, then such a truce is permissible. Due to Allah's statement: 'And if they incline to peace, then incline to it' (al-Anfal 8:61), i.e., if they lean towards peace, contrary to when there is no good in it, because it would be abandoning jihad in form and meaning, and it is an obligation, so abandoning it is not permissible without an excuse. This is when we have a need, and if we have no need, it is not permissible. Then the money taken from them by truce is spent on the categories of jizyah, and there is no khums in it if they did not descend to our territory but sent a messenger, unless the army encircles them and then takes money, then it is ghanimah. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/420, Fath) And we repudiate if [it is] better: meaning if the Imam made a truce with them for a period and then saw that breaking the truce is more beneficial for Muslims, then he should repudiate it to them and fight them, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) repudiated the truce that was between him and the people of Makkah, because when the benefit changed, the annulment became jihad. The repudiation should be in the same manner as the truce was given: if it was widespread, the repudiation must be widespread; if it was not widespread, the repudiation is likewise. This is analogous to the permission regarding stones. This applies if he breaks the truce before the term expires. As for when the term expires, the truce becomes void by its expiry, so he does not need to repudiate to them. If the truce was for a consideration, breaking it before the term expires requires returning the proportionate amount. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/420, Mukhtasar) And we fight without repudiation: repudiation here means notification, unlike in his saying 'we repudiate if better' where it means breaking the covenant with sending notification to them. And his saying 'if their king betrays', because repudiation breaks the covenant, and it is already broken by their betrayal. And it is restricted to 'king', so it applies generally when =
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
The passage covers three related rulings on truce (sulh) with the enemy. First, the Imam may make a truce with the enemy in exchange for money (either given by Muslims or taken from enemy) if it brings benefit and averts destruction. The permissibility is based on the Quranic verse 8:61. If there is no benefit, it is not allowed because jihad is obligatory. The money received is treated like jizyah (no khums) unless the enemy was besieged, then it is ghanimah subject to khums. Second, if after making a truce for a fixed period, the Imam finds that breaking it is more beneficial, he must repudiate (nabdh) the treaty by notifying the enemy, then fight. This is based on the Prophet's repudiation of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah. The repudiation must be in the same manner (public or private) as the truce was made. If the truce expires, no repudiation is needed. If the truce involved payment, the Imam must return the proportionate amount if he breaks it early. Third, if the enemy king betrays the treaty, fighting is allowed without prior repudiation because the treaty is already broken by their betrayal. The term 'nabdh' here means notification, not annulment, in the context of fighting without notification.

Key Terms
نبذ (nabdh): Repudiation of a treaty, which includes notification of annulment.
موادعة (muwada'ah): A truce or peace treaty with the enemy, often involving payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the enemy king betrays the treaty
B) When the treaty expires
C) When it benefits the Muslims
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits truce with payment for benefit, mandates repudiation before breaking a treaty, and allows immediate fighting upon enemy betrayal.

Peace Treaty with the Enemy for Payment
Ruling on sulh with harbis for wealth when beneficial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making peace with harbis involving payment, identify the conditions for permissibility, and apply the distinction between wealth treated as jizya and ghanimah.

Core Idea
Peace with the enemy involving payment is permissible only when it benefits the Muslims; otherwise it is abandoning an obligation.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on making a peace treaty (sulh) with the enemy (harbis) that involves giving or receiving wealth. The matn states it is permissible if there is benefit (khayr). The commentary expands on the conditions and the classification of the wealth received, drawing on the Quranic proof and the principles of jihad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making a peace treaty (sulh) with harbis that involves payment of wealth. Its ruling is permissible (ja'iz) under specific conditions.
The ruling is not absolute; it is contingent on benefit.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is Quran 8:61: 'And if they incline to peace, incline to it.' The imperative indicates permissibility, not obligation, per Hanafi usul.
The verse is cited in the commentary as the primary evidence.
3
Understand the condition of maslahah
The peace is permissible only when there is a genuine benefit (maslahah) and a need (hajah), such as fear of destruction. Without benefit, it is not allowed because jihad is fard.
Benefit is the key condition; it must be assessed by the imam.
4
Classify the wealth received
If the enemy sent a messenger without being besieged, the wealth is treated like jizya (poll tax revenue) and has no khums. If the army surrounded them and then took wealth, it is ghanimah subject to khums.
The distinction depends on the circumstance of the payment.
5
Note the prohibition when no benefit
If there is no benefit, the treaty is not allowed because it would mean abandoning jihad without excuse.
This preserves the obligation of jihad when it is beneficial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونصالحهم ولو بمال إن خيراً، وننبذ لو خيرا،
ونصالحهم كذا في أكثر النسخ، وفي "المستخلص" زاد قبل هذا فصل في الموادعة، ونصالحهم أي إذا رأى الإمام أن يصالح أهل الحرب على مال يأخذونه أو على مال يدفعه عند خوف الهلاك وكان في ذلك مصلحة للمسلمين فيجوز ذلك الصلح؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ جَنَحُوا لِلسَّلْمِ فَاجْنَحْ لَهَا ﴾ (الأنفال:٦١) أي إن مالوا إلى الصلح، بخلاف ما إذا لم يكن فيه خير؛ لأنه يكون ترك جهاد صورة ومعنى وهو فرض فلا يجوز تركه من غير عذر، وهذا إذا كان لنا حاجة، وإن لم يكن لنا حاجة لم يجز، ثم المال الذي يؤخذ منهم بالصلح يصرف مصارف الجزية، ولا خمس فيه إذا لم ينزلوا بساحتهم بل أرسلوا رسولا إلا إذا أحاط الجيش بهم ثم أخذوا المال فهو غنيمة. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٠/١] (فتح)
And we make peace with them even with wealth if it is good, and we denounce if it is good. And we make peace with them: thus in most copies, and in al-Mustakhlas, a chapter on al-Muwada‘ah (truce) is added before this. And we make peace with them: meaning if the imam sees fit to make peace with the people of war for wealth they take or for wealth he pays when fearing destruction, and there is benefit for the Muslims, then such peace is permissible due to His statement: And if they incline to peace, incline to it [Al-Anfal:61] i.e. if they lean toward peace. Unlike when there is no good in it, because it would be abandoning jihad in form and meaning, which is an obligation, so it cannot be left without an excuse. This applies when we have a need; if we have no need, it is not permissible. Then the wealth taken from them in the peace is spent on the expenditures of jizya, and there is no khums (one-fifth) on it if they did not descend to our territory but sent a messenger, unless the army surrounded them and then took the wealth, then it is ghanimah. (Rumz al-Haqaiq 1/420) (Fath)
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on making sulh (peace treaty) with harbis (enemy combatants). The matn states 'we make peace even with payment if beneficial'. The commentary from Fath clarifies that the imam may contract peace by giving or receiving wealth if it serves a maslahah (public interest). The proof is Quran 8:61, which commands inclining to peace when the enemy does. However, if no benefit exists, it is not allowed because jihad is a fard (obligation) that cannot be abandoned without excuse. Conditions: there must be a need (e.g., fear of destruction). Wealth received from such a peace is treated like jizya (poll tax) revenue, with no khums if the enemy merely sent a messenger rather than being besieged. If the army besieged them and then took wealth, it is ghanimah (spoils) subject to khums.

Key Terms
الموادعة (al-muwāda‘ah): Truce or non-aggression pact with enemy.
الصلح (al-sulh): Peace treaty or settlement; here refers to a peace agreement with the enemy.
الجزية (al-jizya): Poll tax levied on non-Muslim subjects; the wealth from sulh is likened to jizya revenue.
الخمس (al-khums): One-fifth share taken from spoils of war (ghanimah).
الغنيمة (al-ghanimah): Spoils of war taken after actual combat or siege.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only when the enemy is weak.

D) Only when the enemy demands it.

Key Takeaway
Making peace with the enemy for payment is permissible only when it serves the Muslims' interest, and the wealth received is treated like jizya unless taken after siege.
