Denouncing a Peace Treaty When Benefit Shifts
Ruling on breaking a peace treaty when benefit shifts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on breaking a peace treaty and apply the conditions for valid denunciation (nabdh).

Core Idea
A peace treaty can be broken when benefit shifts, but only after formal denunciation matching the initial security.

This lesson examines when and how a peace treaty with non-Muslims may be unilaterally revoked by the imam. The Hanafi school permits denunciation (nabdh) when the maslahah of Muslims changes, based on the Prophet's annulment of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah. Understanding the precise conditions ensures that resuming hostilities is lawful and not treacherous.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Extract the primary ruling: the imam may break a peace treaty if he sees greater benefit for Muslims.
The phrase 'وننبذ لو خيراً' (we denounce if it is good) signals permissibility.
2
Locate the proof
The author cites the Prophet's denunciation of the truce with Makkans – a Sunna proof.
The dalil is the Prophet's action at Hudaybiyyah, which shows the principle.
3
Understand the manner of denunciation
The nabdh must match how the aman was given: public if public, private if private.
This prevents treachery and ensures the enemy is aware.
4
Distinguish termination by expiry vs. early breach
If the term expires, the treaty ends automatically – no nabdh needed. If broken early, nabdh is required.
Also note the ruling for a ja‘l (payment) treaty: a proportional refund is required.
5
Apply the analogy
The text draws an analogy to 'permission regarding a stone' – a concise fiqh principle for revocable permissions.
This reinforces that the treaty is revocable under changed conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وننبذ لو خيراً: أي لو صالحهم الإمام مدة ثم رأى أن نقض الصلح أنفع للمسلمين فينبذ إليهم وقاتلهم؛ لأن النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام نبذ الموادعة التي كانت بينه وبين أهل مكة؛ لأن المصلحة لما تبدلت كان النقض جهاداً، ويكون النبذ على الوجه الذي كان الأمان، فإن كان منتشراً يجب أن يكون النبذ كذلك، وإن كان غير منتشر فالنبذ يكون كذلك، وهو على قياس الإذن بالحجر، وهذا إذا نقض الصلح قبل المدة، وأما إذا مضت المدة يبطل الصلح بمضيها، فلا ينبذ إليهم، وإن كان الصلح على جعل، فنقضه قبل مضي المدة رده عليهم بحصته. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٠/١] (مستخلص)
And we denounce if it is good: meaning if the imam made peace with them for a period, then sees that breaking the peace is more beneficial for the Muslims, he should denounce (nabdh) it to them and fight them. Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) denounced the truce between him and the people of Mecca. Since the benefit had changed, the breach became jihad. And the denunciation should be in the manner in which the security (aman) was given: if it was widespread, the denunciation must be widespread; if it was not widespread, the denunciation is likewise. This is analogous to permission regarding a stone. This applies when the peace is broken before the term expires. As for when the term passes, the peace is void by its expiry, so he does not need to denounce. If the peace was for a consideration (ja‘l), then breaking it before the term requires returning a proportionate amount to them. (Rumz al-Haqaiq 1/420) (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage explains the rule of nabdh (denunciation) – formally revoking a peace treaty. The matn phrase 'we denounce if it is good' means that if an existing peace becomes detrimental to Muslims, the imam may break it and resume fighting. The proof is the Prophet's (ﷺ) annulment of the Treaty of Hudaybiyyah when the situation changed. The mechanism: the denunciation must match how the aman was given – public if public, private if private. If the treaty has a fixed term, it ends automatically after that term without need for denunciation. If the treaty involved payment (ja‘l), and it is broken early, the imam must return a proportional amount of the payment.

Key Terms
النَّبْذ (al-nabdh): Formal denunciation of a treaty; breaking the pact with notice.
جعل (ja‘l): Consideration, payment given for peace.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately attack without notice.
B) Inform the enemy of the denunciation (nabdh) in the same manner as the aman was given.
C) Wait until the treaty term expires.
D) Pay a penalty.

Key Takeaway
A peace treaty may be broken early if benefit shifts, but only after formal denunciation matching how the treaty was made; if ja‘l is involved, a proportional refund is due.

Peace with Apostates and the Ruling on Their Wealth
Imam explains peace with apostates and the status of their wealth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on peace with apostates and explain why wealth taken from them is not returned.

Core Idea
Peace with apostates is permissible without wealth; if wealth is taken, it is not returned because it is unprotected.

In the context of jihad, apostates (murtaddin) present a unique challenge. The Imam may find it beneficial to make a temporary peace with them, hoping they return to Islam. However, Hanafi law strictly prohibits taking any wealth from them, and if wealth is taken inadvertently, it must not be returned. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind these rules and contrasts them with other groups like rebels (bughat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key rulings
Locate in the passage the permissibility of peace with apostates without wealth, and the rule that if wealth is taken, it is not returned.
These are the main legal outcomes.
2
Analyze the basis for permissibility of peace
Examine the reason given: 'because Islam is hoped from them' and 'if there is benefit'. Understand that this is a temporary measure, unlike permanent peace with dhimmis.
The standard is maslaha (public interest).
3
Examine the prohibition of taking wealth
The text compares this wealth to jizya, which is not accepted from apostates. Trace the analogy: jizya from people of war (harbiyyun) is permissible, but apostates are excluded from jizya, so taking wealth from them is impermissible.
This is an argument by analogy (qiyas).
4
Understand why taken wealth is not returned
The passage explains that returning it would be 'helping them in fighting'. Apostate wealth is considered ghayr ma'sum (unprotected), unlike the wealth of rebels (bughat) which is returned after the conflict.
The distinction between apostates and rebels is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمرتدين بلا مال، فإن أخذ لم يردّ، ولم نبع سلاحاً منهم، ولم نقتل من آمنه حر أي بدون أخذ مال أي المال على الصلح أو حرة، وننبذ لو شراً، أهل الحرب غير - كانت الخيانة بقتاله بنفسه أو بقتال جماعة ذي منعة بإذنه فيصيروا ناقضين للعهد باتفاقهم على ذلك، فلو خانوا بدون إذنه انتقض العهد في حقهم فقط دون غيرهم؛ لأنه بغير إذن الملك لا يلزم غيرهم. (مستخلص، فتح) والمرتدين بلا مال أي ونصالح المرتدين على ترك قتالهم مدة معلومة من أن تأخذ منهم مالاً، أما جواز الصلح؛ فلأن الإسلام مرجو منهم، فجاز تأخير قتالهم طمعاً للإسلام إذا كانت فيه مصلحة، وأما عدم جواز أخذ المال؛ فلأنه يشبه الجزية المأخوذة من الحربيين والجزية لا تقبل من المرتدين. (مستخلص ،مسكين ،عيني) فإن أخذ لم يرد أي فلو أخذ منهم مال مع أنه لا يجوز لم يرد هذا المال عليهم؛ لأنه مال غير معصوم، وفي الرد عليهم إعانة لهم للقتال معنى، بخلاف أخذ المال من البغاة، فإنه يرد بعد وضع الحرب أوزارها؛ لأنه ليس فيئاً. (مستخلص،فتح)
And with the apostates without wealth, then if taken it is not returned. And we do not sell weapons to them. And we do not kill one who has given protection, [whether] free [man] or free woman. And we repudiate if it is evil. The people of war other than... The treachery was by him fighting himself or by fighting a group with strength by his permission, so they become violators of the covenant by their consensus on that. If they betray without his permission, the covenant is broken only regarding them, not others, because without the king's permission it does not obligate others. (Mukhtasar, Fath) 'And with the apostates without wealth' means: we make peace with the apostates on condition of abandoning fighting them for a known period, without taking wealth from them. As for the permissibility of peace: because Islam is hoped from them, so delaying their fighting is permitted hoping for Islam if there is benefit in it. As for the impermissibility of taking wealth: because it resembles the jizya taken from the people of war, and jizya is not accepted from apostates. (Mukhtasar, Miskin, Ayni) 'If taken, it is not returned' means: if wealth is taken from them despite it being impermissible, this wealth is not returned to them because it is unprotected wealth, and returning it would be in meaning assisting them in fighting. Unlike taking wealth from rebels, for it is returned after the war lays down its burdens because it is not fay'. (Mukhtasar, Fath)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about peace treaties with apostates (murtaddin). The author clarifies that the Imam may make a peace treaty with apostates without taking any wealth from them, for a specified period. The reason for permissibility is that there is hope they will return to Islam, so delaying force can be beneficial. However, taking wealth is not allowed because it resembles jizya, which is not accepted from apostates. If wealth is taken in violation of this, it is not returned because the apostates' wealth is not protected (ghayr ma'sum), and returning it would effectively aid them in fighting. This contrasts with rebels (bughat), whose wealth is returned after conflict because it is not considered fay' (spoils).

Key Terms
المرتدين (al-murtaddin): Apostates: those who have left Islam. Peace with them is permissible without wealth.
مدة معلومة (muddatun ma'lumatun): A specified period for the peace treaty with apostates.
فيئاً (fay'an): Spoils obtained without war, which go to the public treasury.
البغاة (al-bughat): Rebels: Muslims who rise against the legitimate ruler. Their wealth is returned after conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the Imam takes wealth from apostates during a peace treaty, what should be done?
A) It must be returned immediately.
B) It is not returned because their wealth is unprotected.
C) It is returned after the war ends.
D) It is distributed to the army.

Key Takeaway
Peace with apostates is permissible without wealth; any wealth taken is not returned because it is not protected.

Prohibition of Selling Weapons to People of War
Author prohibits sale of weapons and related items to enemy combatants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling weapons to enemy combatants and identify which items are prohibited and which are permissible by istihsan.

Core Idea
Selling weapons to enemies is haram; but food and cloth are permissible by istihsan.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a key issue in the laws of war (jihad): trade with the enemy. The author prohibits selling weapons and related items but makes an exception for food and cloth based on the Prophet's ﷺ precedent with Thumamah. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Islamic commercial ethics during armed conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed? The sale of weapons to the people of war (ahl al-harb). What is the ruling? It is haram (prohibited).
The ruling is based on a clear prohibition from the Prophet ﷺ.
2
Locate the primary dalil
The primary evidence is the Prophet's ﷺ prohibition of selling weapons to enemies and carrying weapons to them. This is a hadith. Identify its type and strength.
The hadith is ahad (solitary) in transmission, so its thubut is zanni, but its dalalah (indication) is qati (definitive) based on Hanafi usul.
3
Note the extension to similar items
The text extends the prohibition to horses, iron, and slaves. Why? Because they all strengthen the enemy in war. Iron is the raw material for weapons, horses are used in combat, and slaves reproduce and increase enemy numbers.
The extension is based on the same effective cause (illa): strengthening the enemy.
4
Understand the exception and its basis
Food and cloth are excluded from the prohibition. This is allowed by istihsan (juristic preference) based on the Prophet's command to Thumamah to supply food to the people of Makkah when they were at war. Note that analogy (qiyas) would prohibit these as well, but istihsan overrides it.
Istihsan is a valid source of law in the Hanafi school, allowing a departure from qiyas when a stronger evidence exists.
5
Recognize the practical scope
Determine which items are prohibited and which are permissible. The prohibition applies regardless of whether a peace treaty exists, because the enemy remains potentially hostile. The permissibility of food and cloth is subject to the condition that it does not actively aid combat.
This ruling applies to any transaction that strengthens the enemy in their war effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم نبع إلخ أي لا ينبغي أن يباع السلاح من أهل الحرب؛ لأن النبي عليهة نهى عن بيع السلاح من أهل الحرب وحمله إليهم؛ ولأن فيه تقويتهم على قتال المسلمين، وكذا لا يباع منهم الخيل والحديد؛ لأن الحديد أصل السلاح سواء كان البيع قبل الصلح أو بعده؛ لأنه على شرف النقض والانتقاض، وكان حرباً علينا، وكذا بيع الرقيق؛ لأنهم يتوالدون فيعودون حرباً علينا، بخلاف القماش والطعام حيث يجوز استحساناً بالنص، وهو أمره عليها ثمامة بضم المثلة، أن يمير أهل مكة أي يأتيهم بالطعام، وهم حرب على النبي عليه، والقياس أن لا يجوز. (مستخلص، فتح) سلاحاً : أ: ما يكون معداً للقتال ويستعمل في الحرب. (معدن)
'And we do not sell' etc. meaning: it is not befitting that weapons be sold to the people of war, because the Prophet ﷺ forbade the sale of weapons to the people of war and carrying them to them, and because it strengthens them against fighting the Muslims. Likewise, horses and iron should not be sold to them, because iron is the origin of weapons, whether the sale is before or after peace, because they are on the verge of breaking and being broken, and they are at war with us. Likewise, the sale of slaves [to them is prohibited] because they will reproduce and return as enemies against us. Unlike cloth and food, where it is permissible by istihsan based on the text: his command to Thumamah (with a damma on the tha) that he supply the people of Mecca i.e. bring food to them, while they were at war with the Prophet ﷺ, and analogy would suggest it is not permissible. (Mukhtasar, Fath) 'Weapon': that which is prepared for fighting and used in war. (Ma'din)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof prohibiting the sale of weapons to the people of war (ahl al-harb). The proof is the Prophet's ﷺ explicit prohibition against selling weapons to enemies and carrying them to them. Additionally, selling items like horses and iron is included because iron is the raw material for weapons. This prohibition applies whether before or after a peace treaty, as enemies are always potential adversaries. Similarly, selling slaves to them is prohibited because they will reproduce and produce more enemies. However, selling cloth and food is permissible by istihsan (juristic preference), based on the incident where the Prophet ﷺ ordered Thumamah to supply food to the people of Mecca when they were at war. Analogy would prohibit it, but istihsan prevails due to the prophetic directive.

Key Terms
السلاح (al-silah): Items prepared for fighting and used in war, including weapons and iron.
استحساناً (istihsanan): Juristic preference that permits selling food/cloth despite qiyas, based on prophetic practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following may be sold to the people of war?
A) Swords
B) Iron
C) Slaves
D) Food

Key Takeaway
Selling weapons, iron, horses, and slaves to enemies is haram, but food and cloth are permissible by istihsan based on prophetic precedent.

Validity of Aman from a Free Muslim
Author states ruling with proof and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on aman granted by a free Muslim and identify the conditions, proofs, and exceptions that make it binding.

Core Idea
Any free Muslim can grant aman that binds the whole community, based on equality and Umm Hani's precedent.

A common assumption is that only the Imam can grant safe conduct to enemies. However, the Hanafi school holds that any free Muslim—man or woman—may do so. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proofs from the Sunnah, and the conditions that must be met.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage discusses the validity of aman (safe conduct) given by a free Muslim to one or more non-Muslims. The ruling is that such aman is valid and must be honored by all Muslims.
The act is granting aman; the ruling is its validity.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
Find the hadith: 'The blood of Muslims is equal' and the incident of Umm Hani at the conquest of Mecca. Understand how these establish that any Muslim's aman binds the community.
The hadith is cited as a proof of equality among Muslims.
3
Analyze the condition of freedom
Note that the text specifies 'free Muslim' and explains that a slave is not from the people of fighting, hence cannot grant valid aman. Consider why freedom is essential.
Freedom is a condition because slaves are not part of ahl al-qital.
4
Identify other conditions and exceptions
Look for implicit conditions: the grantor must be sane (the insane child's aman is invalid). Also note that the Imam may repudiate an aman if it causes harm (evil), and discipline the individual.
Sane child restricted from fighting is like a slave.
5
Summarize the practical implications
Synthesize: any free, sane Muslim can grant aman, and the community must respect it unless the Imam repudiates it for harm. This ensures flexibility in warfare and protects the pledge of any believer.
The ruling is from zahir al-riwayah and mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم نقتل من آمنه إلخ أي إذا آمن رجل حر أو امرأة حرة كافراً أو جماعة أو أهل حصن أو أهل مدينة . الأمان، ولم يكن لأحد من المسلمين قتالهم؛ لقوله علي: "المسلمون تتكافأ دماؤهم" أي تتساوى، فلا يزيد دية الشريف على دية الوضيع ويسعى بذمتهم أدناهم، أي أقلهم عدداً ، وهو الواحد، ولأن كل واحد من المسلمين من أهل القتال. أما الرجل فظاهر، أما المرأة فمن جهة التسبب، إما بالمال أو بالعبيد، فيتحقق الأمان منها؛ لملاقاته محله، ولأنه أجاز عليها أمان أم هانئ رجلاً من المشركين يوم فتح مكة فيما رواه البخاري ومسلم وأحمد، وإنما قيد الحرية في الواحد ؛ لأن الرقيق ليس من أهل القتال. وننبذ لو شراً: أي ينبذ الإمام أمان الواحد لو كان الأمان شراً بأن كان فيه مفسدة كما إذا آمن الإمام بنفسه ثم رأى المصلحة في النبذ، ويؤدب الإمام ذلك الواحد لانفراده برأيه، والصبي الذي لا يعقل لا يصح أمانه، وإن كان يعقل فالمحجور عن القتال كالعبد مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
'And we do not kill one who has given protection' etc. meaning: if a free man or free woman gives protection (aman) to a disbeliever or a group or the people of a fortress or a city, then no Muslim may fight them, due to his statement ﷺ: 'The blood of Muslims is equal' i.e. equivalent. So the blood money of a noble is not increased over that of a lowly one, and the lowest of them can give protection on behalf of all. And because every Muslim belongs to the people of fighting. As for the man, it is apparent; as for the woman, from the aspect of causation, either through wealth or slaves, so her aman is realized because it meets its place, and because the aman of Umm Hani for a man from the polytheists on the day of the conquest of Mecca was permitted by him, as narrated by al-Bukhari, Muslim, and Ahmad. The condition of freedom is specified for the individual because the slave is not from the people of fighting. 'And we repudiate if it is evil' meaning: the Imam can repudiate the aman of an individual if the aman is evil, i.e., if there is harm in it, as when the Imam himself gives aman then sees benefit in repudiation. The Imam should discipline that individual for acting independently. The aman of an insane child is not valid; if he possesses reason but is restricted from fighting, he is like a slave. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof about the validity of aman (safe conduct) given by a free Muslim man or woman to one or more disbelievers. The proof is the hadith 'The blood of Muslims is equal' and the established incident of Umm Hani in the conquest of Mecca. The freedom of the individual is required because a slave is not considered part of the people of fighting. The Imam may repudiate an aman if it causes harm, and should discipline the one who gave it. The aman of an insane child is invalid, and a discerning child restricted from fighting is like a slave (i.e., his aman is invalid).

Key Terms
الأمان (al-aman): Safe conduct granted to a non-Muslim enemy, making them inviolable by Muslims.
يتكافأ دماؤهم (yatakafa'u dima'uhum): Their blood is equal; no distinction in blood money or protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, whose aman (safe conduct) is valid for an enemy combatant?
A) Only the Imam
B) Any free Muslim man or woman
C) Only adult men
D) Only scholars

Key Takeaway
Aman from any free Muslim (male or female) is valid and binding on all Muslims, as long as the grantor is sane and not a slave.

Invalidity of Aman from Dhimmi, Captive, Trader, and Restricted Slave
Lists invalid aman givers and explains the dispute on restricted slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the individuals whose aman is invalid according to Hanafi fiqh and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the restricted slave.

Core Idea
Aman from a restricted slave is invalid in the relied-upon Hanafi position, despite disagreement.

This lesson examines who can validly grant aman (safe passage) to enemies in jihad. The Hanafi school generally requires the giver to be a Muslim with authority, but special cases arise: dhimmi, captive, trader, and restricted slave. The text lists these four categories as invalid, then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute over the slave. Understanding this dispute clarifies the school's position on authority and reliability in granting aman.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on invalid aman givers
From the text, extract the four categories whose aman is invalid: dhimmi, captive, trader, and a slave restricted from fighting (mahjur). Note the reasons given for each.
The reasons for dhimmi: suspicion and lack of authority. For captive/trader: lack of fear and possible coercion.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text states that the aman of a restricted slave is invalid according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), but valid according to Muhammad. Recognize this as a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
Also note that Abu Yusuf later agreed with Muhammad according to al-Karkhi, but the mufta bihi is the earlier position.
3
Follow the proof of each side
Abu Hanifa's proof: the slave's aman is a disposal over the master's property, and a restricted slave cannot bind his master; also, the slave might be sick or old, making him unreliable. Muhammad's proof: generally, a mukallaf Muslim can give aman; a slave is mukallaf.
The external schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali in one narration) agree with Muhammad.
4
Determine the tarjih (preferred view)
The text indicates that the view of the two shaykhs (invalid) is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position. This is because a restricted slave lacks authority and cannot guarantee protection.
A permitted slave's aman is valid by consensus, as stated at the end.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If a restricted slave gives aman, according to Abu Hanifa it is invalid and the enemy can be killed; according to Muhammad, the aman binds Muslims and the enemy is protected.
This difference affects battlefield decisions and liability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطل أمان ذمي وأسير وتاجر وعبد محجور عن القتال. لأنهم متهم بهم أي وبطل أمان عبد وبطل أمان ذمي إلخ أي لا يجوز أمان الذمي؛ لأنه متهم بهم، ولا ولاية له على المسلمين إلا إذا أمره أمير العسكر أن يؤمنهم فيجوز؛ لزوال ذلك المعنى، وكذلك لا يجوز أمان الأسير والتاجر؛ لأنهم لا يخافونهما، والأمان يختص بمحل الخوف، ولأنهما يجبران عليه، فيعرى الأمان عن المصلحة، ولأنهم كل ما اشتد عليهم الأمر فربما يجدون أسيراً أو تاجراً فيتخلصون بأمانه، فلا ينفتح باب الفتح. يصح أمان العبد وكذا من أسلم في دار الحرب ولم يهاجر علينا، لا يصح أمانه، وقوله: "وعبد محجور" إلخ أي لا المحجور عليه عن القتال عند الشيخين، وعند محمد يجوز لمطلق ما روينا وبه قال الشافعي ومالك وأحمد في رواية، وذكر الكرخي أن أبا يوسف مع محمد، ولأبي حنيفة : أنه تصرف على المولى، وأنه لا يعرى عن احتمال أمان المريض أو الشيخ الفاني؛ لأنه من أهل القتال بمال أو رأي وإنما قيد بالمحجور؛ لأنه لو كان الضرر، ويصح مأذوناً يصح أمانه اتفاقاً. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني)
And the aman of a dhimmi, a captive, a trader, and a slave restricted from fighting is invalid. Because they are suspected [of bias]. Meaning: the aman of a slave is invalid, and the aman of a dhimmi is invalid. It is not permissible for a dhimmi to give aman because he is suspected [of bias] and has no authority over Muslims unless the commander of the army orders him to give aman; then it is permissible due to the removal of that meaning. Similarly, the aman of a captive and a trader is invalid because they are not feared, and aman is specific to a place of fear; and because they can be forced into it, so the aman is devoid of benefit; and because whenever the situation becomes severe for them, they might find a captive or trader and extricate themselves through his aman, so the door of conquest would not open. The aman of a slave is valid. Likewise, one who becomes Muslim in dar al-harb and does not migrate to us, his aman is invalid. His statement: 'and a slave restricted' etc. means: the one restricted from fighting according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is not valid, but according to Muhammad it is permissible in absolute terms as we narrated, and this is also the view of al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad in one narration. Al-Karkhi mentioned that Abu Yusuf agreed with Muhammad. Abu Hanifa's proof: it is a disposal over the master, and it is not free from the possibility that a sick person or an old man might give aman because he is among the people of fighting through wealth or opinion; the restriction to a mahjur (restricted) slave is specified because if he were permitted, his aman would be valid by consensus. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute about whose aman is invalid. The general ruling: the aman of a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject), a captive (prisoner of war), a trader (from dar al-harb), and a slave restricted from fighting is invalid. The reasons: a dhimmi is suspected of partiality and has no authority; a captive and trader are not feared and can be coerced, so their aman lacks benefit. Then there is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the slave: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs), a restricted slave's aman is invalid because it is a disposal over the owner's property and cannot be relied upon; but Imam Muhammad says it is absolutely valid. Abu Yusuf is reported by al-Karkhi to have agreed with Muhammad. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that the aman of a restricted slave is invalid, while a permitted slave's aman is valid by consensus.

Key Terms
محجور عن القتال (mahjur 'an al-qital): A slave who is restricted from fighting, either by the master's prohibition or because he is not authorized to engage in combat.
الشيخين (al-shaykhayn): Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two founding scholars of the Hanafi school).
أمان (aman): Safe passage or guarantee of protection granted to an enemy combatant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, is the aman of a slave restricted from fighting valid?
A) Yes, absolutely
B) No, it is invalid
C) Valid only if the slave is wealthy
D) Valid only if the imam approves

Key Takeaway
The aman of a restricted slave is invalid in the Hanafi school, following the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Definitions of War Spoils and Imam's Options for Conquered Land
Definitions, rulings, and inter-madhhab dispute on war spoils

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ghanimah, fay', and nafal, and state the Hanafi ruling on the Imam's options for conquered land, including the dispute with al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The Imam may either divide conquered land among fighters or keep it as public treasury with kharaj, regardless of fighters' consent.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines key terms of war spoils and presents the Hanafi view on the Imam's discretion over conquered land. You will examine the definitions, the dispute with al-Shafi'i, and the reasoning behind Umar's policy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of war spoils
Read the definitions of ghanimah, fay', and nafal. Note the key differences in timing (during war vs after war) and distribution (fighters only vs all Muslims).
Pay attention to what types of property fall under each.
2
State the ruling on each category
Ghanimah is divided after khums among participants; fay' belongs to all Muslims without khums; nafal is extra incentive given by the Imam.
Consider the wisdom behind these distinctions.
3
Analyze the Imam's options for conquered land
The Imam may either divide the land among fighters (like Khaybar) or leave the inhabitants with jizyah and kharaj (like Sawad). This option exists regardless of fighters' consent.
This is the core ruling of the text.
4
Follow the dispute: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Al-Shafi'i argues the land becomes the property of the conquerors. The Hanafi response relies on Umar's action and Companion consensus.
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi proof
The Hadith of Umar and the silent consensus (ijma') of the Companions form the primary evidence. Consider why Umar chose to keep the land as a treasury.
This is a case of dalil naqli and ijma'.

Chapter of Spoils and Their Division. i.e., concerning the explanation of their rulings. i.e., spoils. What the Imam conquers by force is divided among us, or he lets the inhabitants remain and imposes jizyah and kharaj, and kills captives upon their heads. i.e., the Imam. Chapter of Spoils and Their Division: Know that ghanimah is what is taken from the disbelievers by force during the state of war, and its ruling is that after removing the khums, it is divided specifically among the participants, and no one else has a share in it. And fay' is what is taken from them after the war has ceased and the land becomes Dar al-Islam, and its ruling is that it belongs to all Muslims, and it is not subject to khums. And nafal is what is given to the fighter in addition to his share, such as when the Imam or commander says: 'Whoever kills a slain enemy, his spoils are his' or says to a patrol: 'Whatever you acquire is yours.' In the terminology of the Muslims, everything that is permissible to take from their wealth is fay'. (Mukhtasar: Fath 'anwatan: i.e., by force and overpowering, accusative as specification. (Ayni) 'Divided among us' etc.: i.e., when the Imam conquers a town by force, he has the option: if he wishes, he divides it among the participants after taking out the khums, following the Prophet's action at Khaybar; or he lets its inhabitants remain and imposes jizyah on their heads and kharaj on their lands, as Umar did in Sawad al-Iraq, whether the participants are pleased or not. Al-Shafi'i said: He does not have that option because the land has become the property of the participants through their conquest, so it cannot be taken from them. Ahmad said the same in one narration. Our proof: What is narrated from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'By Him in Whose hand is my soul, were it not that I would leave the people with no share, I would not have conquered any town except that I would divide it as the Messenger of Allah divided Khaybar, but I leave it as a treasury for them to share.' Narrated by al-Bukhari. 'Baban' with two ba's and tashdid on the second: i.e., I leave them as one thing; because if the conquered lands are divided among the participants, those who were not present at the division and those who come later from the Muslims would be left with nothing. The meaning is to equalize them in that matter so that it becomes one thing with no preference for one over another. And Umar's action in Sawad al-Iraq as mentioned was with the agreement of the Companions and no one objected, so it was a consensus of the Companions. And those who opposed him, like Bilal and his companions, were not praised. It is also narrated that he said on the pulpit: 'O Allah, suffice me against the evil of Bilal and his companions,' and before the year passed, they died. If it is said: How can consensus be established with their opposition? We say: Their disagreement is not considered against the consensus of the jurists. Some of our scholars said: Division among the participants is preferable when they are in need; if they have no need, the second option is better to serve as a reserve for emergencies. This applies to real estate. As for movable property alone, it is not permissible to grant amnesty to them over it because it is not found in the law. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni) And killing captives: [If he wishes, i.e., other than those we are forbidden to kill, if they do not accept Islam. But if they accept Islam, they are not killed. (Ayni)] i.e., the Imam has the option regarding captives: if he wishes, he may kill them because the Prophet killed the Banu Qurayzah, and because it cuts off the root of corruption; if he wishes, he may enslave them because it repels their evil while providing abundant benefit for the Muslims; if he wishes, he may leave them free as dhimmis for the Muslims and impose kharaj on them as Umar did in Sawad al-Iraq, except for the polytheists of Arabia and apostates, as will come. Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq is from the chapter on spoils of war (ghana'im) and their division. The passage type is a ruling with proof, including definitions and a dispute with al-Shafi'i. The text begins by defining three key terms: ghanimah (spoils taken during active warfare), fay' (property acquired after war ceases), and nafal (bonus given to fighters). Ghanimah is divided among the fighters after removing one-fifth (khums). Fay' belongs to the entire Muslim community and is not subject to khums. Nafal is an extra incentive given by the commander. The main ruling discusses the Imam's options when conquering a town by force (anwatan): he may either divide the land among the fighters (following the Khaybar precedent) or leave the inhabitants in place and impose jizyah (poll tax) on their heads and kharaj (land tax) on their lands (following Umar's practice in Sawad al-Iraq). This option exists regardless of whether the fighters are content. Al-Shafi'i disagreed, arguing that the land becomes the property of the conquerors and cannot be taken away. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of Umar where he feared that dividing land would leave future generations with nothing, so he kept it as a public treasury (khizanah), and the Companions agreed, establishing consensus (ijma'). The text also notes that some Hanafi scholars prefer division if the fighters are in need, otherwise leaving the land as a reserve for emergencies is better. This applies to real estate; movable property cannot be subject to granting amnesty (mann) as it is not sanctioned. The passage then turns to captives: the Imam has three options: kill (as the Prophet did with Banu Qurayzah), enslave, or set free as dhimmis with kharaj. Exceptions include polytheist Arabs and apostates, who will be discussed later. The text is cut off mid-objection.

Key Terms
غنيمة (ghanīmah): Property taken from disbelievers by force during active warfare; divided after khums among participants.
فيء (fay'): Property taken from disbelievers after warfare ceases or without fighting; belongs to all Muslims.
نفل (nafal): Additional reward given to fighters beyond their regular share, often as an incentive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is divided among the fighters after khums
B) It is left as a treasury for future generations
C) The Imam may grant amnesty to the owners
D) It belongs to the Imam alone

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the Imam discretion over conquered land, prioritizing the long-term benefit of all Muslims over immediate distribution to fighters.

Definitions of Ghanimah, Fay', and Nafal
Defines three categories of war booty and their rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ghanimah, fay', and nafal and state their respective rulings on khums and distribution.

Core Idea
War booty is not one category; ghanimah is khumsed for fighters, fay' for all Muslims, and nafal is extra incentive.

This lesson introduces three key terms in the fiqh of jihad. The matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents clear definitions and the legal rulings that follow. Understanding these categories is essential before studying detailed rules of booty division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
Locate ghanimah, fay', and nafal in the Arabic text. Note their common root of 'taking' from the disbelievers.
2
Understand the condition for each
Ghanimah is taken by force during active war. Fay' is taken after war ceases or peaceably. Nafal is an extra grant over the regular share.
The condition of timing and force is the key distinction.
3
State the ruling on khums
Ghanimah is subject to khums (one-fifth) before distribution to fighters. Fay' and nafal are not subject to khums.
4
State the distribution
Ghanimah goes exclusively to the fighters. Fay' belongs to all Muslims (bayt al-mal). Nafal is given as an incentive by the Imam.

Chapter on spoils and their division: Know that ghanimah is what is taken from the disbelievers by force during the time of war, and its ruling is that it is divided after the khums exclusively for the fighters, and no share for others. Fay' is what is taken from them after the laying down of arms and the land becomes Dar al-Islam, and its ruling is that it belongs to all Muslims and is not subject to khums. Commentary
This passage is a definition section from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. It distinguishes three categories of war booty: ghanimah (spoils taken during active hostilities), fay' (wealth obtained after cessation of war, including land conquered peaceably or after war ends), and nafal (additional grant to soldiers as incentive). The rulings: ghanimah is divided among the fighters after deducting the khums (one-fifth for the state); fay' is not subject to khums and is for the general Muslim treasury (bayt al-mal); nafal is over and above the regular share. The final sentence clarifies that in Muslim usage, all permissible war acquisitions are called fay' in a general sense, but the specific categories have distinct rules.

Key Terms
الغنيمة (al-ghanimah): Wealth taken from disbelievers by force during active war.
الفيء (al-fay'): Wealth taken from disbelievers after war ceases or peaceably.
النفل (al-nafal): Extra grant to a fighter beyond his standard share, as incentive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Ghanimah

D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Ghanimah is for fighters after khums; fay' is for all Muslims without khums; nafal is extra incentive.

Disposition of Conquered Land: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Ruling on disposition of land conquered by force, with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on conquered land, contrast it with the Shafi'i view, and evaluate the Hanafi evidence using Umar's hadith and ijma'.

Core Idea
The Imam may leave conquered land under kharaj – a choice rooted in maslaha, not forced division.

This passage explores a key dispute in Islamic land law: must the Imam divide conquered land among the fighters, or can he leave it with the original inhabitants under tax? The Hanafi school, following the precedent of Umar (ra), grants the Imam discretion. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that the land becomes private property of the fighters. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how different usul (proofs) and considerations of public welfare shape fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the Hanafi ruling
The act is the disposition of land conquered by force (anwatan). The Hanafi ruling: the Imam has a choice between dividing among fighters (after khums) or leaving it with inhabitants under jizya/kharaj.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Shafi'i holds that the land becomes the property of the fighters upon conquest, so it cannot be taken away.
Ahmad also held this in one narration.
3
Analyse the Hanafi evidence
Primary evidence: Umar's hadith in Bukhari and the silent consensus (sukuti ijma') of the Companions on Umar's action in Sawad of Iraq.
The hadith shows Umar's intention to preserve land for future Muslims.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi preference
Some Hanafi scholars prefer division if fighters are in need; otherwise leaving the land is better as a reserve for future emergencies.
This preference does not affect the core ruling.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Shafi'i, land must be divided; under Hanafi, it can be preserved as state property generating revenue (e.g., as waqf).

What the Imam conquers by force, he divides among us, or he leaves its inhabitants in place and imposes jizya and kharaj on them. That is, any town from the lands of disbelief. (Summary: conquering by force: i.e., by compulsion and domination, set as a specification. (Ayni) 'Divides among us' etc.: i.e., when the Imam conquers a town by force, he has the choice: if he wishes, he divides it among the fighters after taking the khums, following the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in Khaybar; or he leaves its inhabitants on it and imposes jizya on their heads and kharaj on their lands as Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) did in Sawad of Iraq, whether the fighters are pleased or not. Al-Shafi'i said: He does not have that option because it has become the property of the fighters by virtue of their seizure and domination, so it is not permissible to take it from them. Ahmad also said this in one narration. Our evidence: What is narrated from Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that he said: 'By Him in Whose hand is my soul, were it not that I would leave the people as one group with nothing, I would not conquer any village except I divided it as the Messenger of Allah divided Khaybar, but I leave it as a treasury for them to inherit.' Bukhari narrated it. 'Baban' with two baa's, the second with shaddah, meaning: I leave them as one thing. Because if he divides the conquered lands among the fighters, those who were not present in the division and those who come later from the Muslims would be left with nothing. The meaning is: I will equalize them in that so that it becomes one thing with no preference for one over another. Umar's action in Sawad of Iraq was done with the agreement of the Companions and no one denied it, so that was ijma' of the Companions. Those who opposed him like Bilal (may Allah be pleased with him) and his companions were not praised. It is narrated that he said on the pulpit: 'O Allah, suffice me against the evil of Bilal and his companions.' Within the year they died. If it is said: how can ijma' be established with their opposition? We say: their disagreement is not considered alongside the consensus of the jurists. Some of our associates said: The preferable option is division among the fighters when they are in need; if there is no need, the second option is better to be a reserve for emergencies. This applies to real estate. As for movable property, granting it to them is not permissible because it is not established in sharia.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i on the disposition of land conquered by force. The Hanafi position, based on the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab and consensus of the Companions, holds that the Imam has the choice: either to divide the land among the fighters (after khums) or to leave it in the hands of the original inhabitants with jizya and kharaj. The Shafi'i position says this is not allowed because the land becomes the property of the fighters immediately upon conquest. The Hanafi proof includes Umar's statement from Bukhari, the ijma' of the Companions on Umar's action, and the reasoning that leaving land as a treasury for future generations is beneficial. The opposition of some Companions like Bilal is dismissed because the great majority of jurists agreed. Additionally, some Hanafi scholars opine that division is preferable if fighters are needy; otherwise, leaving it is better for the Muslim community. This ruling applies to landed property, not movables.

Key Terms
عنوةً (anwatan): By force, through military conquest.
الخراج (al-kharaj): Land tax imposed on conquered non-Muslim inhabitants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Must divide it among the fighters immediately.
B) May either divide it among fighters or leave it to inhabitants with kharaj.
C) Must leave it as communal property for all Muslims.
D) Must return it to the original owners.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school gives the Imam the option to leave conquered land under kharaj, prioritizing the public interest over immediate division, based on Umar's practice and Companions' consensus.

Treatment of Captives in Hanafi Siyar: Options and Prohibitions
Rulings on treatment of prisoners of war and prohibitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three options available to the Imam regarding captives, and identify which actions are prohibited (fida', mann) along with the evidence of abrogation.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school prohibits ransoming or freeing captives gratuitously due to abrogation by the verse of the sword.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq deals with the treatment of prisoners of war (usara) in Hanafi fiqh. The Imam has three choices: kill, enslave, or make them free dhimmis. However, the text then prohibits returning captives to dar al-harb, ransoming them (fida'), and releasing them without compensation (mann). These prohibitions are based on the principle of abrogation, where earlier verses permitting mann and fida' in Surah Muhammad are superseded by the verse of the sword in Surah Bara'ah. The intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples regarding exchanging captives is also examined.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Imam's three options for captives
From the text, list the three options: killing, enslavement, and making them free dhimmis with kharaj. Note the exception for Arab polytheists and apostates.
The option of dhimmi status is limited to non-Arab non-Muslims not apostates.
2
Understand the prohibition of returning captives
The text prohibits sending captives back to enemy territory because it strengthens the enemy. Note the exception if they become Muslim before capture.
If captives embrace Islam before capture, they cannot be enslaved or killed.
3
Analyze the prohibition of fida' (ransom) and mann (gratuitous release)
The text states that both fida' and mann are prohibited. However, there is intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Hanifa forbids even exchanging captives, while the two disciples allow exchanging captives (but not money ransom in general).
Muhammad allows money ransom if Muslims have need; Abu Yusuf allows it only before division of spoils.
4
Examine the evidence for abrogation
The Hanafi position relies on the verse of the sword (Surah Bara'ah) abrogating the earlier permission in Surah Muhammad. The Prophet's reprimand at Badr for taking ransom is cited as proof.
The opposition (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) argue that Surah Muhammad is later, but Hanafis counter that the verse of the sword is in the last revealed surah.
5
Distinguish the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In Hanafi, captives must be killed or enslaved (except if they become Muslim before capture). In other schools, they may be ransomed or released. Know the difference for fatwa.
The mufta bihi in Hanafi is the prohibition of mann and fida'; exchange of captives is permissible according to some.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقتل الأسرى: [ إن شاء أي غير الذي نهينا عن قتلهم إذا لم يسلموا، وأما إذا أسلموا فلا يقتل. (عيني)] أي الإمام في الأسارى بالخيار إن شاء قتلهم؛ لأنه عليه السلام قتل بني قريظة، ولأن فيه حسم مادة الفساد، وإن شاء جعلهم أرقاء؛ لأن فيه دفع شرهم مع وفور المنفعة لأهل الإسلام، وإن شاء تركهم أحراراً ذمة للمسلمين، ويضع عليهم الخراج كما فعل عمر رضي الله عنه بسواد العراق إلا في مشركي العرب والمرتدين على ما يجيء، فإن قيل: =  = ينبغي أن لا يخير في ترك القتل؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاقْتُلُوهُمْ حَيْثُ وَجَدْتُمُوهُمْ ﴾ (النساء: ٨٩) قلنا: قد اختص من هذا النص أهل الذمة والمستأمنون والنساء فيختص المتنازع فيه بفعل عمر (عيني، فتح) أحراراً ذمةً لنا: أي حال كونهم ذمة للمسلمين غير مشركي العرب والمرتدين فلا يقبل منهم إلا الإسلام أو السيف (ط) وحرم ردّهم: أي لا يجوز للإمام أن يرد الأسارى إلى دار الحرب؛ لأن فيه تقويتهم على المسلمين، حرباً عليهم فإن أسلموا لا يقتلهم؛ لأن الشر قد اندفع بدون القتل، ولكن له أن يسترقهم توفيراً للمنفعة بعد انعقاد السبب الموجب للتملك، وهو الأخذ والاستيلاء، بخلاف ما أسلموا قبل الأخذ حيث لا يجوز استرقاقهم؛ لأنه صاروا أولى الناس بأنفسهم قبل انعقاد السبب للملك. (مستخلص، فتح) والفداء: [ أي وحرم أيضاً فكاك الأسير عندنا بالأسير المسلم عندهم (محشي بالكسر والمد وبالفتح مع القصر مصدر فداه استنفده، والمفاداة بين اثنين بأن تدفع رجلاً وتأخذ رجلاً، والفداء أن تشتريه، أي وحرم الفداء أيضاً فلا يجوز للإمام أن يعطي أساراهم، ويستنفد بهم أسارى المسلمين عن أيديهم عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا: يجوز مفاداة الأسارى بأسارى المسلمين، وأما المفاداة بالمال فلا يجوز عندنا، وقال الشافعي: يجوز الأمران أي المفاداة بالأسارى وبالمال؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِمَّا مَناً بَعْدُ وَإِمَّا فِدَاءً (محمد:٤)، وقال محمد: لا بأس بأخذ المال فداء إذا كان للمسلمين حاجة وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة وعن أبي يوسف يجوز ذلك قبل القسمة لا بعدها. قلنا: نسخ ذلك بآية السيف، ولهما في جواز مفاداة الأسارى بالأسارى: أن في ذلك تخليص المسلم من يد الكافر، وذلك أولى من قتل الكافر أو الانتفاع به بالاسترقاق، وله: أن المفاداة إعانة لأعداء الدين وتقوية لهم حرباً علينا، ودفع شر الحرب أولى من استنفاد الأسير المسلم. (عيني، فتح) والمن: أي وحرم المن أيضاً الأسارى، أخذ مجاناً أن يطلقهم الإمام وهو شيء، وقال الشافعي يجوز وبه قال مالك وأحمد للآية بغیر المذكورة، ولما فعله عليه السلام من المن على بعض الأسارى يوم بدر وأخذ الفداء عن أنفسهم. قلنا: نسخ ذلك كله بآية السيف؛ لأن المن والفداء مذكور في سورة محمد، وآية السيف نزلت في سورة براءة وهي آخر سورة نزلت: وعوتب عليه السلام على الأخذ يوم بدر بقوله تعالى لَوْلا كِتَابٌ مِنَ اللهِ سَبَقَ الله (الأنفال: ٦٨) الآية، فجلس عليه السلام وأبو بكر يبكيان، وقال عليه السلام: "لو نزل من السماء عذاب لما نجا إلا عمر"، وكان قد أمر بقتلهم دون أخذ المال منهم. (عيني، فتح)
And killing captives: If he wishes - i.e., for those whom we are not prohibited from killing if they do not become Muslim. If they become Muslim, then they are not killed. (Ayni) That is, the Imam has the choice regarding captives: if he wishes, he kills them because the Prophet (peace be upon him) killed Banu Qurayza, and because it cuts off the source of corruption; if he wishes, he enslaves them because it repels their evil while providing great benefit to the Muslims; if he wishes, he leaves them as free dhimmis for the Muslims, and imposes kharaj on them as Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) did in Sawad of Iraq, except for Arab polytheists and apostates as will come. If it is said: He should not have the choice to leave them alive because of the verse 'Kill them wherever you find them' (4:89). We say: This text is specified by those who are dhimmis, musta'mins, and women, so the disputed case is specified by the action of Umar. (Ayni, Fath) 'Free dhimmis for us': meaning that they are dhimmis of the Muslims, except for Arab polytheists and apostates, from whom only Islam or the sword is accepted. (T) And it is prohibited to return them: i.e., it is not permissible for the Imam to return captives to dar al-harb because it strengthens them against the Muslims, as warfare against us. If they become Muslim, he does not kill them because the evil is repelled without killing, but he may enslave them to secure benefit after the cause for ownership has arisen, which is capture and seizure. Contrary to if they become Muslim before capture, where enslavement is not allowed because they become owners of themselves before the cause for ownership is established. (Mustakhlas, Fath) And ransom: i.e., it is also prohibited to ransom a captive with a Muslim captive in their hands (according to us). Abu Hanifa says it is not permissible to release their captives in exchange for Muslim captives in their hands. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: exchanging captives is permissible. As for ransom with money, it is not permissible according to us. Al-Shafi'i says both are permissible (ransom with captives or money) due to the verse 'then either a favor after that or ransom' (47:4). Muhammad said: there is no harm in taking money as ransom if the Muslims have a need, and it is a narration from Abu Hanifa; from Abu Yusuf, it is permissible before division but not after. We say: that was abrogated by the verse of the sword. According to them (the two), the permissibility of exchanging captives is because it rescues the Muslim from the hand of the disbeliever, which is better than killing or enslaving the disbeliever. According to him (Abu Hanifa), ransoming is aiding the enemies of the religion and strengthening them against us, and repelling the evil of war is more important than rescuing a Muslim captive. (Ayni, Fath) And mann (gratuitous release): i.e., it is also prohibited to release captives without compensation, i.e., the Imam sets them free for nothing. Al-Shafi'i says it is permissible, as do Malik and Ahmad, due to the mentioned verse and because the Prophet (peace be upon him) released some captives at Badr and took ransom. We say: all that was abrogated by the verse of the sword because mann and fida' are mentioned in Surah Muhammad, while the verse of the sword was revealed in Surah Bara'ah, which was the last surah revealed. The Prophet (peace be upon him) was reprimanded for taking ransom at Badr in the verse 'If not for a previous decree from Allah...' (8:68). He and Abu Bakr wept, and he said: 'If punishment were to descend from heaven, none would be saved except Umar.' He had ordered them to be killed without taking money. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on the treatment of prisoners of war. The Imam has three options: kill, enslave, or set free as dhimmis. The last option is limited to non-Arab polytheists and non-apostates; Arab polytheists and apostates may only be killed or accept Islam. The text then addresses prohibitions: it is haram to return captives to dar al-harb (enemy territory) because it empowers them. If captives embrace Islam before capture, they cannot be enslaved. Regarding ransom (fida'), there is a split: Abu Hanifa forbids exchanging Muslim captives for enemy captives, while the two disciples permit it. Ransom with money is generally prohibited, though Muhammad allows it if Muslims have need, and Abu Yusuf allows it before division. The Shafi'i school permits both types. The Hanafi position is that the verses permitting mann (gratuitous release) and fida' in Surah Muhammad were abrogated by the 'verse of the sword' in Surah Bara'ah. The Prophet's action at Badr is cited where he initially took ransom but was rebuked by Allah, indicating abrogation. Thus the relied-upon Hanafi position prohibits both mann and fida', except for the permissibility of exchanging captives according to the two disciples, which is a minority view within the school.

Key Terms
الأسرى (al-usara): Captives of war, prisoners taken from the enemy.
الفداء (al-fida'): Ransom: freeing a captive in exchange for something (money or another captive).
المن (al-mann): Gratuitous release of a captive without any compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the ruling on exchanging a Muslim captive for an enemy captive?
A) Permissible according to all Hanafis.
B) Prohibited according to Abu Hanifa but permissible according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
C) Permissible only with money, not captives.
D) Permissible only after division of spoils.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits ransoming or gratuitously releasing captives due to abrogation by the verse of the sword, though intra-Hanafi differences exist regarding exchanging captives.

Treatment of Livestock Left Behind in Enemy Territory
Ruling on livestock left behind in enemy territory

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the treatment of enemy livestock that cannot be transported and explain why hamstringing is prohibited.

Core Idea
If captured livestock cannot be taken, slaughter and burn — but never mutilate.

This brief ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a specific scenario in jihad: when Muslim forces capture enemy livestock but cannot bring them back to Islamic territory. The Hanafi school prohibits hamstringing (cutting the legs) these animals, as it is a form of mutilation (muthla). Instead, the animals must be slaughtered and burned to prevent the enemy from benefiting from them, while avoiding unnecessary cruelty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited act
Recognize that hamstringing (al-'aqr) — cutting the legs of livestock to immobilize them — is explicitly forbidden in this context.
The prohibition stems from the general ban on mutilation (muthla) in war.
2
State the prescribed alternative
When livestock cannot be transported, the command is to slaughter them and then burn the carcasses.
This ensures the enemy cannot recover the animals, while avoiding unnecessary suffering.
3
Understand the legal rationale
The ruling balances military necessity with Islamic ethics: denying enemy resources without resorting to mutilation.
The Prophet ﷺ prohibited mutilation even in warfare (Abu Dawud).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعقر مواش شق إخراجها، فتذبح وتحرق،
And hamstringing livestock whose removal is difficult is prohibited; instead they are slaughtered and burned.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This brief ruling addresses the treatment of enemy livestock that cannot be easily driven back to Muslim territory. According to the Hanafi school, it is forbidden to hamstring (cut the tendons) these animals, as that is a form of mutilation (muthla). Instead, if the animals cannot be taken, they should be slaughtered and their bodies burned. This prevents the enemy from benefiting from them while avoiding unnecessary cruelty. This rule is based on the general prohibition of muthla in war.

Key Terms
العقر (al-'aqr): Hamstringing: cutting the legs of an animal to immobilize it.
المثلة (al-muthla): Mutilation of the body, even of animals or enemies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Leave them behind.
B) Hamstring them so they cannot be used.
C) Slaughter and burn them.
D) Sell them to locals.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, enemy livestock that cannot be transported must be slaughtered and burned, never hamstrung, due to the prohibition of mutilation (muthla).

Rulings on Captives: Prohibition of Ransom and Free Release
States the imam's options for captives and prohibits return, ransom, and free release

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school prohibits ransoming (fida') and free release (man) of captives, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on abrogation.

Core Idea
Once captured, an enemy combatant may be enslaved or freed as a dhimmi — ransom and free release are prohibited by abrogation.

The imam has two permissible options for captives: enslaving them or releasing them as dhimmis. However, three actions are prohibited: returning them to enemy territory, ransoming them, and granting free release. This lesson focuses on the prohibition of ransom (fida') and free release (man), examining the Hanafi proof of abrogation and the opposing views of Imam al-Shafi'i and others.

Guided Path
1
Identify the permissible options and prohibited actions
From the matn, note the imam's two options: istirqaq (enslavement) or leaving free as dhimmis. Then list the three prohibitions: return to dar al-harb, fida', and man.
The commentary expands each prohibition with reasons and rulings.
2
Understand the Hanafi proof for prohibition
The Hanafi school argues that the verses allowing man and fida' in Surah Muhammad (47:4) are abrogated by Ayat al-Sayf (9:5), which commands killing polytheists wherever found. Additionally, the Prophet was criticized for accepting ransom at Badr (Quran 8:68).
Abrogation is a key usul principle: a later verse overrides an earlier one.
3
Compare with the Shafi'i proof
Imam al-Shafi'i relies on the apparent meaning of Quran 47:4, which says 'either grace (man) or ransom (fida')', and the fact that the Prophet himself practiced ransom and free release at Badr.
The Shafi'i position is that the imam may choose among four options, including man and fida'.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and the role of abrogation
The Hanafis respond that 47:4 is abrogated by 9:5, which was revealed later in Surah Bara'ah, the last surah. The Badr incident is cited as a reproach, not a precedent. Thus, the prohibition stands.
The practical difference: Hanafi limits to two options; Shafi'i allows four.

Source Text

من دار الحرب. قطعا كمادة الانتفاع

بعودهم

كان للمسلمين حاجة وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة وعن أبي يوسف يجوز ذلك قبل القسمة لا بعدها. قلنا: نسخ ذلك بآية السيف، ولهما في جواز مفاداة الأسارى بالأسارى: أن في ذلك تخليص المسلم من يد الكافر، وذلك أولى من قتل الكافر أو الانتفاع به بالاسترقاق، وله: أن المفاداة إعانة لأعداء الدين وتقوية لهم حرباً علينا، ودفع شر الحرب أولى من استنفاد الأسير المسلم. (عيني، فتح)
He may enslave, or leave free as dhimmis for us, and prohibited is returning them to dar al-harb, and ransom (fida') and free release (man). ['And their return' meaning returning captives from dar al-harb...]
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on the imam's disposition of captives. The matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq gives the imam two permissible options: enslaving or freeing as dhimmis. It then prohibits three actions: returning captives to enemy territory, ransoming them (fida'), and granting free release (man). The commentary expands each prohibition. For return, the reason is that it strengthens the enemy. For ransom, Abu Hanifa holds it absolutely prohibited; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow exchanging prisoners but not money; Shafi'i allows both. The Hanafi response is abrogation by Ayat al-Sayf. For free release, Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad permit it based on Quran 47:4; Hanafis say it is abrogated. The incident of Badr is cited where the Prophet accepted ransom and was criticized in Quran 8:68. Thus, the relied-upon Hanafi position is that captives may only be enslaved or freed as dhimmis, never returned, ransomed, or released freely.

Key Terms
الفداء (al-fida'): Ransom paid to release a captive, either by exchanging prisoners or paying money
المن (al-man): Free release of a captive without any compensation
الأسارى (al-usara): Captives, prisoners of war
الاسترقاق (al-istirqaq): Enslavement of captives

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on ransoming Muslim captives by exchanging them with captured polytheists?
A) Permissible in all cases
B) Prohibited absolutely
C) Permissible only before division of spoils
D) Permissible only with the consent of all fighters

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits ransom (fida') and free release (man) of captives because these actions strengthen the enemy and are abrogated by the command to fight polytheists universally.

Hamstringing Livestock in Jihad: Hanafi Prohibition and Dispute
Prohibits hamstringing and commands slaughter and burning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hamstringing livestock in jihad, explain the prohibition based on the hadith, and compare it with the Maliki and Shafi'i positions.

Core Idea
Hamstringing is forbidden as mutilation; instead, slaughter then burn to deny the enemy benefit.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a specific military scenario: livestock that cannot be taken out of enemy territory. The Hanafi school prohibits hamstringing (cutting tendons) because it is considered mutilation (muthla), which is strictly forbidden in Islam. Instead, the animals should be slaughtered and then burned to prevent the enemy from using them. This lesson examines the Hanafi position, its evidence from hadith, and the differing views of other schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: hamstringing livestock is haram (prohibited) because it constitutes mutilation. The alternative is to slaughter and then burn the carcasses.
The matn says: 'عقر مواش شق إخراجها، فتذبح وتحرق' – hamstringing is forbidden, slaughter and burn.
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
Imam Malik permits hamstringing to deny benefit to the enemy. His basis is the principle of military necessity (maslaha).
Malik's view is that hamstringing is a legitimate tactic to prevent the enemy from using the animals.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis respond that hamstringing is mutilation (muthla), which is universally prohibited in Islam. The hadith 'لا يعذب بالنار إلا رب النار' is cited to forbid burning alive, but slaughter before burning removes that prohibition.
The hadith is ahad, but its meaning is clear: punishment by fire is reserved for Allah. Slaughtering first makes the burning permissable as a post-mortem act.
4
Note the Shafi'i and Hanbali position
Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad hold that the livestock should be left untouched; they are not hamstrung, slaughtered, or burned.
Their reasoning: the animals are property that may be recovered later; destroying them is unnecessary.
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is the actual battlefield procedure: Hanafi: slaughter and burn; Maliki: hamstring; Shafi'i/Hanbali: leave behind. Each affects enemy benefit and animal welfare.
Mufta bihi in Hanafi school: slaughter and burn, not hamstring.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعقر مواش شق إخراجها، فتذبح وتحرق،
وعقر مواش: أي وحرم أيضاً قطع عراقيب المواشي؛ لأنه مثلة، وقال مالك: تعقر لئلا ينتفع بها. (عيني، فتح، مستخلص) شق إخراجها: جملة وقعت صفة لمواشي، يعني إذا عاد الإمام إلى دار الإسلام ولهم هناك مواش عجزوا عن سوقها معهم وإخراجها عن دار الحرب لا تعقر ولا تترك أيضاً، بل تذبح وتحرق؛ الحيوان لغرض -
أي في دار الحرب قبل القسمة أي اشترك الغنيمة صحيح جائز، وفي ذبحها كسر شوكة الأعداء، وإعدامهم هذه المنفعة المرغوبة فيها، وإنما تحرق بعد الذبح قطعاً لمادة الانتفاع، ولا تحرق ابتداء؛ لقوله علي: "لا يعذب بالنار إلا رب النار"، وما لا يحرق كالأسلحة يدفن في موضع لا يقفون عليه وعند الشافعي: يتركها هناك ولا يذبحها، وبه قال أحمد رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٢/١]
(مستخلص، فتح)
And [prohibited is] hamstringing livestock whose removal is difficult, so they are slaughtered and burned. [Commentary:] Hamstringing livestock means cutting the hamstrings; it is prohibited because it is mutilation. Malik said: they are hamstrung so that the enemy cannot benefit from them. 'Whose removal is difficult' describes livestock: when the imam returns to dar al-Islam and they have livestock there that they cannot drive out, they are not hamstrung nor left behind, but slaughtered and burned...
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on livestock that cannot be taken out of enemy territory. The matn prohibits hamstringing (cutting the tendons of the legs) because it is mutilation (muthla), which is forbidden in Islam. Instead, the animals should be slaughtered and then burned. The commentary explains that slaughtering breaks the enemy's morale and burning prevents any use. Burning is only after slaughter, not before, because the Prophet said: 'None punishes with fire except the Lord of the fire.' Weapons and items that do not burn are buried. Maliki school allows hamstringing to deny benefit, but Hanafis reject it. Shafi'i and Ahmad hold that the livestock should be left untouched.

Key Terms
العقر (al-'aqr): Hamstringing, cutting the tendons of the legs of animals
المثلة (al-muthla): Mutilation of a corpse or living being, prohibited in Islam
الذبح (al-dhabh): Slaughtering animals according to Islamic ritual

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hamstring them to prevent use by the enemy
B) Slaughter them and then burn the carcasses
C) Leave them behind untouched
D) Burn them alive

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school prohibits hamstringing livestock in jihad as mutilation, mandating slaughter and burning instead, based on the hadith forbidding punishment by fire.

Dividing Spoils in Dar al-Harb: Prohibition and Deposition
Prohibits dividing spoils in enemy territory but allows depositing them for transport

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dividing spoils in dar al-harb, explain the underlying usul principle of ownership, and compare it with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Ownership of spoils is not final until they reach dar al-Islam; division before that is invalid.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a key question in the fiqh of jihad: where can the imam divide the spoils? The matn states a prohibition against dividing in enemy territory, but allows depositing them (ida') for transport. This ruling is rooted in a fundamental Hanafi usul principle that ownership of booty (tamlik) is not established until the spoils are brought into dar al-Islam. This leads to a major inter-madhhab disagreement, as well as minor differences within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn first states a prohibition: 'Qismat al-ghanimah fi darihim' – dividing spoils in their land is prohibited. Then it states an exception: 'la al-ida'' – depositing is not prohibited. Identify the hukm level for division: haram according to the Sheikhayn, makruh tanzihi according to Muhammad.
Note the distinction between division (transfer of ownership) and deposit (trust without ownership).
2
Trace the Hanafi proof for prohibition
The Hanafi proof is a hadith: 'The Prophet forbade selling spoils in dar al-harb.' Division is considered a sale in meaning. The underlying principle is that ownership is not established until the spoils are brought to dar al-Islam (ihraz). Therefore, any division that purports to transfer ownership before that point is invalid.
This dalil is ahad, thus zanni al-thubut, leading to the hanafi position that the prohibition is based on this hadith.
3
Identify the Shafi'i counterposition
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that ownership is established upon capture (after defeat of the enemy). Therefore, dividing spoils in dar al-harb is permissible. This is based on a different interpretation of the hadith or other evidence.
Malik and Ahmad agree with Shafi'i, except Malik postpones division of captives.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Sheikhayn) hold it is haram. Muhammad al-Shaybani regards it as makruh tanzihi. The mufta bihi is the view of the Sheikhayn.
Some say if the imam divides based on ijtihad (e.g., due to necessity), it may be permissible by agreement.
5
Understand the exception of ida' (deposit)
The matn says 'la al-ida'' – depositing is not prohibited. This means the imam may distribute the spoils to the fighters as a trust (amanah) to be carried to dar al-Islam, then recollect and properly divide later. This does not transfer ownership, so it avoids the prohibition.
If the fighters refuse to carry the spoils, the imam may hire them with ajr al-mithl, but there is disagreement on compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقسمة الغنيمة في دارهم لا الإيداعُ،
وقسمة الغنيمة في دارهم: أي وحرم أيضاً قسمة الغنيمة في دار الحرب يعني لم يقسم الإمام الغنيمة فيها، وقال الشافعي: لا بأس بقسمتها بعد تمام انهزام المشركين وهذا بناء على أصل وهو أن الملك لا يثبت قبل الإحراز بدار الإسلام عندنا وعنده يثبت ويبتني على هذا، مسائل منها: هذه المسألة، وبقوله قال مالك وأحمد إلا أن مالكاً قال: تؤخر قسمة السبي إلى دار الإسلام، ثم حرمة قسمة الغنيمة في دارهم هي قول الشيخين، وعند محمد: يكره كراهة تنزية، وقيل: جائز بالاتفاق الكراهة؛ لأنه فعل مجتهد فيه، وقد أمضاه.
ووجه
بلا
قيل: إذا قسم عن اجتهاد جاز بالاتفاق، وإن قسم لا عن اجتهاد ولا لحاجة الغزاة، فهي موضع الخلاف، حرمة قسمتها في دارهم نهيه عليها عن بيع الغنائم في دار الحرب والقسمة بيع معنى ولأنها تعتمد الملك ولا ملك قبل الإحراز بدار الإسلام عندنا. (عيني، فتح) لا الإيداع [ أي لا يحرم إيداع الغنيمة عند الغانمين ليحملوها إلى دار الإسلام. (عيني)] أي لا يحرم إيداع الغنيمة؛ لأنه لا يفيد الملك إجماعاً، والمعنى إن لم يجد الإمام ما يحمل عليها الغنائم، فقسمها بينهم قسمة إيداع ليحملوها إلى دار الإسلام، ثم يسترجعها منهم، ويقسمها بينهم رضاهم، جاز له أن يفعل ذلك، فإن أبوا أن يحملوها، أجرهم على ذلك بأجرة المثل في رواية؛ لأنه دفع ضرر عام بضرر خاص، وفي رواية أخرى لا يجبرون؛ لأنه لا يجبر على عقد الإجارة ابتداء. (عيني، فتح)
And dividing spoils in their land [is prohibited], not depositing them. [Commentary:] Dividing spoils in dar al-harb is prohibited, meaning the imam should not divide them there. Al-Shafi'i said: there is no harm in dividing them after the defeat of the polytheists. This is based on a principle: ownership is not established before bringing to dar al-Islam according to us, but according to him it is established. On this depend many issues, including this one. Malik and Ahmad follow his view, except that Malik said postponing division of captives to dar al-Islam. The prohibition of division in dar al-harb is the view of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf); according to Muhammad it is makruh tanzihan. It is said: if he divides based on ijtihad, it is permissible by agreement, etc. Deposition is not prohibited: it is permissible to deposit spoils with the fighters to carry them to dar al-Islam, etc.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage deals with the location of dividing spoils. The matn prohibits dividing spoils in dar al-harb but allows depositing them (i.e., distributing them for safekeeping to transport). The Hanafi position is that ownership of spoils is not established until they are brought into dar al-Islam; therefore, division in enemy territory is invalid. Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that ownership is established upon capture, so division is permissible. Within the Hanafi school, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the Sheikhayn) hold it is haram, while Muhammad regards it as makruh tanzihi. The commentary also discusses the issue of depositing spoils: if the imam cannot find transport, he may distribute the spoils as a deposit (qismat al-ida') to be carried by the fighters, then recollect and properly divide later. If they refuse to carry, they may be hired, but there is a difference of opinion on compulsion.

Key Terms
قسمة الغنيمة (qismat al-ghanimah): Division of spoils among the fighters
الإيداع (al-ida'): Deposit; distribution of spoils as a trust to be carried to dar al-Islam

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it prohibited to divide spoils in dar al-harb?
A) Because the enemy might retake them
B) Because ownership is not established until the spoils are brought to dar al-Islam
C) Because the imam must consult all fighters first
D) Because the spoils must be counted and registered

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi prohibition on dividing spoils in dar al-harb is rooted in the principle that ownership is conditional on conveyance to dar al-Islam, distinguishing it from the permissibility of depositing spoils for transport.
