Prohibition of Selling Spoils Before Division
Prohibits selling spoils before division, citing a hadith and noting disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling spoils before division, cite the hadith proof, and compare it with the position of the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools.

Core Idea
Selling spoils before official division is haram due to explicit hadith; other schools permit based on ownership upon capture.

This lesson examines the ruling on selling spoils of war before the imam divides them among the fighters. The Hanafi school holds it to be haram based on a hadith from the Prophet, while the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools permit it. Understanding the legal reasoning behind each position strengthens your appreciation of how different usul principles lead to differing fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi ruling is that selling any portion of spoils before official division by the imam is haram. This is based on an explicit hadith and the principle that ownership is not fully established until division.
The matn phrase 'wa bay'uha qablaha' indicates prohibition.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their view
The 'three' (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) permit selling spoils before division. Their view is based on the principle that ownership is established upon capture (mallakuhum al-ghanimah bil-isti'la').
The text says 'ind al-thalathah: tuba'.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The opposing schools rely on the general principle that once the spoils are captured, the fighters have a right to them and can dispose of their share before formal division.
This is a qiyas on other forms of acquired property.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is the explicit hadith: 'لا يحل لامرئ يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر أن يبتاع مغنماً حتى يقسم' which directly forbids buying spoils before division. By legal analogy (first indication, not qiyas), selling is also forbidden.
The hadith also extends to garments and riding animals of fay'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in Hanafi law, a fighter cannot sell his share of spoils before the imam's division, while in the other schools he can. This affects transactions and ownership disputes.
Also affects rules of ghanimah and fay' in broader fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيعها قبلها: أي وحرم أيضاً بيعها أي بيع الغنائم قبلها أي قبل القسمة المنهي عنه في الحديث، وعند الثلاثة: تباع، وهذا أيضاً مبني على الأصل الذي مر آنفا، والحجة عليهم ما روي عنه عليه: "لا يحل لامرئ يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر أن يبتاع مغنماً حتى يقسم، ولا أن يلبس ثوباً من فيء المسلمين حتى إذا أخلقه رده فيه، ولا أن يركب دابة من فيء المسلمين حتى إذا أعجفها ردها فيه"، رواه أحمد وأبو داود رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٣/١] (فتح)
And selling them before it: i.e., also prohibited is selling spoils before division, which is prohibited in the hadith. According to the three schools (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad), they are sold. This is also based on the principle mentioned earlier. The proof against them is the hadith: 'It is not lawful for a person who believes in Allah and the Last Day to buy a spoil until it is divided, nor to wear a garment from the fay' of Muslims until when he wears it out he returns it, nor to ride an animal from the fay' of Muslims until when it becomes emaciated he returns it.' Narrated by Ahmad and Abu Dawud.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement prohibiting the sale of spoils before division. The matn states that it is prohibited to sell any part of the spoils before the imam has divided them among the fighters. The commentary clarifies that this prohibition is based on a hadith from the Prophet. The three other schools (Shafi'i, Malik, Hanbali) permit such sale, based on their principle that ownership is established upon capture. However, the Hanafi proof is the explicit hadith forbidding purchase of spoils before division, which is quoted. The hadith also extends to using garments or animals from the fay' before division, requiring their return if worn out.

Key Terms
الغنيمة (al-ghanimah): Spoils of war taken from the enemy
القسمة (al-qismah): Official division of spoils by the imam among the fighters

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Permissible with the imam's permission
B) Prohibited based on a hadith
C) Permissible if the spoils have been captured
D) Makruh but not haram

Key Takeaway
Selling spoils before division is haram in Hanafi law due to explicit hadith, while other schools permit it based on early ownership.

Sharing of Spoils by Reinforcements: Rad' and Madad
Determines that reinforcements who join before or after battle share in spoils based on the principle of crossing the frontier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the sharing of spoils for reinforcements (rad' and madd) and distinguish it from the Shafi'i view by identifying the differing legal causes (sabab).

Core Idea
Reinforcements share in spoils because they crossed the frontier with intent – the cause is intention and entry, not combat.

In the law of jihad, the distribution of spoils (ghanimah) raises critical questions about who is entitled. One key issue is whether reinforcements arriving after the battle share in the spoils. This lesson explores the Hanafi position that such reinforcements are entitled, based on the principle of mujawazah (crossing the frontier), and contrasts it with the Shafi'i view requiring battlefield presence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the matn declares both rad' (auxiliary) and madd (reinforcement) share in the spoils. Note that rad' includes those who help even without fighting.
The Hanafi school broadens entitlement beyond actual combat.
2
Identify the opposing school and its view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that reinforcements who arrive after the battle has concluded do not share in the spoils.
The Shafi'i position is more restrictive, limiting shares to those present at the battle.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof is that the cause (sabab) for entitlement is witnessing the battle (shuhud al-waq'ah). Latecomers lack this cause.
This reflects a textualist approach focusing on the literal occurrence of battle.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that the cause is mujawazat al-darb (crossing the frontier) with the intention of fighting. Both fighters and reinforcements share this cause, so they are equally entitled.
The Hanafi reasoning prioritises the underlying intention and entry into enemy territory over physical combat.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Hanafi law, latecomers still get a share; under Shafi'i they do not.
This difference can significantly affect distribution in extended campaigns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرك الردء والمدد فيها،
وشرك الردء والمدد [ أي الذي لحقهم للإعانة (ط)] مخففاً بمعنى اشترك ومشدداً أي شرك الإمام والردء من أردءت أي أعنت وفلان ردء فلان أي معينه فهو فعل بمعنى مفعول وهو اسم ما يعان به أي الذي أعين به وإن لم يقاتل، والمدد بمعنى فاعل أي الممد والمعاون الذي أعاننا وصار ممداً كالذي، قاتل، فالأول محمول على ما كان معيناً عند المجاوزة، والثاني على من يلحق في دار الحرب أي هما يشتركان في الغنيمة؛ لاستوائهما في السبب الذي هو مجاوزة الدرب الفاصل بين دار الإسلام ودار الحرب على قصد القتال، وقال الشافعي: لا يشاركهم المدد بعد انقضاء القتال، وهذا مبني على أن السبب هو المجاوزة عندنا، وشهود الوقعة عنده. (عيني وفتح)
And sharing of the rad' (auxiliary) and madd (reinforcement) in it. [Commentary:] Rad' and madd: Rad' means one who helps, even if he did not fight; madd means one who comes as reinforcement. Both share in the spoils because they share the cause, which is crossing the frontier between dar al-Islam and dar al-harb with the intention of fighting. Al-Shafi'i said: the reinforcement does not share after the fighting is over. This is based on the principle that according to us, the cause is crossing the frontier, while according to him, it is witnessing the battle.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage addresses whether reinforcements (rad' and madd) who join the army after the battle has started or even after it has ended share in the spoils. The matn establishes that they do share. The commentary explains that the cause for entitlement to spoils is crossing the frontier (mujawazat al-darb) with the intention of fighting, not actual participation in battle. Therefore, both those who assist from the beginning (rad') and those who join later (madd) are entitled to a share. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that reinforcements who join after the battle do not share, because for him the cause is witnessing the battle (shuhud al-waq'ah). This is a fundamental difference in the usul al-fiqh of jihad: Hanafi considers the niyyah and entry into enemy territory as the basis, while Shafi'i requires actual presence at the battle.

Key Terms
الردء (al-rad'): Auxiliary or supporter who helps the army, even without fighting
المدد (al-madad): Reinforcement that joins the army later
المجاوزة (al-mujawazah): Crossing the frontier (the boundary between dar al-Islam and dar al-harb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal cause (sabab) for entitlement to a share in spoils?
A) Actually participating in the battle
B) Crossing the frontier into dar al-harb with the intention of fighting
C) Being appointed by the imam
D) Capturing a prisoner or taking booty

Key Takeaway
Reinforcements who cross into enemy territory with the intention to fight share in the spoils, because the cause is the crossing, not the fighting.

Division of Spoils in Dar al-Harb: Prohibition and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on division of spoils in dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dividing spoils in dar al-harb and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, and apply the principle of ownership via ihraz.

Core Idea
Dividing spoils before bringing them to dar al-Islam is prohibited because ownership is not yet established.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the critical question of when and where spoils of war (ghanima) may be divided. The Hanafi school holds that division must wait until the spoils are physically brought into dar al-Islam, based on the principle that ownership only occurs after 'ihraz' (removal). However, there is an intra-madhhab difference on the severity of the prohibition: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf deem it haram, while Imam Muhammad considers it makruh tanzih. The passage also clarifies that deposit (not division) is permissible by consensus, and explains rules for slaughtering enemy livestock and partnership of reinforcements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and the dispute
Extract from the text that division of spoils in dar al-harb is prohibited (haram) according to the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and makruh tanzih according to Imam Muhammad. Note that deposit is not prohibited.
The text explicitly states 'حرمة قسمة الغنيمة في دارهم هي قول الشيخين'.
2
Understand the usul principle
The prohibition is based on the hadith prohibiting sale of spoils in dar al-harb. Since division is a sale in meaning (بيع معنى), it is analogically prohibited. Moreover, ownership (ملك) is not established until the spoils are removed to dar al-Islam (ihraz).
This is a key Hanafi principle: لا ملك قبل الإحراز.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf treat the prohibition as haram, while Imam Muhammad sees it as makruh tanzih. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the Shaykhayn.
The text notes that if the imam divides based on ijtihad and need, it is permissible by agreement.
4
Apply the principle to related rulings
Deposit (الإيداع) is permissible by consensus because it does not transfer ownership. Partnership of reinforcements (ردء ومدد) in the spoils is valid even if they did not fight, because the cause is crossing into dar al-harb with intention of jihad.
Slaughtering enemy livestock to break morale is allowed, but burning is only after slaughter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الغنائم وقسمتها وقسمة الغنيمة في دارهم لا الإيداعُ، وبيعها قبلها، وشرك الردء والمدد فيها، أي في دار الحرب قبل القسمة أي اشترك الغنيمة صحيح جائز، وفي ذبحها كسر شوكة الأعداء، وإعدامهم هذه المنفعة المرغوبة فيها، وإنما تحرق بعد الذبح قطعاً لمادة الانتفاع، ولا تحرق ابتداء؛ لقوله علي: "لا يعذب بالنار إلا رب النار"، وما لا يحرق كالأسلحة يدفن في موضع لا يقفون عليه وعند الشافعي: يتركها هناك ولا يذبحها، وبه قال أحمد رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٢/١] (مستخلص، فتح) وقسمة الغنيمة في دارهم: أي وحرم أيضاً قسمة الغنيمة في دار الحرب يعني لم يقسم الإمام الغنيمة فيها، وقال الشافعي: لا بأس بقسمتها بعد تمام انهزام المشركين وهذا بناء على أصل وهو أن الملك لا يثبت قبل الإحراز بدار الإسلام عندنا وعنده يثبت ويبتني على هذا ،مسائل منها : هذه المسألة، وبقوله قال مالك وأحمد إلا أن مالكاً قال: تؤخر قسمة السبي إلى دار الإسلام، ثم حرمة قسمة الغنيمة في دارهم هي قول الشيخين، وعند محمد: يكره كراهة تنزية، وقيل: جائز بالاتفاق الكراهة؛ لأنه فعل مجتهد فيه، وقد أمضاه. ووجه بلا قيل: إذا قسم عن اجتهاد جاز بالاتفاق، وإن قسم لا عن اجتهاد ولا لحاجة الغزاة، فهي موضع الخلاف، حرمة قسمتها في دارهم نهيه عليها عن بيع الغنائم في دار الحرب والقسمة بيع معنى ولأنها تعتمد الملك ولا ملك قبل الإحراز بدار الإسلام عندنا. (عيني، فتح) لا الإيداع [ أي لا يحرم إيداع الغنيمة عند الغانمين ليحملوها إلى دار الإسلام. (عيني)] أي لا يحرم إيداع الغنيمة؛ لأنه لا يفيد الملك إجماعاً، والمعنى إن لم يجد الإمام ما يحمل عليها الغنائم، فقسمها بينهم قسمة إيداع ليحملوها إلى دار الإسلام، ثم يسترجعها منهم، ويقسمها بينهم رضاهم، جاز له أن يفعل ذلك، فإن أبوا أن يحملوها، أجرهم على ذلك بأجرة المثل في رواية؛ لأنه دفع ضرر عام بضرر خاص، وفي رواية أخرى لا يجبرون؛ لأنه لا يجبر على عقد الإجارة ابتداء. (عيني ،فتح)
Chapter of Spoils and Their Division. And the division of spoils in their land [is prohibited], but deposit is not [prohibited], and selling them before [division], and the partnership of the reinforcement and the auxiliary force in it. That is, in dar al-harb before division, i.e., partnership in the spoils is valid and permissible. And regarding slaughtering them to break the strength of the enemy and to deprive them of this desired benefit, and they are burned after slaughter to cut off the source of benefit, and they are not burned initially due to his statement: 'None punishes with fire except the Lord of fire.' And what does not burn, like weapons, is buried in a place where they will not find it. And according to al-Shafi'i, he leaves them there and does not slaughter them, and Ahmad said the same. And the division of spoils in their land: that is, it is also prohibited to divide the spoils in dar al-harb, meaning the imam does not divide the spoils there. Al-Shafi'i said: There is no harm in dividing them after the complete defeat of the polytheists. This is based on a principle: that ownership is not established before removal to dar al-Islam according to us, but according to him it is established. Based on this, there are issues, one of which is this. And this is the same view as Malik and Ahmad, except that Malik said: The division of captives is delayed until dar al-Islam. Then, the prohibition of dividing spoils in their land is the view of the two shaykhs. According to Muhammad, it is disliked with a tanzih dislike. And it is said: it is permissible by agreement, and the dislike is because it is an action subject to ijtihad and he has passed it. And there is a face: if he divides based on ijtihad, it is permissible by agreement, and if he divides without ijtihad and without the need of the ghuzat, then it is the place of disagreement. The prohibition of dividing in their land is due to his prohibition of selling spoils in dar al-harb, and division is a sale in meaning, and because it depends on ownership, and there is no ownership before removal to dar al-Islam according to us. (Ayni, Fath) 'Not deposit': that is, it is not prohibited to deposit the spoils with the ghazis to carry them to dar al-Islam. (Ayni) That is, it is not prohibited to deposit the spoils, because it does not effect ownership unanimously. And the meaning is: if the imam does not find something to carry the spoils on, he may divide them among them as a deposit division so that they carry them to dar al-Islam, then take them back from them and divide them among them with their consent; it is permissible for him to do that. If they refuse to carry them, he hires them for that with an equivalent wage in one narration, because it repels a general harm with a specific harm. In another narration, they are not compelled, because one is not compelled to contract a lease initially. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq's commentary addresses the rules for handling spoils (ghanima) while still in dar al-harb. The initial summary lists four rulings: division is prohibited, deposit is not prohibited, selling is prohibited, and partnership of reinforcements is valid. The commentator first explains the ruling on slaughtering livestock to break enemy morale and burning after slaughter, citing the hadith 'None punishes with fire except the Lord of fire.' He notes the Shafi'i position that livestock is left unslaughtered. Then he focuses on division in dar al-harb: according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (Shaykhayn), it is haram for the imam to divide spoils before bringing them to dar al-Islam, because ownership does not occur until then. Imam Muhammad holds it is only makruh tanzih. The proof is the Prophet's prohibition of selling spoils in dar al-harb, and division is considered a sale in meaning. Deposit is permissible by consensus, and the imam may use a deposit division if needed, with options for hiring carriers. The intra-Hanafi dispute on division is clearly presented, with the Shaykhayn's view being the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
غنيمة (ghanima): Spoils of war taken from the enemy by force.
إحراز (ihraz): The act of bringing the spoils into dar al-Islam, which establishes ownership.
ردء (ird'): Reinforcement troops that join the army to assist in battle.
مدد (madad): Auxiliary forces that arrive later as support.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on dividing spoils in dar al-harb according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf?
A) Permissible (ja'iz)
B) Recommended (mustahabb)
C) Prohibited (haram)
D) Disliked (makruh tanzih)

Key Takeaway
The division of spoils in dar al-harb is prohibited (Shaykhayn) or disliked (Muhammad) because ownership requires physical removal to dar al-Islam; deposit is always permissible by consensus.

The Prohibition of Selling Spoils Before Division
Ruling with hadith proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling spoils before division, cite the hadith evidence, and explain why the Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools differ.

Core Idea
No one may sell spoils before division; ownership is not yet established.

In this passage, the commentator presents the Hanafi position that selling ghanima before its division is haram. He contrasts it with the view of the three other major schools and provides the hadith proof. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the principle that ownership of spoils only materializes after they are brought to dar al-Islam and divided.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: selling spoils before division is haram according to the Hanafi school.
The commentator clearly states 'wa harum aydan bay'aha' – and it is also prohibited to sell them.
2
Identify the opposing view
Note that the three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) allow selling before division.
The text says 'inda al-thalathah: tuba' – according to the three, it is sold.
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof
Examine the hadith cited: the Prophet prohibited buying a spoil until it is divided. Since sale is the counterpart of purchase, selling is equally prohibited.
The hadith is from Ahmad and Abu Dawood. Its import is clear and prohibitory.
4
Consider the underlying principle
Understand that the Hanafi position is built on the principle that ownership of spoils does not occur until they are brought to dar al-Islam and formally divided.
The text references 'al-asl alladhi marra anifan' – the principle mentioned earlier.
5
Determine the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis deem such sales invalid and haram; other schools consider them valid and permissible.
This has implications for the buyer and seller: in Hanafi law, the contract is void; in others, it is enforceable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الغنائم وقسمتها وقسمة الغنيمة في دارهم لا الإيداعُ، وبيعها قبلها، وشرك الردء والمدد فيها، وبيعها قبلها: أي وحرم أيضاً بيعها أي بيع الغنائم قبلها أي قبل القسمة المنهي عنه في الحديث، وعند الثلاثة: تباع، وهذا أيضاً مبني على الأصل الذي مر آنفا، والحجة عليهم ما روي عنه عليه: "لا يحل لامرئ يؤمن بالله واليوم الآخر أن يبتاع مغنماً حتى يقسم، ولا أن يلبس ثوباً من فيء المسلمين حتى إذا أخلقه رده فيه، ولا أن يركب دابة من فيء المسلمين حتى إذا أعجفها ردها فيه"، رواه أحمد وأبو داود رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٣/١] (فتح)
Chapter of Spoils and Their Division. And the division of spoils in their land [is prohibited], but deposit is not [prohibited], and selling them before [division], and the partnership of the reinforcement and the auxiliary force in it. And selling them before it: i.e., it is also prohibited to sell them, i.e., selling the spoils before it (before division) which is prohibited in the hadith. According to the three (other schools), it is sold. And this is also based on the principle mentioned earlier. The proof against them is what was narrated from him: 'It is not permissible for a person who believes in Allah and the Last Day to buy a spoil until it is divided, nor to wear a garment from the fay' of the Muslims until he wears it out and returns it, nor to ride an animal from the fay' of the Muslims until it becomes emaciated and returns it.' Narrated by Ahmad and Abu Dawood.

Commentary
This passage specifically addresses the second ruling from the initial summary: the prohibition of selling spoils before they are divided. The commentator states that this is also prohibited (haram) based on an explicit hadith of the Prophet prohibiting such transactions. He notes that the three other imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) permit selling before division, but the Hanafi position relies on the clear text of the hadith and the foundational principle that ownership does not occur until the spoils are brought to dar al-Islam. The quoted hadith provides direct evidence, making this a strong prohibition.

Key Terms
بيع (bay'): Sale; the act of transferring ownership for a price.

Partnership of Reinforcements in Spoils
Ruling on sharing ghanima with reinforcements (ird' and madad)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on sharing spoils with reinforcements and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-evidence.

Core Idea
The Sabab of entitlement to spoils is crossing into dar al-harb with intention, not actual combat.

Reinforcements arriving after a battle may be seen as latecomers, but the Hanafi school grants them a share in the spoils based on a deeper legal cause. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind that ruling and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position, which ties entitlement to witnessing the battle. Understanding this difference clarifies a key principle in the law of ghanima.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school holds that reinforcements (ird' and madad) share in the spoils even if they did not fight.
The source explicitly says 'both share in the spoils because they are equal in the cause, which is crossing the pass...'
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, stating that reinforcements arriving after the fighting ends do not share.
The Shafi'i position is based on a literal reading of a hadith.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the Shafi'i dalil: the hadith 'Al-ghanimah liman shahida al-waq'ah' (spoils are for those who witness the battle).
'Shahida' is interpreted as physical presence at the time of battle.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Examine how the Hanafis reinterpret 'shuhud' as presence in the campaign, not necessarily combat, and emphasize that the cause (sabab) is crossing the border with intention.
The Hanafi argument is based on the broader objective of jihad and early practice.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: reinforcements arriving after the battle get a share in Hanafi but not in Shafi'i law.
This difference matters in military expeditions where reinforcements join later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الغنائم وقسمتها وقسمة الغنيمة في دارهم لا الإيداعُ، وبيعها قبلها، وشرك الردء والمدد فيها، وشرك الردء والمدد [ أي الذي لحقهم للإعانة (ط)] مخففاً بمعنى اشترك ومشدداً أي شرك الإمام والردء من أردءت أي أعنت وفلان ردء فلان أي معينه فهو فعل بمعنى مفعول وهو اسم ما يعان به أي الذي أعين به وإن لم يقاتل، والمدد بمعنى فاعل أي الممد والمعاون الذي أعاننا وصار ممداً كالذي ،قاتل، فالأول محمول على ما كان معيناً عند المجاوزة، والثاني على من يلحق في دار الحرب أي هما يشتركان في الغنيمة؛ لاستوائهما في السبب الذي هو مجاوزة الدرب الفاصل بين دار الإسلام ودار الحرب على قصد القتال، وقال الشافعي: لا يشاركهم المدد بعد انقضاء القتال، وهذا مبني على أن السبب هو المجاوزة عندنا، وشهود الوقعة عنده. (عيني وفتح)
Chapter of Spoils and Their Division. And the division of spoils in their land [is prohibited], but deposit is not [prohibited], and selling them before [division], and the partnership of the reinforcement and the auxiliary force in it. And the partnership of the reinforcement and the auxiliary: i.e., those who join them for assistance. The word 'ird' is with a light shaddah, meaning they become partners, and with a shaddah, meaning the imam shares. The ird' is from 'ardatu' meaning I assisted, and so-and-so is the ird' of so-and-so, i.e., his assistant. It is an infinitive in the sense of a passive participle, and it is the name of that by which one is assisted, i.e., that with which he is assisted, even if he did not fight. And the madad is in the sense of the active participle, i.e., the one who is sent as reinforcement and the helper who helped us and became a reinforcer like the one who fought. So the first (ird') is understood as one who was an assistant at the time of crossing the border, and the second (madad) is the one who joins in dar al-harb. Thus, both share in the spoils because they are equal in the cause, which is crossing the pass separating dar al-Islam and dar al-harb with the intention of fighting. Al-Shafi'i said: The reinforcement does not share with them after the fighting ends. This is based on the fact that the cause is crossing the border according to us, and witnessing the battle according to him.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage explains the third ruling from the initial summary: the partnership of reinforcements (ird' and madad) in the spoils. The commentator clarifies the linguistic meanings and then states that both types of reinforcements share in the ghanima because the reason for entitlement (sabab) is crossing the border from dar al-Islam into dar al-harb with the intention of fighting, not necessarily participation in combat. This is in contrast to Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that only those who actually witness the battle are entitled. The Hanafi position is based on the broader understanding of the cause of entitlement. The hadith 'Al-ghanimah liman shahida al-waq'ah' is interpreted by Shafi'i literally, but the Hanafis argue that the crossing itself is the operative cause, as evidenced by the practice of the Companions who included those who crossed with them.

Key Terms
ردء (ird'): A reinforcement that assists the army at the time of crossing into dar al-harb.
مدد (madad): Auxiliary forces that join later in dar al-harb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Actual fighting in battle
B) Witnessing the battle
C) Crossing the border into dar al-harb with intention to fight
D) Being present at the distribution

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school bases entitlement to spoils on crossing the border with the intention of jihad, not on actual combat, leading to the inclusion of late-arriving reinforcements.

Rulings on the Market-Goer and the Fighter Who Dies Before Spoils Division
States rulings on share entitlement for non-fighters and fighters who die before spoils division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the suqi's entitlement to spoils and on the inheritability of a fighter's share, citing the proofs and the condition of ihraz.

Core Idea
Entitlement to spoils requires both presence and intention to fight; ownership only after securing in Dar al-Islam.

Two related issues arise in the division of spoils: who qualifies as a participant, and when does a deceased fighter's share transfer to heirs. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the market-goer who merely trades and the fighter who fights. It also sets a specific moment for ownership—after the spoils are brought to safety. These rulings are based on the underlying principle that spoils are not fully owned until properly secured.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
In this passage, there are two distinct rulings: first, on the market-goer (suqi) who accompanies the army for trade, and second, on the fighter who dies in Dar al-Harb before the spoils are brought to Dar al-Islam.
Both rulings are connected by the same underlying principle of ownership.
2
Examine the suqi ruling
The hanafi position is that the suqi does not share in spoils unless he actually fights. The opposing Shafi'i view gives him a share because he witnessed the battle.
Identify the proofs: sufi's intention vs. general hadith.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The cause of entitlement is crossing the border with the intention of fighting for Islam. The suqi intends trade, so he does not qualify. The report from Ibn Umar (mawquf) supports this, but the Shafi'is do not accept it as binding.
Note the concept of intention (niyyah) in determining entitlement.
4
Examine the deceased fighter ruling
If a fighter dies in Dar al-Harb after the war but before the spoils are secured (ihraz) in Dar al-Islam, his share is not inherited. If he dies after ihraz, his share is inherited regardless of where he dies.
The moment of ownership (tamlik) is when the spoils are brought to safety.
5
Compare with other schools
Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that even death after the defeat is settled entitles heirs to the share. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf.
Practical consequence: when does a share become part of the estate?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا السوقي [ أي لا يشرك السوقي الذي يخرج مع العسكر للبيع والشراء وكذا حربي ومرتد أسلم ثمه. (ط، عيني) أي لا يشارك السوقي في العسكر مع المقاتل فيها بلا قتال فإن قاتل يشاركهم؛ لأنه بمباشرة القتال ظهر أن قصده القتال والتجارة تبع له وفي قول الشافعي: له سهم؛ لأنهم شهدوا الوقعة، وقال عليه: "الغنيمة لمن شهد الوقعة"؛ لأن الجهاد قد وجد منهم بتكثير السواد، فصاروا كالمقاتلين، ولنا أن . سبب الاستحقاق المجاوزة على قصد ولم يوجد؛ لأنهم قصدوا التجارة، لا إعزاز الدين، وإرهاب العدو، وما رواه موقوف على ابن عمر ما وهو ليس بحجة عند بعض المشايخ خصوصاً على أصل الشافعي، فإن عنده لا يقلد إلا صحابي، أو هو محمول على أنه شهدها على قصد القتال تبيين الحقائق ١٠٣/٤] (مستخلص، فتح) ولا من مات فيها : [ أي في دار الحرب قبل أن تخرج الغنيمة إلى دار الإسلام. (عيني)] أي ولا يشارك المقاتل من الغانمين بعد انقضاء الحرب في دار الحرب حتى لا يرثه ،وارثه وقوله وبعد الإحراز بدارنا يورث نصيبه" أي وإن مات بعد إحراز الغنيمة بدار الإسلام يورث نصيبه سواء مات في دار الحرب أو دار الإسلام، وقال الشافعي: من مات بعد استقرار الهزيمة يورث نصيبه وبه قال مالك ،وأحمد وهذه المسألة أيضاً مبنية على الأصل المذكور. (عيني ، فتح)
The market-goer [i.e., he does not share the market-goer who goes out with the army for buying and selling, and likewise a harbi and an apostate who embraced Islam there (T, Ayni) i.e., the market-goer in the army does not share with the fighters without fighting; if he fights he shares with them, because by directly fighting it appears that his intention is fighting and trade is subsidiary. According to Shafi'i: he has a share because they witnessed the battle, and he (peace be upon him) said: 'Spoils are for whoever witnessed the battle' because jihad was done by them by increasing the numbers, so they become like fighters. Our evidence: the cause of entitlement is crossing the border with intention, and it is not found because they intended trade, not strengthening the religion and intimidating the enemy. And what he narrated is mawquf on Ibn Umar, which is not proof according to some mashayikh, especially according to Shafi'i's principle, for with him he does not follow except a Sahabi, or it is interpreted that he witnessed it with the intention of fighting. (Mukhtalaf, Fath) And not the one who dies in it: [i.e., in Dar al-Harb before the spoils are taken out to Dar al-Islam (Ayni)] i.e., the fighter among the spoils-takers does not participate after the war ends in Dar al-Harb, so that his heir does not inherit him. And his saying: 'and after securing in our dar, his share is inherited' i.e., if he dies after securing the spoils in Dar al-Islam, his share is inherited, whether he died in Dar al-Harb or Dar al-Islam. And Shafi'i said: whoever dies after the defeat is settled, his share is inherited, and Malik and Ahmad said this. And this issue is also based on the mentioned principle. (Ayni, Fath)
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings from the chapter on spoils. First, regarding the market-goer (suqi) who accompanies the army for trade without fighting: he does not share in the spoils unless he actually fights. The Hanafi position is that entitlement to spoils requires crossing into enemy territory with the intention of fighting for Islam. The suqi intends trade, so he does not qualify. The Shafi'i view, citing the hadith 'Spoils are for whoever witnessed the battle,' gives him a share because his presence increases the army's numbers. The Hanafi response is that this hadith is understood as referring to those who witnessed with the intention of fighting, and the report from Ibn Umar is mawquf (stopped at the Companion) and thus not a conclusive proof, especially for Shafi'is who only follow marfu hadith. Second, concerning a fighter who dies in Dar al-Harb after the war but before the spoils are brought to Dar al-Islam: his share is not inherited. But if he dies after the spoils are secured in Dar al-Islam, his share is inherited regardless of where he dies. Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that even death after the defeat is settled entitles his heirs to his share. The difference in both rulings stems from the same principle: for Hanafis, ownership of spoils is only fully established after they are brought to Dar al-Islam and divided. The passage is expanded from the matn with commentary from Ayni and Fath.

Key Terms
السوقي (al-sūqī): The market-goer who accompanies the army for trade and commerce, not for fighting.
الغنيمة (al-ghanīmah): Spoils taken from the enemy by force.
الإحراز (al-iḥrāz): Bringing the spoils into a secure place in Dar al-Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does a fighter's share in spoils become inheritable?
A) Immediately after the battle ends
B) After the spoils are brought to Dar al-Islam
C) After the death of the fighter
D) After the defeat of the enemy is settled

Key Takeaway
Entitlement to spoils depends on intention to fight (niyyah) and actual possession (ihraz); these are the Hanafi criteria for ownership.

Permissibility of Using Spoils in Dar al-Harb Before Division
Explains permissibility conditions and scope of using spoils before division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using spoils before division, identify the conditions for permissibility, distinguish permitted vs. prohibited items, and explain the cross-madhhab differences.

Core Idea
Using spoils before division is permissible out of necessity; the ruling ceases upon leaving Dar al-Harb.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on using spoils (ghanimah) while the army is still in Dar al-Harb before official distribution. Drawing on the hadith of Ibn Umar and the Prophet's directive at Khaybar, the school permits soldiers to consume certain items for immediate need. We will explore the scope, conditions, limitations, and how this ruling compares with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? Using spoils before division. According to Hanafis, it is mubah (permissible) when there is genuine need, as long as the Imam has not forbidden it.
The permissibility is a rukhsah (dispensation), not a general license.
2
Locate the dalil
Two primary proofs: (1) The practice of the Companions as narrated by Ibn Umar (Bukhari): 'We would find honey and grapes in our expeditions and eat them without carrying them away.' (2) The Prophet's instruction regarding the food of Khaybar: 'Eat it and feed it, and do not carry it away.'
These proofs establish the principle that immediate consumption is allowed, but hoarding or trade is not.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The Hanafi principle dawarān al-ḥukm ʻalā dalīl al-ḥājah (the ruling revolves around evidence of need) is applied. The need is being in Dar al-Harb with limited supplies. The proofs indicate permissibility only to the extent of need, and the permission ends when need ends.
Thus, after leaving Dar al-Harb, use is no longer allowed.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools restrict permissible use to food only; Malik restricts to food for essential nutrition. Hanafis permit a broader range: fodder, food, firewood, weapons, oil, but not non-consumables like wood for utensils or clothing.
The Hanafi position relies on a wider interpretation of 'need' and the proofs.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and consequences
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi within the school. Practical consequence: a soldier may use a captured horse for riding or weapons for fighting but must return them after use; leftover fodder must be returned to the spoils pool.
Failure to return or selling before division requires compensation to the spoils fund.

And they benefit in it with fodder etc.: i.e., there is no harm for the army to feed fodder in Dar al-Harb, even with wheat when there is no barley, and they eat whatever food they find, whether prepared for eating or not, to the extent that it is permissible to slaughter livestock like cows and similar, and they return their hides to the spoils. Likewise grains, sugar, fresh fruits, and everything that is eaten customarily. Likewise using firewood for burning, and what is prepared for making bowls and cups is not permitted to be used. Likewise, benefiting from weapons and oil is permissible, due to what was narrated from Ibn Umar that he said: 'We would find honey and grapes in our military expeditions and eat them and not take them up.' Bukhari narrated it. And this is evidence that their custom was to benefit from what they needed, and the use in a narration is conditioned by need, because these things are common property, so benefiting is not permitted except out of need. The reason for permission is his (peace be upon him) statement regarding the food of Khaybar: 'Eat it and feed it, and do not carry it away'; and because the ruling revolves around evidence of need. According to Shafi'i and Ahmad in one opinion, it is not permissible to oil oneself nor treat with medicines. According to Malik, one does not benefit in other than food. Our unrestricted permission applies to whoever has a share in the spoils or receives a gratuity, whether rich or poor, and he feeds those with him of children, women, and slaves, likewise the reinforcements, because he has a share in it. He does not feed the hired worker or the merchant unless it is wheat bread or cooked meat, then there is no harm then; because he owns it by consumption. And what is not customarily eaten... [PAGE_166] ...and oil without division nor sell them, and after leaving it no, and whatever remains is returned to the spoils. And whoever among them embraces Islam secures himself. It is not permissible for him to take things like medicines, perfume, violet oil, and the like. And his saying: 'without division' means these things are benefited from without division among them. And it is disliked to benefit from clothes and goods, unless they need them: then the Imam divides among them in Dar al-Harb, because the prohibited is permitted for necessity, so the disliked is more appropriate. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni) He does not sell, and oil with an opening of the dal, i.e., oil for their bodies or the hooves of their animals for turning (Ayni). 'Without division' is attached to 'they benefit', i.e., they benefit from these things without division among them, and this is when the Imam does not forbid them; if he forbids them, it is not permissible for them to do so. (Ayni) And he does not sell them: i.e., these mentioned things which it is permissible to benefit from before division, because he has no ownership; rather, taking is permitted out of necessity. If someone sells before division, the price is returned to the spoils, because it is a substitute for a specific property that belonged to the group. And his saying: 'And after leaving it no', i.e., it is not permissible to benefit from these things after leaving Dar al-Harb, because the permissibility (i.e., necessity) is removed, and because they have become the property of the spoils-takers, and it is not permissible to take joint property except with the permission of the other partner. And because the rule revolves around evidence of need, which is being in Dar al-Harb; when the evidence of need disappears, the need is removed. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni) From: no: i.e., they do not benefit from the mentioned things due to the removal of necessity (Ayni). And what remains: i.e., whatever remains in his hand of fodder and similar permissible things (Ayni). He returns: after exiting to Dar al-Islam due to the removal of their need (Ayni). To the spoils: because it is the right of the spoils-takers, so benefiting from it does not remain. This is before division. As for after division, if he was rich and the item still exists, he gives it in charity or its value if it was destroyed; if he was poor, he benefits from it and nothing is due from him if it perishes, because when return becomes difficult, it falls under the ruling of luqatah. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/424) (Fath)
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of using spoils while still in Dar al-Harb before official division. The Hanafi school permits soldiers to use certain items out of necessity: fodder for animals, food for personal consumption (even cooking meat), firewood for burning, weapons, and oil for application. This permission is based on the practice of the Companions as narrated by Ibn Umar and the Prophet's instruction regarding Khaybar. The use is conditioned by need and is a dispensation (rukhsah). Items not for immediate consumption, like wood for utensils, are not allowed. Shafi'i and Ahmad restrict permission further, allowing only food; Malik limits it to food for essential nutrition. Hanafis extend permission to anyone who has a share in the spoils, including the poor and rich, and those with them. After leaving Dar al-Harb, the permission ceases; remaining items must be returned to the spoils. If sold before division, the sale price is returned to the spoils. Selling is prohibited. After division, if items remain with a wealthy person, charity or value payment is required; a poor person may keep them due to hardship of return, akin to luqatah (found property).

Key Terms
الغنيمة (al-ghanīmah): Spoils taken from the enemy; before division, it is common property of the Muslims.
الضرورة (al-ḍarūrah): Necessity that permits the use of common property before division.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what must be done with leftover fodder after the army leaves Dar al-Harb?
A) It can be kept by the soldier
B) It is returned to the spoils (ghanimah)
C) It is destroyed
D) It is given to the poor

Key Takeaway
In Dar al-Harb, soldiers may use spoils out of necessity, but leftover items must be returned and the permission ceases upon departure.

What a Convert in Dar al-Harb Secures and What Becomes Fay'
Ruling on what a resident of dar al-harb secures by converting to Islam while in dar al-harb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which assets a convert in dar al-harb secures and which become fay', and explain the Hanafi reasoning behind the fetus dispute.

Core Idea
A convert secures his person, young children, and moveable property, but his land, fighting slaves, and his wife's unborn child are fay'.

When a resident of dar al-harb embraces Islam while still in enemy territory, he gains protection for himself and certain possessions. However, not everything he owns is automatically secured—some assets become booty (fay') for the Muslim state. This lesson examines the precise scope of what is secured and what is not, focusing on the Hanafi position and a key dispute over the fetus of his wife.

Guided Path
1
Identify secured assets
From the text, list what a convert secures: his own person (cannot be killed or enslaved), his young children (sighar), every property in his actual possession, and deposits with Muslims or dhimmis. Understand the evidence: the hadith 'Whoever accepts Islam while in possession of property, it is his' and the principle of prior possession.
Note that this only applies if he converts before being captured; if after, he is already a slave.
2
Identify unsecured assets
The text specifies exceptions: adult child (not a follower in Islam), wife (she remains a harbiya and is enslaved), her fetus (follows her as part of her), real estate (not considered in his actual possession because it is rooted in dar al-harb), and a fighting slave (who has left his master's control by rebelling).
Real estate and fighting slave have intra-Hanafi differences but the mufta bihi is that they are fay'.
3
Analyze the fetus dispute
The Hanafi position: the fetus is part of the mother (juz' minha) and follows her into slavery; it is fay'. The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali position: the fetus is a separate entity that becomes Muslim by following the father, like a born child, so it cannot be enslaved. The Hanafi response: following in this context is like 'remaining' (baga') in slavery, not initiation; Islam does not prevent remaining in slavery, unlike the initiated enslavement of a free person. The fetus is not yet a separate person.
4
Understand the underlying principles
The key Hanafi principle is that Islam prevents initiation of enslavement (ibtida' al-istirqaq) but does not remove existing enslavement (baga'). The fetus is still part of the mother and thus follows her legal status. Taba'iyyah (following) operates differently for born children (who follow the father) and unborn children (who follow the mother). Also, real estate is not considered in the convert's 'hand' because it is part of the enemy's territory.
This shows the Hanafi emphasis on factual possession (yad haqiqiyyah) and the distinction between movable and immovable property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أسلم منهم من أهل دار الحرب في دار الحرب. (عيني) أحرز نفسه أي إن ذهبنا إلى دار الحرب، فمن أسلم منهم فيها، أحرز بإسلامه نفسه؛ لأن الإسلام ينافي ابتداء الاسترقاق، وأحرز أيضاً أولاده الصغار؛ لأنهم المسلمون بإسلامه تبعاً، فلا يجوز قتلهم، ولا استرقاقهم، وأحرز أيضاً كل مال في يده؛ لقوله عليه: "من أسلم على مال فهو له"، ولأنه سبقت يده الحقيقية من يد الظاهرين عليه، وكذلك أحرز كل مال كان له وديعة عند مسلم أو ذمي؛ لأنه في يد صحيحة محترمة، ولأنه في يده المودع كيده؛ لأنه عامل له في الحفظ، وهذا إذا أسلم قبل أن يأخذه المسلمون، وإن أسلم بعده، فهو عبد؛ لأنه أسلم بعد انعقاد الملك.

[رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٥/١] (فتح)
One from them of the people of dar al-harb accepts Islam in dar al-harb. (Ayni) 'He secures himself' i.e., if we go to dar al-harb, and one of them accepts Islam there, he secures his own person by his Islam because Islam prevents the initiation of enslavement; and he also secures his young children because they are Muslims by following his Islam, so it is not permissible to kill them or enslave them; and he also secures every property in his hand due to his saying: 'Whoever accepts Islam while in possession of property, it is his', and because his actual prior possession takes precedence over the possession of those who have apparent possession over him; and likewise he secures every property that he had as a deposit with a Muslim or dhimmi because it is in a sound and inviolable hand, and because the depositary's hand is like his own hand since he acts as his agent in preservation. This is if he accepts Islam before the Muslims take him; if he accepts Islam afterwards, he becomes a slave because he accepted Islam after the ownership (of the Muslims) was established. And there are four issues here: First: the harbi accepts Islam in his abode and does not come out to us until we overcome them, so the ruling is as the author mentioned. Then we overcome the abode, so all his property there is fay' except his young children due to their following him in Islam, and except second: what he deposited with a Muslim or dhimmi comes out to us due to the soundness of their hand. Third: a musta'min accepts Islam in our abode, then we overcome his abode, so all that he left behind, even his young children, is fay' due to the severance of protection and the lack of their following him in Islam due to the difference of the two abodes. Fourth: a Muslim enters their abode - or a trader or dhimmi with aman, and buys from them properties and children, then we overcome the abode, so all is his except houses and lands, for they are fay'. (Mukhtasar, Fath, Ayni) 'And his child' i.e., his young children due to the existence of the protector which is Islam. (Ayni) 'And every property with him' i.e., likewise he secures every property due to the existence of the protector. (Ayni) 'Except his adult child' i.e., one who accepts Islam from the harbis in dar al-harb does not secure his adult children; rather they belong to the gains because they are kafir harbis and are not Muslims by following his Islam. Likewise he does not secure his wife because she is a kafira harbiya not following him in Islam, so she is enslaved; likewise he does not secure her fetus because it is a part of her. According to al-Shafi'i: the fetus is not fay' because it is a Muslim by following its father like a separated child, and Malik and Ahmad said the same. We said: a Muslim can be enslaved by following like the child of a slave woman from other than her master; so in the case of following, this is like remaining (in slavery), and Islam does not contradict remaining in slavery, unlike the separated child due to lack of partiality. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/425] (Mukhtasar) 'And his wife' i.e., he does not secure her because she is a harbiya not following him, so she is enslaved. (Ayni) 'And her fetus' i.e., he does not secure the fetus also because it is part of her, so it follows her in slavery. (Ayni) 'And his real estate' [also what is in it of uncut crops (T Ayni)] i.e., except his real estate, meaning he does not secure his real estate, so it becomes fay'. Al-Shafi'i said: it is his because it is in his hand like movables, so it is not fay'. This is a narration from Muhammad, and from Abu Yusuf there are two narrations. Abu Hanifa's view: real estate is in the hands of the people of the abode and their authority, since it is part of dar al-harb, so he does not have actual possession over it. This is based on the principle that possession is not established over real estate in reality according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), while according to Muhammad it is established. Also what is in it of uncut crops is fay' because it is in the hands of the people of the abode. (Ayni, Fath) 'And his fighting slave' [i.e., except his slave and his female slave who fights and her fetus (T)] i.e., he also does not secure his fighting slave because when he rebels against his master, he left his hand; and he restricted it to 'fighting' because if he does not fight, he belongs to the master. And what is usurped or deposited with a harbi becomes fay'; likewise if it is usurped in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi according to Abu Hanifa. Muhammad said: it is not fay'. Abu Yusuf is with him in one narration and with Abu Hanifa in another. Likewise his female slave who fights and her fetus because it is part of the mother. Regarding the fighting slave, there is a difference among the three imams, but the apparent view is with them because he has not ceased to be property. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/425] (Fath)
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This is a significant passage in Hanafi siyar dealing with the conversion of a harbi (enemy combatant) within dar al-harb. The matn states that such a person 'secures himself' (ahraza nafsahu) by embracing Islam. The commentary explains that this means he cannot be enslaved or killed; his young children (sighar) follow him and are also protected as Muslims. His property in his actual possession is also secured, based on the hadith 'Whoever accepts Islam while in possession of property, it is his.' Similarly, property deposited with a Muslim or dhimmi is secured because the depositary's hand is like his own. However, this applies only if he accepts Islam before the Muslims capture him; if after, he is already a slave. The text then lists four specific scenarios (arba'u masail) clarifying borderline cases: (1) He converts in his home but does not come out; when Muslims conquer, his property is fay' (booty) except his young children. (2) Deposited property brought out by Muslims or dhimmis is secure. (3) A musta'min (one given safe conduct) who converts in dar al-islam then his former abode is conquered: all his property including young children is fay' due to change of abode. (4) A Muslim trader buys property and children from dar al-harb with aman; when conquered, movables are his but land is fay'. The passage then details exceptions: an adult child, wife, her fetus, real estate, and a fighting slave are not secured. The adult child and wife are not followers in Islam. The fetus is considered part of the mother (Hanafi view), and thus follows her into slavery—contra Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad who consider it a separate Muslim entity. Real estate (fixed property) is not deemed to be in the convert's actual possession because it is part of the enemy's territory; here there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold possession over real estate is not established, while Muhammad holds it is. The fighting slave (abd muqatil) is not secured because by fighting he has left his master's control; non-fighting slaves remain. The discussion ends with a difference among the three imams (i.e., Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) regarding the fighting slave, but the preferred view is that he remains property (fay').

Key Terms
أحرز نفسه (ahraza nafsahu): Secures his own person from being killed or enslaved by virtue of embracing Islam.
فيء (fay'): Booty acquired from the enemy without fighting or through conquest; here, property that becomes part of the Muslim state treasury.
عبد مقاتل (abd muqatil): A slave who fights alongside his master or independently; such a slave is considered to have left his master's control.
تبعاً (taba'an): By way of following; the legal status of children or dependents being derived from the head of household.
بقاء (baqa'): Remaining; the state of continuing in an existing condition, such as slavery, as opposed to initiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the fetus of a convert's wife in dar al-harb considered fay'?
A) Because the fetus is not a human being
B) Because the fetus is part of the mother and follows her in slavery
C) Because the father's Islam does not affect the fetus
D) Because the fetus is considered property of the state

Key Takeaway
A convert in dar al-harb secures himself, his young children, and moveable property, but his adult relatives, real estate, fighting slaves, and his wife's fetus become fay' (booty).

What Is Not Secured for a New Muslim in Dar al-Harb: Hanafi Positions and Disputes
Rulings on exemptions from ghanimah for a new Muslim in dar al-harb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for each category of person and property when a harbi converts to Islam in dar al-harb, and explain the inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi disputes on pregnancy, real estate, and the fighting slave.

Core Idea
Islam protects the convert's minor child and movable property, but not adult children, wife, unborn child, real estate, or a rebellious slave.

When a person in enemy territory accepts Islam, what happens to their family and belongings? This passage from the Hanafi fiqh tradition, as commented by 'Ayni and others, lays out the rules and the reasoning behind them, along with disagreements from Imam al-Shafi'i and within the Hanafi school itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the general principle
A harbi (enemy combatant) converts to Islam while still in dar al-harb. The general principle is that Islam acts as a protector ('āṣim) for him, securing his person and property that is in his hand or dependent on him.
The 'āṣim principle is key: it protects minors and movable property but does not extend to independent adults or property under enemy authority.
2
List what is secured and why
The minor child is secured because he is a dependent and Islam protects him. All movable property in the convert's hand is secured, including property held as a trust (wadi'ah) with a Muslim or dhimmi.
Trust property is considered in the owner's hand via the depositary's hand (يد المودع يد المالك).
3
List what is not secured and the basis
Five categories are not secured: adult child (independent harbi), wife (not a follower in Islam), pregnancy (part of the mother), real estate (under authority of dar al-harb), and a fighting slave (rebellious against master).
Each has a specific reason: independence, non-dependence, part-whole relationship, physical location, or rebellion.
4
Examine the dispute on pregnancy
Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik, Ahmad) argue the unborn child is free because it becomes Muslim by the father's conversion, like a separated child. The Hanafi response is that the pregnancy is a part of the mother, so it follows her status; Islam does not negate existing slavery, as seen with the child of a slave-girl.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on whether the fetus is an independent entity or a part of the mother.
5
Examine the disputes on real estate and fighting slave
Real estate: Abu Hanifa says it is fay' because it is not in the convert's hand (still under dar al-harb authority); Muhammad holds it can be secured by hand; Abu Yusuf has two narrations. Fighting slave: if he fights, he leaves the master's hand and becomes fay'; if not, he remains with the master. Intra-Hanafi differences exist on whether usurped or trust property becomes fay'.
The text notes 'al-zahir ma'ahum' (the apparent view is with the opposing imams) for the fighting slave, but the Hanafi position is defended.

Source Text

باب الغنائم وقسمتها
وطفله وكلّ مال معه، أو كان وديعة عند مسلم أو ذمي، دون ولده الكبير أي ماله وزوجته وحملها وعقاره وعبده المقاتل. معه هم: لأن يد المودع يد المالك = تاجر أو ذمي بأمان، واشترى منهم أموالاً وأولاداً، ثم ظهرنا على الدار، فالكل له إلا الدور والأرضين، فإنهما فيء. (عيني) دون ولده الكبير أي لا يحرز من أسلم من الحربيين في دار الحرب أولاده الكبار بل للغانمين؛ لأنهم كفار حربيون وليسوا مسلمين بإسلامه تبعاً وكذلك لا يحرز زوجته؛ لأنها كافرة حربية لا تابعة له في الإسلام فتسترق، وكذا لا يحرز حملها؛ لأنه جزء منها، وعند الشافعي: لا يكون الحمل فيئاً؛ لأنه مسلم تبعاً لأبيه كالولد المنفصل، وبه قال مالك وأحمد. قلنا: المسلم يسترق تبعاً كولد الجارية من فكان هذا في حق التبع بمنزلة البقاء، والإسلام لا ينافي بقاء الرق، بخلاف المنفصل؛ لعدم الجزئية. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٥/١] (مستخلص) وزوجته أي لا يحرزها؛ لأنها حربية غير تابعة له فتسترق. (عيني) وحملها: أي لا يحرز الحمل أيضاً؛ لأنه جزء منها فيتبعها في الرق. (عيني) غير مولاها، وعقاره: [وكذا ما فيه من زرع لم يحصد (ط عيني)] أي ودون عقاره يعني لا يحرز عقاره فيكون فيئاً، وقال الشافعي: هو له؛ لأنه في يده كالمنقول فلا يكون فيئاً، وهو رواية عن محمد، وعن أبي يوسف روايتان، ولأبي حنيفة: أن العقار في يد أهل الدار وسلطانهم؛ إذ هو من جملة دار الحرب فلم يكن في يده حقيقة، وهذا مبني على أن اليد لا تثبت على العقار حقيقة عند الشيخين، وعند محمد تثبت، وكذا ما فيه من زرع لم يحصد يكون فيئاً؛ لأنه في يد أهل الدار. (عيني،فتح) وعبده المقاتل: [ أي ودون عبده وأمته المقاتلة وحملها . (ط)] أي ولا يحرز أيضاً عبده المقاتل؛ لأنه لما تمرد على مولاه خرج من يده، وقيد بالمقاتل؛ لأنه لو لم يقاتل يكون للمولى، وما كان غصباً يد حربي أو وديعة فيء، وكذا إذا كان في يد مسلم أو ذمي غصباً عند أبي حنيفة، وقال محمد: لا يكون فيئاً، وأبو يوسف معه في رواية، ومع أبي حنيفة في أخرى، وكذا أمته المقاتلة وحملها؛ لأنه جزء الأم، وفي العبد المقاتل خلاف الأئمة الثلاثة، والظاهر معهم؛ لأنه لم يخرج عن كونه مالاً. [رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٥/١] (فتح)
Chapter of Spoils and Their Division. And his minor child and all property with him, or if it was a trust with a Muslim or a dhimmi, but not his adult child (i.e., his property), his wife, her pregnancy, his real estate, and his fighting slave. With him they are: because the hand of the depositary is the hand of the owner. A merchant or dhimmi with aman, they bought from them goods and children, then we overcame the abode, then everything is for him except houses and lands, for they are fay'. (Mukhtalif, Fath 'Ayni) 'And his child' means his minor children due to the presence of the protector which is Islam. ('Ayni) 'And all property with him' means likewise every property is secured due to the presence of the protector. ('Ayni) 'Except his adult child' means that whoever among the harbis converts to Islam in dar al-harb does not secure his adult children, rather they are for the combatants; because they are kafir harbis and are not Muslims by his conversion as dependents. Likewise he does not secure his wife; because she is a kafira harbiyya not subordinate to him in Islam, so she is enslaved. Similarly he does not secure her pregnancy; because it is a part of her. According to al-Shafi'i: the pregnancy is not spoils; because it is Muslim as dependent of the father like the separated child, and Malik and Ahmad said the same. We said: a Muslim is enslaved as a dependent like the child of a slave-girl, so this with respect to being a dependent is like remaining, and Islam does not negate remaining in slavery, unlike the separated child due to the lack of being a part. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/425) (Mukhtasar) 'And his wife' means he does not secure her; because she is harbiyya not subordinate to him, so she is enslaved. ('Ayni) 'And her pregnancy' means he does not secure the pregnancy either; because it is part of her so it follows her in slavery. ('Ayni) Except her owner. 'And his real estate' [And likewise crops not yet harvested (T 'Ayni)] means and except his real estate, meaning he does not secure his real estate, so it becomes fay'. Al-Shafi'i said: it is for him; because it is in his hand like movable property so it is not fay', and it is a narration from Muhammad, and from Abu Yusuf two narrations. Abu Hanifa's view: real estate is in the hand and authority of the people of the abode; since it is part of dar al-harb so it is not truly in his hand. This is based on that a hand over real estate is not established truly according to the two shaykhs, while according to Muhammad it is established. Also crops not yet harvested are fay' because they are in the hand of the people of the abode. ('Ayni, Fath) 'And his fighting slave' [And except his male and female fighting slave and her pregnancy (T)] means he also does not secure his fighting slave; because when he rebels against his master, he leaves his hand. It is restricted to the fighting one, because if he does not fight, it is for the master. What is usurped in the hand of a harbi or a trust is fay', likewise if it is in the hand of a Muslim or dhimmi as a usurpation according to Abu Hanifa. Muhammad said: it is not fay'. Abu Yusuf is with him in one narration and with Abu Hanifa in another. Similarly his female fighting slave and her pregnancy; because it is part of the mother. Regarding the fighting slave, there is disagreement among the three imams, and the apparent is with them because he has not left being property. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/425) (Fath)
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of what property and family members are secured (i.e., not taken as ghanimah) when a harbi converts to Islam in dar al-harb. The commentator explains that the minor child is secured because Islam is a protector ('asim), and similarly all movable property is secured. However, the adult child is not secured because he is an independent harbi. The wife is not secured because she is not a follower in Islam; she is enslaved. The pregnancy is treated as part of the mother, so it follows her ruling. The commentator then presents al-Shafi'i's objection that the pregnancy should be Muslim like a separated child, and the Hanafi response: the pregnancy is like the child of a slave-girl who can be enslaved while Muslim; it is like remaining in slavery, not a new acquisition. For real estate, Abu Hanifa holds it is fay' because it remains under the authority of dar al-harb, unlike movable property. There is intra-Hanafi difference: Muhammad holds that real estate can be secured by hand, but Abu Yusuf has two narrations. The fighting slave is not secured because he has rebelled; if he does not fight, he remains with his master. There is further disagreement among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on whether a usurped or deposited item in the hand of a Muslim is fay'. The passage concludes that the apparent view (al-zahir) supports the three imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) in that the fighting slave is still property, so he should be secured if not fighting? Actually the text says 'the apparent is with them' meaning the opposing imams, but the commentary elaborates that the slave did not leave being property, so perhaps the Hanafi view is qualified.

Key Terms
عاصم (‘āṣim): Protector; here it refers to Islam as a protective factor that prevents the enslavement or confiscation of property.
مقاتل (muqātil): Fighting; refers to a slave who actively fights against the Muslims, which leads to his being considered as having left his master's hand.
وديعة (wadī‘ah): Trust; property left for safekeeping with a Muslim or dhimmi. The ruling depends on whether it can be considered in the owner's hand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the pregnancy of a woman whose husband converts to Islam in dar al-harb considered fay'?
A) Because the pregnancy is not yet born
B) Because it is part of the mother and follows her ruling
C) Because the father's Islam does not affect the unborn child
D) Because the mother is a kafira

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between dependents and independent harbis, and between movable property and real estate, in determining what is secured for a new Muslim in dar al-harb.

Cavalry Shares: One Horse, Two Shares
Intra-Hanafi dispute on cavalry shares with multiple horses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi shares for foot soldiers and cavalrymen, distinguish Abu Hanifa's position from the Sahibayn, and apply the tarjih to determine the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A cavalryman gets two shares total, regardless of how many horses he fights with.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the standard division of spoils (ghanimah) between infantry and cavalry. While the basic shares are agreed — one for a foot soldier, two for a cavalryman — a key intra-Hanafi dispute arises when a cavalryman has more than one horse. Abu Hanifa limits him to two shares; the Sahibayn argue for three. We will examine the proofs and resolve which position is relied upon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base shares
The foot soldier (rajil) gets one share by consensus. The cavalryman (faris) gets two shares, provided his horse is fit for combat.
Consensus means all schools agree on the base shares.
2
Understand the dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that a cavalryman with two or more horses still gets only two shares total. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and the three other imams (Shafi'i, Malik, Ahmad) say three shares.
Abu Yusuf also had an independent view allowing four shares for two horses, but that is not relied upon.
3
Analyze the proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: Hadith of Mujammi' ibn Jariya that the Prophet gave a cavalryman two shares at Khaibar, and the rational argument that a warrior cannot fight on two horses at once. The Sahibayn's proof: Hadith of Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet gave three shares to a cavalryman.
The hadith of Ibn 'Umar is narrated by Ahmad and Abu Dawud.
4
Apply the tarjih
The commentator interprets Ibn 'Umar's hadith as tanfil (an extra grant), not the standard share. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's position: two shares total.
Tanfil is a discretionary extra grant by the imam, not part of the standard division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل
أي في بيان كيفية القسمة
للراجل سهم وللفارس سهمان ولو له فرسان والبراذين كالعتاق
جمع برذون وهو فرس العجم
فصل: زيد في بعض النسخ "في كيفية القسمة" في المتن، وفي بعضها اكتفى على "فصل" فقط، وأفرد بيان القسمة بفصل؛ لكثرة شعبها، والقسمة: جعل النصيب الشائع معيناً وهو الصحيح، وكيفية القسمة أن يقسم الإمام الغنيمة، ويفرز خمس المال أولا، ويقسم أربعة أخماسه بين الغانمين بالتفصيل الذي ذكره المصنف. (محشي، فتح) للراجل سهم: [ أي يقسم الإمام الغنيمة فيفرز خمسها أولاً ويقسم الباقي بين الغانمين بأن يكون للراجل سهم بالإجماع. (ط، عيني)] الراجل خلاف الفارس، ويجمع على رجل كصاحب وصحب أي الماشي على أقدام، فله سهم بالإجماع (عيني،فتح، مستخلص)
وللفارس سهمان [إن كان فرسه صحيحاً كبيراً صالحاً للقتال. (ط)] ولا يسهم لفرس مشترك للقتال عليه، إلا إذا استأجر أحد الشريكين حصة الآخر قبل الدخول، فالسهم للمستأجر، وعم إطلاقه ما لو كان الفرس مغصوباً أو مستعاراً أو مستأجراً، ويشترط أن يكون الفرس صالحاً للقتال، فلو صغيراً أو كبيراً أو مريضاً غير صالح للقتال عليه، كان له سهم الراجل، وكذا إذا كان الفرس مهراً.
الجماعة،
ولو له فرسان واصل بما قبله أي لا يعطى للفارس إلا سهمان ولو كان له فرسان أو أكثر، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا للفارس ثلاثة أسهم؛ لقول ابن عمر لما أنه علي أسهم للفارس ثلاثة أسهم، وللراجل سهماً، رواه و به قالت الثلاثة. وله: قول مجمع بن جارية له قسمت خيبر إلى أن قال: "إنه عليها أعطى الفارس سهمين، والراجل سهما"، رواه أحمد وأبو داود، وحديث ابن عمر لهما محمول على التنفيل كما روي أنه عليها أعطى سلمة بن الأكوع سهم الفارس والراجل، رواه أحمد ومسلم بمعناه وقال أبو يوسف: من كان له فرسان يسهم لفرسين، أي له أربعة أسهم للفرسين إن دخل دار الحرب بهما؛ لأنه علي أعطى الزبير خمسة أسهم، ولهما أنه عليها لم يسهم يوم خيبر لصاحب الأفراس إلا لفرس واحد، والصحيح من حكاية الزبير اهلها الله أنه أعطاه أربعة أسهم: سهماً له، وسهماً لأمه صفية، وسهمين لفرسه، رواه أحمد، فلا يلزم حجة، ولئن صح فهو محمول على التنفيل؛ ولأن البراء بن أوس قاد فرسين، فلم يسهم رسول الله الله إلا الفرس واحد، ولأن القتال لا يتحقق بفرسين دفعة واحدة، ولهذا لا يسهم على ثلاثة أفراس (عيني، مستخلص، فتح) والبراذين كالعتاق: البراذين بفتح الموحدة وبالذال المعجمة جمع برذون بكسر الباء: خيل العجم يعني ما أمه وأبوه ،عجميان ويقال له الكودن ،يوكف ويشبه به البليد، وقوله: "كالعتاق" بكسر العين جمع عتيق، وهو الفرس العربي من كرائم الخيل، أي البراذين والعتاق سواء في استحقاق السهم؛ لأن إرهاب العدو يضاف إلى -
Section: meaning on the explanation of the manner of division. For the foot soldier is one share, and for the cavalryman is two shares, even if he has two horses, and pack horses are like Arabian horses. (Collection: baradhin, sing. bardhawn, which is the horse of the non-Arabs.) Section: In some copies 'on the manner of division' is added in the matn, and in some they sufficed with just 'Section', and he dedicated a separate section for the explanation of division due to its many branches. Division is making the common share specific, and that is the correct view. The manner of division is that the imam divides the spoils, separates the fifth first, and divides the remaining four-fifths among the combatants in the detail mentioned by the author. (Mahshi, Fath) For the foot soldier is one share: [i.e., the imam divides the spoils, separates its fifth first and divides the remainder among the combatants such that the foot soldier gets one share by consensus. (T, 'Ayni)] The foot soldier is the opposite of the cavalryman, plural rujjāl like sāḥib and ṣaḥb, meaning the one who walks on foot, so he gets one share by consensus. ('Ayni, Fath, Mukhtasar) And for the cavalryman is two shares [if his horse is sound, mature, fit for fighting. (T)] And no share is given for a jointly owned horse used for fighting, unless one of the partners rents the other's share before entering, then the share goes to the renter. The general application includes cases where the horse is usurped, borrowed, or rented. It is required that the horse be fit for fighting; if it is small, or mature but sick and unfit for fighting, then he gets the share of a foot soldier, similarly if the horse is a foal. The group: 'even if he has two horses' is connected to what precedes, meaning the cavalryman is given only two shares even if he has two horses or more. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: for the cavalryman is three shares, due to the saying of Ibn 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) that the Prophet (peace be upon him) assigned to the cavalryman three shares and to the foot soldier one share, narrated by... and the three (imams) said the same. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof is the saying of Mujammi' ibn Jariya: 'Khaibar was divided... until he said: the Prophet (peace be upon him) gave the cavalryman two shares and the foot soldier one share,' narrated by Ahmad and Abu Dawud. The hadith of Ibn 'Umar is interpreted as a nafil (extra grant) just as it is narrated that the Prophet gave Salama ibn al-Akwa' the share of a cavalryman and a foot soldier, narrated by Ahmad and Muslim with its meaning. Abu Yusuf said: whoever has two horses gets a share for two horses, i.e., he gets four shares for the two horses if he enters dar al-harb with them, because the Prophet gave al-Zubair five shares. And for them (Abu Hanifa and the two?) it is that the Prophet did not assign on the day of Khaibar for the owner of multiple horses except for one horse. The correct report about al-Zubair (may Allah be pleased with him) is that he gave him four shares: a share for himself, a share for his mother Safiyya, and two shares for his horse, narrated by Ahmad, so it is not a binding proof, and even if authentic it is interpreted as a nafil, and because al-Bara' ibn Aws led two horses, and the Messenger of Allah only gave a share for one horse, and because fighting cannot be realized with two horses simultaneously, and thus he does not give shares for three horses. ('Ayni, Mukhtasar, Fath) And pack horses (baradhin) are like Arabian horses (al-‘itāq): baradhin (pl. of bardhawn) are the horses of the non-Arabs, meaning those whose mother and father are both non-Arabic, also called kawdan, slow and compared to the dullard. His saying 'like the Arabian ones' (al-‘itāq, pl. of ‘atīq) which is the Arabian horse of noble lineage, meaning pack horses and Arabian horses are equal in entitlement to the share, because the intimidation of the enemy is attributed to -
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This section explains the division of spoils (ghanimah) between foot soldiers and cavalrymen. The commentator first defines the shares: a foot soldier gets one share by consensus, while a cavalryman gets two shares, provided his horse is fit for combat. Jointly owned horses do not receive an extra share unless one partner rents the other's share. The main dispute is whether a cavalryman with more than one horse gets additional shares. Abu Hanifa holds that he gets only two shares total, regardless of the number of horses. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue for three shares, citing Ibn 'Umar's narration that the Prophet gave three shares to a cavalryman. Abu Hanifa counters with Mujammi' ibn Jariya's report of two shares at Khaibar and interprets Ibn 'Umar's report as a nafil (extra grant). Abu Yusuf has an independent view allowing four shares for two horses, but the commentator refutes this with the practice at Khaibar and the principle that a warrior cannot fight with two horses at once. Finally, the commentator clarifies that pack horses (baradhin) are treated the same as Arabian horses (al-‘itāq) because both contribute to intimidation of the enemy, so the rider of a pack horse also gets the cavalryman's two shares.

Key Terms
راجل (rājil): Foot soldier; a combatant who fights on foot.
فارس (fāris): Cavalryman; a combatant who fights on a horse.
برذون (bardhawn): Pack horse; a non-Arabian horse, often slower and less noble.
تنفيل (tanfīl): Extra grant; additional shares given beyond the standard distribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how many shares does a cavalryman receive if he has two horses?
A) Two shares total
B) Three shares total
C) Four shares total
D) One share only

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that a cavalryman receives two shares total, even if he has multiple horses, because the standard sunnah limits the horse share to one mount per warrior.
