Jihad — Armed Conflict Rules
intermediate
10 min
Basic Shares for Warriors and the Multiple Horses Dispute
Explains the basic share allocation and intra-Hanafi dispute on multiple horses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on shares for foot soldiers and horsemen, identify the condition for a horse to qualify, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding multiple horses with the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Every foot soldier gets one share; every horseman gets two shares, regardless of how many horses he brings.

War booty (ghanimah) is divided by the Imam: one-fifth is set aside, and the remaining four-fifths are distributed among the warriors. The basic rule is clear: foot soldier one share, horseman two shares. However, what if a horseman owns multiple horses? The Hanafi school has an internal dispute that illustrates how prophetic practice is weighed against conflicting narrations. This lesson walks through the evidence and the final ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule and its consensus status
Locate the statement that a foot soldier (rajil) gets one share and a horseman (faris) gets two shares. Note that this is by consensus (ijma). Then find the condition that the horse must be sound, large, and fit for fighting.
If the horse is unfit, the rider is treated as a foot soldier.
2
Examine the multiple horses scenario
The text asks: what if a horseman has two or more horses? Extract the three positions: Abu Hanifa (two shares total), the two companions Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (three shares), and the separate opinion of Abu Yusuf (four shares for two horses).
The dispute is solely about the number of shares; all agree the horseman himself gets one share.
3
Trace the proofs for each position
The two companions rely on the hadith of Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet gave three shares to a horseman. Abu Hanifa relies on the hadith of Mujammi' ibn Jariyah that at Khaybar the Prophet gave two shares to a horseman. Also note Abu Yusuf's citation of the story of al-Zubayr.
Both sides use ahadith; Abu Hanifa's evidence is considered more specific to the actual practice.
4
Understand the Hanafi tarjih (preference) and its reasoning
The commentator explains why Abu Hanifa's view is preferred: the hadith of Ibn 'Umar is interpreted as a supererogatory grant (tanzil), not a standard rule. Additional evidence: al-Bara' ibn Aws had two horses but only got a share for one, and fighting on two horses simultaneously is impossible.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is Abu Hanifa's: two shares total.
5
Apply the ruling on type of horse (baradhin vs itaq)
The text equates non-Arab packhorses (baradhin) with purebred Arab horses (itaq) in entitlement to a share. This refutes a contrary view from Syria. The reason is that terrorizing the enemy is achieved with both types.
This ruling overrides a regional custom that excluded packhorses.

Book of Siyar and Jihad. How the division is made. Section: Explaining how the division is made. For the foot soldier is one share, and for the horseman are two shares, even if he has two horses. And the packhorses are like the purebred horses. (Packhorses: plural of packhorse, which is a non-Arab horse.) Section: In some copies, 'on how the division is made' is added in the text, and in some they suffice with just 'Section', and the explanation of division is made a separate section due to its many branches. Division: making the common share specific, and that is correct. The way of division is that the Imam divides the spoils, first separates the fifth of the wealth, and divides the four-fifths among the warriors in the detail mentioned by the author. (Hashiyah, Fath) For the foot soldier is one share: [that is, the Imam divides the spoils, separates its fifth first, and divides the rest among the warriors such that the foot soldier has one share by consensus. (T, Ayni)] The foot soldier is opposite of the horseman, and is pluralized as 'rajl' like 'sahib' and 'sahb', i.e., one who walks on feet. He has one share by consensus. (Ayni, Fath, Mustakhlas) And for the horseman are two shares [if his horse is sound, large, and fit for fighting. (T)] No share is given for a horse shared for fighting upon, unless one of the two partners leases the other's share before entering, then the share is for the lessee. Its generality includes if the horse is usurped, borrowed, or leased. The condition is that the horse be fit for fighting; if small, large, or sick, not fit for fighting upon, he gets the foot soldier's share. Similarly if the horse is a colt. And if he has two horses—connected to what preceded—i.e., the horseman is not given more than two shares even if he has two horses or more. This is according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions said: the horseman gets three shares, due to the statement of Ibn 'Umar—may God be pleased with him—that the Prophet—peace be upon him—gave the horseman three shares and the foot soldier one share, narrated by... and with it the three said. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof: the statement of Mujammi' ibn Jariyah who said that at the division of Khaybar the Prophet gave the horseman two shares and the foot soldier one share, narrated by Ahmad and Abu Dawud. The hadith of Ibn 'Umar is interpreted as supererogatory (tanzil), as it is narrated that the Prophet gave Salamah ibn al-Akwa' the share of a horseman and a foot soldier, narrated by Ahmad and Muslim with its meaning. And Abu Yusuf said: whoever has two horses gets shares for two horses, i.e., four shares for the two horses if he enters Dar al-Harb with them, because the Prophet gave al-Zubayr five shares. And the two companions' proof: the Prophet did not give on the day of Khaybar to the owner of multiple horses except for one horse. The correct version of the story of al-Zubayr—may God be pleased with him—is that he gave him four shares: one for himself, one for his mother Safiyyah, and two for his horse, narrated by Ahmad, so it is not a conclusive proof. And if it is authentic, it is interpreted as supererogatory. And because al-Bara' ibn Aws led two horses, and the Messenger of God—peace be upon him—only gave share for one horse. And because fighting cannot be carried out with two horses simultaneously; thus no share is given for three horses. (Ayni, Mustakhlas, Fath) And the packhorses are like the purebred: 'al-baradhin' with fatih on the ba' and dhal mu'jamah, plural of 'bardhun' with kasr on the ba': non-Arab horses, meaning both parents are non-Arab, also called 'kawdan', it is slow and resembles the dull. His saying 'like the purebred' with kasr on the 'ayn, plural of 'atiq', which is the Arab horse of noble breeds, i.e., packhorses and purebreds are equal in entitlement to the share, because terrorizing the enemy is attributed to the horse, as Allah says: 'And from the tethering of horses you terrorize thereby the enemy of Allah' (al-Anfal: 60). The name 'khayl' applies to all types of horses: Arab, packhorse, hajin, and muqrif. The hajin is one whose father is from the packhorses and mother is Arab. The muqrif is one whose father is Arab and mother from the packhorses. He only said 'the packhorses are like the purebred' because the people of Syria say: no share is given for packhorses, and they narrated a strange hadith. Our proof: the benefit in each is considered, for the Arab horse is stronger in attack and escape, and packhorses are more patient and gentler, so they are equal. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/426) (Mustakhlas, Fath) 'La al-rahilah' can be read with raf' and the implied phrase 'la takunu al-rahilatu ka al-ajtiaq', or with jar and then the implied 'la yus'hamu li-ajl al-rahilah'. Al-rahilah: the riding mount from camels, male or female, the ta' is for singularity or transference from adjective to noun. 'And the mule' is conjoined to it. The meaning: no share for the owner of the riding camel and the mule, because terrorizing is not achieved with them, as one does not fight on them, so their rider is like the foot soldier. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/426) (Fath) And the consideration is for the horseman, etc., such that if he enters Dar al-Harb as a horseman, then his horse dies and he fights on foot, he is entitled to the horseman's share by agreement. If he enters as a foot soldier, then buys a horse, he is entitled to the foot soldier's share. According to Abu Hanifa, he is entitled to the horseman's share. If he sells it after completion of the war, the share of horsemen does not lapse; if he sells it during combat, the share of horsemen lapses. According to al-Shafi'i, consideration is whether he is foot or horseman at the end of the war, because the cause of entitlement is overpowering and fighting, so the state of the fighter at that time must be considered. Malik and Ahmad also said this. Our proof: the crossing itself is fighting, because fear of them is caused by it, and the state after it is a state of continuity, so it is not considered. And because ascertaining the reality of fighting is difficult, as is witnessing the battle (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/426) because it is the state of meeting of the two lines, so the crossing is established in its place, as it is the cause that apparently leads to it if done with the intention of fighting. Al-mujawazah means crossing the 'darb', which linguistically is the wide gate on the road, and every entrance of the Romans is a 'darb' from their 'durub'. But what is meant by 'darb' here is the isthmus separating the two lands, i.e., Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb. If one crosses the 'darb', he enters Dar al-Harb; if the people of war cross the 'darb', they enter Dar al-Islam. (Mustakhlas, Fath) Al-mujawazah: separation from the 'darb' separating Dar al-Islam and Dar al-Harb. (T, Ayni) 'And for the slave, etc.': this is a predicate with its conjunctions, and the subject is delayed, namely 'al-rdhi', i.e., for the slave if he fights unconditionally, whether he is a male slave, a mudabbar, a mukatab, a partially freed, or a umm walad; for the woman whether free or slave, if she fights, treats the wounded, cares for the sick, serves the warriors, or guards their belongings; for the boy, similarly the insane and the feeble-minded, if they fight with the Imam's permission; and for one who fights or guides on the road, i.e., for these mentioned persons is the 'radh' i.e., a small gift according to the Imam's discretion. It is narrated from Ibn 'Abbas that the Prophet used to go to battle with women who would treat the wounded and be given gifts (yuhdhayna) from the spoils, meaning they were given. As for a full share, it was not assigned to them. Also from him: the slave and woman did not get a share, except that they were given gifts from the spoils of the people, narrated by Ahmad and Muslim.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the rules for dividing war booty (ghanimah). The passage type is a ruling_with_proof, as the commentator elaborates on the author's statements and provides evidence. The discussion covers several key points: 1) The basic rule: A foot soldier (rajil) gets one share, a horseman (faris) gets two shares. This is by consensus. 2) Conditions for the horse: it must be sound, large, and fit for fighting. If not, the rider is treated as a foot soldier. 3) Multiple horses: According to Imam Abu Hanifa, even if a horseman has two or more horses, he still gets only two shares total (one for himself and one for his horse). Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that he gets three shares—one for himself and one for each of two horses. Abu Yusuf further said if one has two horses, he gets four shares? Actually the text says Abu Yusuf said: 'Whoever has two horses, shares are given for two horses, i.e., four shares for the two horses if he enters Dar al-Harb with them.' But the main dispute recorded is between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) where the latter say three shares. The commentator presents proofs: The Sahibayn cite the hadith of Ibn 'Umar that the Prophet gave three shares to a horseman; Abu Hanifa cites the hadith of Mujammi' ibn Jariyah that at Khaybar the Prophet gave two shares to a horseman. The commentator then explains that the hadith of Ibn 'Umar is interpreted as supererogatory (tanzil) because there is another report that the Prophet gave Salamah ibn al-Akwa' both the share of a horseman and foot soldier. 4) Type of horse: Non-Arab horses (baradhin) are treated the same as purebred Arab horses (itiq) in deserving a share, because the benefit of terrorizing the enemy applies to both. This refutes a view from Syria that no share is given for packhorses. 5) Mounts not used for fighting: Riding camels (rahilah) and mules do not entitle to a share; the rider is like a foot soldier. 6) Timing of entitlement: The status (foot or horse) is determined at the time of crossing the border (mujawazah)—i.e., when the army leaves Dar al-Islam. This is the Hanafi view against al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad who consider the status at the end of war. The Hanafi reasoning: crossing itself is a form of fighting because it instills fear, and it is easier to determine. 7) Non-combatants: Slaves (male or female), women, boys, the insane, and feeble-minded, if they participate by fighting or supporting (such as treating wounds or guiding), do not get a full share but receive a small gift (radh) at the Imam's discretion. This is based on practice of the Prophet and Companions.

Key Terms
الراجل (al-rajil): The foot soldier, one who walks on foot in battle.
الفارس (al-faris): The horseman, one who fights on horseback.
سهم (sahm): A share of the spoils (ghanimah) allocated to an eligible participant.
البراذين (al-barādhin): Packhorses or non-Arab horses (both parents non-Arab), also called 'kawdan'.
العتاق (al-‘itāq): Purebred Arab horses of noble lineage.
التنفيل (al-tanfīl): A supererogatory grant or extra allowance given from the spoils beyond the normal share.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling for a horseman who has two horses?
A) He gets three shares.
B) He gets two shares.
C) He gets four shares.
D) He gets one share.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, following Abu Hanifa, awards a horseman only two shares regardless of the number of horses he brings, based on the specific practice at Khaybar.

Determining Shares: Horseman or Foot Soldier
Rulings on shares for horses, riders, and the time of consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of al-mujawazah to determine whether a fighter receives a horseman's or foot soldier's share, and explain the difference with the Shafi'i school.

Core Idea
Crossing the border is the decisive moment for spoils shares because it marks the beginning of combat.

When a fighter enters enemy territory, should his share be based on his status at the moment of crossing, or at the end of the war? This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the Hanafi position, which hinges on the concept of al-mujawazah. You will learn the proof for this view and the practical consequence of the disagreement with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling: only horses get shares
Note that the passage begins by stating that riding camels and mules do not entitle their owners to a share; only horses (the khayl) are considered for a horseman's share. The rider of a non-horse mount is treated like a foot soldier.
2
Determine the time of consideration
The Hanafi school holds that the decisive moment is when the fighter crosses the boundary (darb) into enemy territory (al-mujawazah). This is the central ruling of this passage.
Contrast with the Shafi'i view: end of the war.
3
Understand the Hanafi proof
The text argues that crossing itself is an act of fighting because it instills fear in the enemy. The state after crossing is one of continuity, not a new state. Also, verifying actual combat is impractical.
The proof is based on the concept that the beginning of the operation is itself combat.
4
Compare with the opposing school
Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik, Ahmad) consider the fighter's state at the end of the war because the cause of entitlement is overpowering (qahr) and fighting. The Hanafi response is that crossing is the start and a clear indicator.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf in usul.
5
Apply to scenarios
Practice with cases: a fighter enters as a horseman then his horse dies; or enters on foot then buys a horse. Apply the Hanafi rule consistently.
The intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the second scenario: Abu Hanifa's relied-upon view is foot soldier share.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا الراحلة يحتمل أن يقرأ بالرفع وتقدير الكلام لا تكون الراحلة كالعتاق، أو يقرأ بالجر وتقديره حينئذ: لا يسهم لأجل الراحلة، والراحلة: المركب من الإبل ذكراً كان أو أنثى، والتاء فيه للوحدة أو للنقل من الوصفية إلى الاسمية، والبغل عطف عليها، والمعنى: لا يسهم لصاحب الراحلة والبغل؛ لأن الإرهاب لا يقع بهما؛ إذ لا يقاتل عليهما، فكان راكبهما كالراجل. رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٦/١] (فتح) والعبرة للفارس إلخ حتى لو دخل دار الحرب فارساً، فنفق فرسه، وقاتل راجلاً، استحق سهم الفارس اتفاقاً، ولو دخلها راجلاً، فاشترى فرساً، استحق سهم الراجل، وعن أبي حنيفة: أنه يستحق سهم الفارس، ولو باعه بعد الفراغ لم يسقط سهم الفرسان، ولو باعه في حال القتال سقط سهم الفرسان، وعند الشافعي: يعتبر كونه راجلاً أو فارساً حال انقضاء الحرب؛ لأن سبب الاستحقاق هو القهر والقتال، فوجب اعتبار حال القاتل عند ذلك، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، ولنا أن المجاوزة نفسها قتال؛ لأنه يلحقهم الخوف بها، والحال بعدها حالة الدوام فلا تعتبر؛ ولأن الوقوف على حقيقة القتال متعسر، وكذا على شهود الوقعة رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٦/١] لأنه حال التقاء الصفين، فيقام المجاوزة مقامه؛ إذ هو السبب المفضي إليه ظاهراً إذا كان على قصد القتال. والمراد بالمجاوزة التجاوز عن الدرب، وهو لغة: الباب الواسع على السكة، وعلى كل مدخل من مداخل الروم درب من دروبها لكن المراد من الدرب ههنا هو البرزخ الحاجز بين الدارين أي دار الإسلام ودار الحرب، حتى لو جاوزت الدرب دخلت في دار الحرب، ولو جاوز أهل الحرب الدرب دخلوا في دار الإسلام. (مستخلص، فتح) المجاوزة: أي الانفصال من الدرب الفاصل بين دار الإسلام ودار الحرب. (ط، عيني) وللمملوك إلخ: خبر مقدم مع معطوفاته، والمبتدأ مؤخر، وهو قوله: الرضخ أي للمملوك إذا قاتل مطلقاً سواء كان قناً أو مدبراً أو مكاتباً أو معتق البعض أو ولد أم الولد وللمرأة سواء كانت حرة أو أمة إذا كانت تقاتل أو تداوي الجرحى أو تقوم على المرضى أو تخدم الغانمين أو تحفظ متاعهم وللصبي وكذا المجنون والمعتوه إذا قاتلوا بإذن الإمام وللذي إذا قاتل أو دل على الطريق أي لهؤلاء المذكورين الرضخ أي العطاء القليل بحسب ما يرى الإمام؛ روي عن ابن عباس لما أن النبي عليه كان يغزو بالنساء فيداوين الجرحى ويحذين من الغنيمة أي يعطين، وأما السهم، فلم يضرب لهن، وعنه أيضاً لم يكن للعبد والمرأة سهم، إلا أن يحذيا من غنائم القوم، رواه أحمد ومسلم، والدمّي الرضحُ، لا السهم، والخمس لليتامى والمساكين، وابن السبيل، وقدم ذوو القربى ويرضخ لهؤلاء إذا كانوا يقاتلون، والمرأة وإن كانت عاجزة عن القتال طبعاً، ولكن مداواتها الجرحي قائم مقام القتال، والذمي لا يعطى له إلا بالقتال أو بدلالته على الطريق، وقوله: "لا" "السهم أي لا يعطى لهؤلاء السهم؛ لما روينا، ولا يبلغ بالرضخ السهم إلا في دلالة الذمي، فإنه يزاد على السهم إذا كان في دلالته منفعة عظيمة؛ لأن ما يأخذه أجرة فيعطى بالغاً ما بلغ. فإن قلت: روى الترمذي أنه علي أسهم لقوم من اليهود قاتلوا معه وللصبيان، وروى أحمد وأبو داود والنسائي أيضاً. قلت: كله محمول على الرضخ [رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٦/١] (فتح)
It is probable that no riding camel is read in the nominative, and the implied speech is: the riding camel is not like the thoroughbred horse, or it is read in the genitive and the implied speech then: no share is given for the riding camel. The riding camel is the mount from camels, male or female, and the ta is for singularity or for transfer from adjectival to nominal. The mule is conjoined. The meaning: no share is given to the owner of the riding camel or mule because intimidation does not occur with them; they are not fought upon, so their rider is like a foot soldier. (Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 1/426) (Fath) And the consideration is for the horseman, etc., so if he enters the abode of war as a horseman, then his horse dies and he fights on foot, he deserves the horseman's share by agreement. If he enters it on foot, then buys a horse, he deserves the foot soldier's share. From Abu Hanifa: he deserves the horseman's share. If he sells it after finishing, the horseman's share does not drop; if he sells it during fighting, the horseman's share drops. According to al-Shafi'i: consideration is whether he is on foot or mounted at the end of the war because the cause of entitlement is overpowering and fighting, so the state of the fighter must be considered at that time. Malik and Ahmad say the same. Our proof: crossing itself is fighting because fear afflicts the enemy by it, and the state after it is a state of continuity, so it is not considered; and because ascertaining the reality of fighting is difficult, as is witnessing the battle. (Rumuz) because it is the moment of the two lines meeting, so crossing is taken in its place as it is the cause leading to it apparently if done with the intention to fight. Crossing means passing the boundary (darb), which in language is a wide door on the road, and every entrance of the Byzantine territories is one of their darbs, but the meaning here is the barrier between the two abodes, i.e., the abode of Islam and the abode of war, so if one crosses the darb, he enters the abode of war, and if the enemy crosses the darb, they enter the abode of Islam. (Mustakhlas, Fath) Crossing: departure from the darb separating the two abodes. (T, Ayni) And for the slave, etc.: a fronted predicate with its conjunctions, and the subject is delayed, which is his saying: 'radh', i.e., for the slave if he fights absolutely, whether he is a qinn, mudabbar, mukatab, mu'ataq al-ba'd, or umm walad; and for the woman whether free or slave if she fights or treats the wounded, or tends the sick, or serves the ghunamin (spoilers), or guards their goods; and for the boy, likewise the insane and the mentally deficient, if they fight with the Imam's permission; and for the dhimm refugee if he fights or shows the way. To these mentioned, radh (a small gift) is given according to what the Imam sees. It is narrated from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet used to go on expeditions with women who treated the wounded and were given from the booty, i.e., they were given, but a share was not struck for them. Also from him: the slave and woman had no share except that they were given from the spoils of the people, narrated by Ahmad and Muslim. And the bloodied one receives radh, not a share. And the khums is for orphans, the poor, and the wayfarer, and the relatives of the Prophet are given precedence. And radh is given to these if they fight, and the woman, even if she is naturally incapable of fighting, her treating the wounded stands in the place of fighting. The dhimm refugee is given only for fighting or for showing the way. And his statement: 'not the share' means these are not given a share, based on what we narrated. And radh does not reach the share except in the case of a dhimm refugee showing the way, for he is increased beyond the share if his guidance has great benefit, because what he takes is a fee, so he is given whatever it reaches. If you say: al-Tirmidhi narrated that 'Ali gave shares to a group of Jews who fought with him and to boys, and Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and al-Nasa'i also narrated it. I say: All of it is interpreted as radh. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 1/426] (Fath)
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding the division of spoils (ghanima) among participants in a military expedition. It covers several key points: (1) Only horses (khayl) are entitled to a full share (sahm) for the mount; riding camels and mules do not receive shares because they are not used for fighting and intimidation. The rider of such an animal is treated like a foot soldier. (2) The status of being a horseman or foot soldier is determined at the moment of crossing the boundary (darb) into enemy territory (al-mujawazah). This is a Hanafi position, based on the idea that crossing itself is an act of fighting because it instills fear in the enemy. Imam al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad consider the status at the end of the war, but the Hanafi school holds that the difficulty of verifying actual fighting makes the crossing a reliable indicator. (3) Non-combatants such as slaves, women, children, the insane, and dhimm refugees who participate in some way (fighting, treatment, guidance) are given a small discretionary grant called radh (رضخ), not a full share (sahm). This is based on prophetic practice. The radh is at the Imam's discretion, and in the case of a dhimm refugee providing valuable guidance, it may exceed a regular share because it is considered a fee. The passage also addresses a potential counter-narration about 'Ali giving shares to Jews and children, explaining that these were radh, not full shares.

Key Terms
المجاوزة (al-mujāwazah): The act of crossing the boundary (darb) separating the abode of Islam from the abode of war.
الدرب (al-darb): The barrier or border that separates Dar al-Islam from Dar al-Harb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what determines whether a fighter receives the share of a horseman or a foot soldier?
A) His status at the end of the battle
B) His status when crossing the boundary into enemy territory
C) The number of enemies he kills
D) The type of mount he rides throughout the expedition

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines a fighter's share at the moment of crossing the border into enemy territory, because crossing itself is an act of combat.

Radh: Discretionary Grants for Non-Combatants
Rulings on radh for non-combatants in spoils division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify who is eligible for radh (discretionary grant) and state the conditions under which it is given, based on the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Non-combatants who assist in jihad receive a small grant (radh), not a full share.

Not everyone who participates in a military expedition is entitled to a full share (sahm) of the spoils. Slaves, women, children, the insane, and dhimm refugees may receive a smaller discretionary grant called radh. This lesson examines the categories of people eligible for radh, the conditions that trigger it, and the basis in prophetic practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify who receives radh
Read the passage starting from 'And for the slave' (وللمملوك). It lists categories: slave (of various types), woman, boy, insane/mentally deficient, and dhimm refugee.
Note the inclusion of umm walad and mu'ataq al-ba'd among slaves.
2
Note the conditions for receiving radh
These individuals must perform some service: fighting, treating the wounded, tending the sick, serving the soldiers, guarding goods, or showing the way. For women, even if naturally incapable of fighting, treating the wounded stands in place of fighting.
The Imam's permission is needed for slaves, women, children, etc. to participate.
3
Understand the basis: Prophetic practice
The passage references Ibn Abbas: the Prophet used to take women on expeditions, they treated the wounded, and they were given (radh), not a full share. Hadith from Ahmad and Muslim support this.
This is a dalil naqli (textual proof).
4
Distinguish radh from a full share
Radh is a small gift at the Imam's discretion, not a fixed share. It is less than a full share, except for the dhimm refugee who may receive more than a share if his guidance is exceptionally beneficial.
The passage explicitly says: 'And radh does not reach the share except in the case of a dhimm refugee showing the way'.
5
Address potential counter-evidence
The passage considers a narration that 'Ali gave shares to Jews and children. The commentator interprets this as radh, not full shares.
This shows the importance of reconciling conflicting reports.

Crossing means passing the boundary (darb), which in language is a wide door on the road, and every entrance of the Byzantine territories is one of their darbs, but the meaning here is the barrier between the two abodes, i.e., the abode of Islam and the abode of war, so if one crosses the darb, he enters the abode of war, and if the enemy crosses the darb, they enter the abode of Islam. (Mustakhlas, Fath) Crossing: departure from the darb separating the two abodes. (T, Ayni) And for the slave, etc.: a fronted predicate with its conjunctions, and the subject is delayed, which is his saying: 'radh', i.e., for the slave if he fights absolutely, whether he is a qinn, mudabbar, mukatab, mu'ataq al-ba'd, or umm walad; and for the woman whether free or slave if she fights or treats the wounded, or tends the sick, or serves the ghunamin (spoilers), or guards their goods; and for the boy, likewise the insane and the mentally deficient, if they fight with the Imam's permission; and for the dhimm refugee if he fights or shows the way. To these mentioned, radh (a small gift) is given according to what the Imam sees. It is narrated from Ibn Abbas that the Prophet used to go on expeditions with women who treated the wounded and were given from the booty, i.e., they were given, but a share was not struck for them. Also from him: the slave and woman had no share except that they were given from the spoils of the people, narrated by Ahmad and Muslim. And the bloodied one receives radh, not a share. And the khums is for orphans, the poor, and the wayfarer, and the relatives of the Prophet are given precedence. And radh is given to these if they fight, and the woman, even if she is naturally incapable of fighting, her treating the wounded stands in the place of fighting. The dhimm refugee is given only for fighting or for showing the way. And his statement: 'not the share' means these are not given a share, based on what we narrated. And radh does not reach the share except in the case of a dhimm refugee showing the way, for he is increased beyond the share if his guidance has great benefit, because what he takes is a fee, so he is given whatever it reaches. If you say: al-Tirmidhi narrated that 'Ali gave shares to a group of Jews who fought with him and to boys, and Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and al-Nasa'i also narrated it. I say: All of it is interpreted as radh. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 1/426] (Fath)
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding the division of spoils (ghanima) among participants in a military expedition. It covers several key points: (1) Only horses (khayl) are entitled to a full share (sahm) for the mount; riding camels and mules do not receive shares because they are not used for fighting and intimidation. The rider of such an animal is treated like a foot soldier. (2) The status of being a horseman or foot soldier is determined at the moment of crossing the boundary (darb) into enemy territory (al-mujawazah). This is a Hanafi position, based on the idea that crossing itself is an act of fighting because it instills fear in the enemy. Imam al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad consider the status at the end of the war, but the Hanafi school holds that the difficulty of verifying actual fighting makes the crossing a reliable indicator. (3) Non-combatants such as slaves, women, children, the insane, and dhimm refugees who participate in some way (fighting, treatment, guidance) are given a small discretionary grant called radh (رضخ), not a full share (sahm). This is based on prophetic practice. The radh is at the Imam's discretion, and in the case of a dhimm refugee providing valuable guidance, it may exceed a regular share because it is considered a fee. The passage also addresses a potential counter-narration about 'Ali giving shares to Jews and children, explaining that these were radh, not full shares.

Key Terms
الرضخ (al-rakhḍ): A small discretionary grant given to non-combatant participants in a military expedition, such as slaves, women, children, and dhimm refugees.
السهم (al-sahm): A full share of the spoils allotted to a combatant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT eligible for radh?
A) A slave who fights
B) A woman who treats the wounded
C) A dhimmi refugee who shows the way
D) A free Muslim horseman who fights

Key Takeaway
Non-combatant participants in a jihad expedition receive a discretionary grant (radh) instead of a full share, as established by the practice of the Prophet.

Division of Khums: Three Shares and Precedence of Relatives
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on khums distribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dividing khums into three shares, explain the proofs from caliphal practice and hadith, and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i position regarding the share of the Prophet's relatives.

Core Idea
Khums is divided into three shares; no separate share for relatives, only the poor among them receive priority.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the distribution of the one-fifth of spoils (khums). The Hanafi school holds it should be divided among orphans, the poor, and wayfarers only, citing the practice of the four caliphs and specific hadith evidence. A major disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i concerns whether the Prophet's relatives receive a separate share. The passage also resolves an internal Hanafi difference on giving precedence to the poor among those relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and base Hanafi ruling
Determine that the act is the distribution of khums from spoils. State the Hanafi division into three shares: orphans, poor, wayfarers.
The khums is one-fifth of the spoils, set aside before the remaining four-fifths are distributed among the fighters.
2
Locate the primary proof from Quran
Read the verse (al-Anfal 41) that lists the categories. Note that the Hanafi school interprets the list as defining three categories, not five.
The verse mentions Allah, Messenger, dhawu al-qurba, orphans, poor, wayfarer. Hanafi school says the share for Allah and Messenger is not a separate category.
3
Analyze the opposing Shafi'i view
Understand that al-Shafi'i divides khums into five shares, including a separate share for the Prophet's relatives (dhawu al-qurba), and rich relatives also benefit.
Shafi'i relies on the literal wording of the verse and the practice of the Prophet giving to Banu al-Muttalib.
4
Examine the Hanafi response and proofs
The Hanafi school responds with the consensus (ijma') of the four caliphs who divided into three, a hadith about compensation only for poor relatives, and the story of 'Ali and Jubayr that shows 'nearness in support' (qurb al-nusra) is the criterion.
The caliphal practice is considered a binding precedent (madhhab al-sahaba).
5
Refine with the rule of precedence for poor relatives
Within each of the three shares, the poor among the Prophet's relatives are given priority over other recipients. This is the relied-upon view (al-Karkhi), not al-Tahawi's view that their share is charity.
The pronoun 'minhum' in 'the poor of them' refers to dhawu al-qurba.

Source Text

والخمس لليتامى والمساكين، وابن السبيل، وقدم ذوو القربى شروع في بيان حكم الخمس الذي كان أفرزه الإمام أولاً، أي يقسم الخمس على ثلاثة أسهم: سهم لليتامى، وسهم للمساكين، وسهم لابن السبيل، فيعطى لكل واحد منهم سهم؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّمَا غَنِمْتُمْ مِنْ شَيْءٍ فَأَنَّ اللَّهُ حُمُسَهُ وَلِلرَّسُولِ وَلِذِي الْقُرْبَى وَالْيَتَامَى وَالْمَسَاكِينِ وَابْنِ السَّبِيلِ ﴾ (الأنفال:٤١)، وعند الشافعي: يخمس أخماساً: سهم لذوي القربى وسهم للنبي عليه يخلفه فيه الإمام، ويصرفه إلى مصالح المسلمين، والباقي للثلاثة، ويستوي في ذوي القربى فقيرهم وغنيهم، ويقسم بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، ويكون ذلك لبني المطلب، ولا يكون لغيرهم؛ لأنه لم يفرق في الكتاب ولا في السنة بين الغني والفقير. ولنا: أن الخلفاء الأربعة قسموا الخمس على ثلاثة أسهم كما قلنا وكفى بهم قدوة، وقال عليه: "يا معشر بني هاشم إن الله كره لكم غسالة الناس وأوساخهم، وعوضكم منها بخمس الخمس"، والعوض إنما يثبت في حق من يثبت له المعوض وهم الفقراء، وورد أنه جاءه علي عثمان الله من بني عبد شمس، وجبير بن مطعم من بني نوفل فقالا: إنا لا ننكر فضل بني هاشم، لكن نحن وبنو المطلب في القرابة إليك سواء فما بالك أعطيتهم وأحرمتنا؟ فقال: "إنهم لا هكذا" وشبك بين أصابعه في الجاهلية والإسلام"، فدل أن المراد من النص قرب النصرة لا قرب القرابة، هاشم وبني يزالوا معي فكان قسمة الخلفاء بمحضر من الصحابة، فكان إجماعاً [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٦/١] (مستخلص، فتح) وقدم ذوو القربى إلخ الضمير في "منهم" يرجع إلى ذوي القربى، وفي "عليهم" إلى الأصناف الثلاثة، أي قدم الفقراء من ذوي قرابة النبي عليه على الأصناف الثلاثة المذكورة، والحاصل أن يتامى ذوي القربى يدخلون في سهم اليتامى، ومساكين ذوي القربي يدخلون في سهم المساكين، وأبناء السبيل يدخلون في سهم أبناء السبيل، ولكن فقراء ذوي القربى من كل صنف يقدمون على الذين يدخلون فيهم، وهو الأصح، وهو اختيار الكرخي، وقال الطحاوي: الفقير منهم؛ سهم قبيل الصدقة، فلا يحل لهم كأغنيائهم، وقوله: "ولا حق لأغنيائهم" أي أغنياء ذوي القربى، خلافاً للشافعي كما تقدم، ومثل قوله قال مالك وأحمد. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) سقط
And the khums is for orphans, the poor, and the wayfarer, and the relatives of the Prophet are given precedence. This is the beginning of explaining the ruling of the khums that the Imam first separates. That is, the khums is divided into three shares: a share for orphans, a share for the poor, and a share for the wayfarer; each is given a share, due to the Most High's saying: 'And know that whatever you take as spoils, then indeed, for Allah is one-fifth of it and for the Messenger and for the near relatives and for the orphans and for the needy and for the wayfarer' (al-Anfal 41). According to al-Shafi'i: He divides it into five-fifths: a share for the near relatives, a share for the Prophet (which the Imam uses after him for Muslim interests), and the rest for the three categories. The near relatives, rich and poor, are equal, and it is divided among them with the male getting twice the female, and it is for Banu al-Muttalib, not others, because the Book and Sunnah do not distinguish between rich and poor. Our proof: The four caliphs divided the khums into three shares as we say, and they are sufficient as examples. And the Prophet said: 'O Banu Hashim, verily Allah has disliked for you the washing of people and their filth, and He has compensated you for it with the fifth of the fifth.' Compensation is only established for those who have the right to compensation, and they are the poor. And it is narrated that 'Ali and 'Uthman from Banu 'Abd Shams and Jubayr ibn Mut'im from Banu Nawfal came to him and said: We do not deny the virtue of Banu Hashim, but we and Banu al-Muttalib are equal in kinship to you. Why did you give them and deprive us? He said: 'They are not like this' and he interlocked his fingers, 'in Jahiliyya and Islam.' This indicates that what is intended by the text is nearness in support (nusra), not nearness in kinship. So the division of the caliphs in the presence of the Companions was a consensus. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq 1/426] (Mustakhlas, Fath) And the relatives are given precedence. The pronoun in 'minhum' refers to the relatives, and in 'alayhim' to the three categories, i.e., the poor of the Prophet's relatives are given precedence over the three mentioned categories. The conclusion is that orphans of the relatives enter the share of orphans, the poor of the relatives enter the share of the poor, and wayfarers of the relatives enter the share of wayfarers, but the poor of the relatives from each category are given precedence over those who enter among them. This is the most sound view, and it is the choice of al-Karkhi. al-Tahawi said: the poor among them have a share that is a type of charity, so it is not permissible for their rich, as is the case for them. And his statement: 'there is no right for their rich' means the rich of the relatives, contrary to al-Shafi'i as mentioned, and Malik and Ahmad said the same. (Mustakhlas, Fath, Ayni) Sakat.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage explains the division of the khums (the one-fifth portion of spoils allocated for specific beneficiaries). The Hanafi position is that the khums is divided into three shares: one for orphans (yatama), one for the poor (masakin), and one for wayfarers (ibn al-sabil). The verse from Surah al-Anfal is cited as the primary proof. The school differs from Imam al-Shafi'i, who divides the khums into five shares, including a separate share for the Prophet's relatives (dhawu al-qurba) and for the Prophet himself (which the Imam uses for Muslim interests). The Hanafi school argues that the practice of the four rightly-guided caliphs was to divide into three shares, and this consensus (ijma') is strong evidence. Furthermore, a hadith indicates that compensation for the Prophet's relatives is only for the needy among them, and the story of 'Ali, 'Uthman, and Jubayr shows that nearness in support (nusra), not just blood kinship, is the criterion. The passage further clarifies that within the three shares, the poor of the Prophet's relatives are given priority. For example, if there are both poor relatives and other poor people, the relatives receive first. This is the preferred view (al-azhar) and the choice of al-Karkhi. al-Tahawi held a different view, but the relied-upon position is that the relatives' poor get precedence.

Key Terms
الخمس (al-khumus): The one-fifth portion of spoils of war that is set aside for specific charitable beneficiaries: orphans, the poor, and wayfarers (with precedence for the Prophet's poor relatives).
ذوو القربى (dhawū al-qurbā): The relatives of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him), specifically the needy among them according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school divides khums into three shares, with the poor of the Prophet's relatives given priority, based on caliphal consensus and hadith evidence.

Radkh: The Discretionary Grant for Non-Combatants
Ruling on radkh with proof and rebuttal of an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on radkh, distinguish it from sahm, and identify which non-combatants qualify for radkh.

Core Idea
Non-combatants receive discretionary grants (radkh), not full shares—except when their service is exceptional.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains how spoils are divided in Hanafi law. While combatants earn full shares (sahm), non-combatants such as women nurses, guides, and others receive a discretionary grant called radkh. The amount is set by the Imam based on contribution. We will walk through the categories, the proof, and a key objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main rule
Read the first sentence: 'And the non-combatant [gets] radkh, not a share.' This is the core distinction. Note that khums (one-fifth) is set aside for orphans, the poor, wayfarers, and the Prophet's relatives.
2
List the categories eligible for radkh
The passage specifies: those non-combatants who fight (e.g., women nursing the wounded), women treating wounds (considered equivalent to fighting), and dhimmis who fight or guide. Each has a different basis.
Women's nursing is 'qaim maqam al-qital'—takes the place of fighting.
3
Analyze the dhimmi guide exception
A dhimmi guide may receive more than a full share if his guidance is extremely beneficial. This is treated as a wage, not a spoil. The Imam can give any amount.
This overrides the general rule that radkh never reaches the level of sahm.
4
Evaluate the objection and Hanafi response
The text raises that Ali (RA) gave shares to Jews and children in some narrations. The Hanafi response is that those are classified as radkh, not full shares. This shows the school's method of reconciling evidence.
This defense uses the principle of haml (interpretation) to preserve the rule.
5
Derive the underlying principle
The Hanafi principle is that only actual combatants get sahm; others get radkh at the Imam's discretion. The amount is based on contribution, not entitlement.
This reflects the school's emphasis on precision in defining combatant status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب السير والجهاد
كيفية القسمة
والدمّي الرضحُ، لا السهم، والخمس لليتامى والمساكين، وابن السبيل، وقدم ذوو القربى
ويرضخ لهؤلاء إذا كانوا يقاتلون، والمرأة وإن كانت عاجزة عن القتال طبعاً، ولكن مداواتها الجرحي قائم مقام القتال، والذمي لا يعطى له إلا بالقتال أو بدلالته على الطريق، وقوله: "لا" "السهم أي لا يعطى لهؤلاء السهم؛ لما روينا، ولا يبلغ بالرضخ السهم إلا في دلالة الذمي، فإنه يزاد على السهم إذا كان في دلالته منفعة عظيمة؛ لأن ما يأخذه أجرة فيعطى بالغاً ما بلغ. فإن قلت: روى الترمذي أنه علي أسهم لقوم من اليهود قاتلوا معه وللصبيان، وروى أحمد وأبو داود والنسائي أيضاً. قلت: كله محمول على الرضخ [رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٦/١] (فتح)
الرضخ: بالمعجمتين وهو النصيب يعطي لهم على حسب ما يراه الإمام. (عيني)
The book of Siyar and Jihad. The method of division. And the non-combatant [gets] radkh, not a share. And the khums is for orphans, the poor, and the wayfarer. And the relatives [of the Prophet] are given precedence. And discretionary grant is given to these [non-combatants] if they fight. And the woman, even if she is by nature incapable of fighting, but her treatment of the wounded takes the place of fighting. And the dhimmi is not given [a share] except for fighting or for guiding the way. And his saying: 'No' to the share means that these are not given a full share, due to what we have narrated. And the discretionary grant does not reach the level of a full share except in the case of a dhimmi's guidance. For he is increased beyond a full share if his guidance is of great benefit, because what he takes is a wage, so he is given whatever amount it reaches. If you say: al-Tirmidhi narrated that Ali gave shares to a group of Jews who fought with him and to children, and Ahmad, Abu Dawud, and al-Nasa'i also narrated it. I say: All of that is interpreted as discretionary grant (radkh). [Reference from Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/426] (Fath) Al-radkh: with two dotted letters, it is the portion given to them according to the Imam's discretion. (Ayni)
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof from the book of Jihad on the division of spoils. The passage begins by stating the general rule: non-combatants (al-dammi) receive a discretionary grant (radkh) and not a full share (sahm). The khums (one fifth) is reserved for specific categories: orphans, the poor, and wayfarers, with precedence given to the Prophet's relatives. The text then explains that discretionary grants are given to non-combatants who participate in fighting. Women, though physically weak, are considered as contributing to combat when they treat wounds, so they qualify for radkh. A dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) receives nothing unless he fights or acts as a guide. The grant cannot reach the level of a full share unless the dhimmi's guidance is exceptionally beneficial, in which case he may receive more because it is treated as wages. The passage then addresses an objection: why were shares given to Jews and children in narrations from Ali? The Hanafi response is that those grants are classified as radkh, not full shares. Finally, the definition of radkh is given: a portion given at the Imam's discretion. The Hanafi position here is that radkh is distinct from the full share (sahm) which is reserved for actual combatants.

Key Terms
الرضخ (al-radkh): A discretionary grant given to non-combatants and others who assist in war, less than a full share.
السهم (al-sahm): A full share of the spoils given to combatants.
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who receives protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Less than a full share always.
C) More than a full share if his guidance is very beneficial.
D) Nothing at all.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, non-combatants receive a discretionary grant (radkh) determined by the Imam, while full shares (sahm) are reserved for actual combatants.

Khums Division: The Hanafi Position and Dispute with al-Shafi'i
Detailed ruling on khums division with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on dividing khums into three shares, contrast it with the Shafi'i five-share view, and cite the proofs from the practice of the caliphs and hadith.

Core Idea
Khums is divided into three shares; the Prophet's share and safi lapsed; poor relatives are prioritized over other recipients.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq and its commentaries outlines the Hanafi position on the distribution of the khums (one-fifth of war spoils). It addresses the number of shares, the eligibility of the Prophet's relatives, and the fate of the Prophet's special entitlements. The discussion includes a detailed rebuttal of the Shafi'i five-share division and other minority views.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify that the khums is divided into three shares: for orphans, the poor, and wayfarers. The Prophet's share and his right to select an item (safi) lapsed after his death.
This is based on the verse (8:41) and the practice of the four caliphs.
2
Identify the Hanafi dalil
Note the proof from the practice of the four caliphs (consensus) and the hadith where the Prophet told Banu Hashim that they were compensated with the khums of the khums, indicating it is for the poor.
The hadith of Ali, Uthman, and Jubayr shows the criterion is support, not kinship.
3
Understand the Shafi'i position
The Shafi'i school divides khums into five shares: for Allah, the Messenger, near relatives, orphans, poor, wayfarers. The Prophet's share passes to the Imam, and relatives receive even if rich.
They rely on the same verse but interpret the shares literally.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Share of Allah is for blessing. The Prophet's share lapsed with his death because it was attached to prophethood. The relatives only receive if poor, as shown by the compensation in the hadith.
The caliphs' division in the presence of companions constitutes ijma.
5
Note the priority of poor relatives
Poor relatives of the Prophet are given precedence over the general categories: orphan relatives, poor relatives, and wayfarer relatives each take priority in their respective groups.
This is the preferred view of al-Karkhi, and it is the mufta bihi.

والخمس لليتامى إلخ [ أي ويقسم الخمس المفرز بعد قسمة أربعة الأخماس على ثلاثة أسهم ويعطى لكل واحد منهم سهم وجاز صرفه لصنف واحد ولو صرفه للغانمين لحاجتهم جاز (ط، عيني)] شروع في بيان حكم الخمس الذي كان أفرزه الإمام أولاً، أي يقسم الخمس على ثلاثة أسهم: سهم لليتامى، وسهم للمساكين، وسهم لابن السبيل، فيعطى لكل واحد منهم سهم؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّمَا غَنِمْتُمْ مِنْ شَيْءٍ فَأَنَّ اللَّهُ حُمُسَهُ وَلِلرَّسُولِ وَلِذِي الْقُرْبَى وَالْيَتَامَى وَالْمَسَاكِينِ وَابْنِ السَّبِيلِ ﴾ (الأنفال:٤١)، وعند الشافعي: يخمس أخماساً: سهم لذوي القربى وسهم للنبي عليه يخلفه فيه الإمام، ويصرفه إلى مصالح المسلمين، والباقي للثلاثة، ويستوي في ذوي القربى فقيرهم وغنيهم، ويقسم بينهم للذكر مثل حظ الأنثيين، ويكون ذلك لبني المطلب، ولا يكون لغيرهم؛ لأنه لم يفرق في الكتاب ولا في السنة بين الغني والفقير. ولنا: أن الخلفاء الأربعة قسموا الخمس على ثلاثة أسهم كما قلنا وكفى بهم قدوة، وقال عليه: "يا معشر بني هاشم إن الله كره لكم غسالة الناس وأوساخهم، وعوضكم منها بخمس الخمس"، والعوض إنما يثبت في حق من يثبت له المعوض وهم الفقراء، وورد أنه جاءه علي عثمان الله من بني عبد شمس، وجبير بن مطعم من بني نوفل فقالا: إنا لا ننكر فضل بني هاشم، لكن نحن وبنو المطلب في القرابة إليك سواء فما بالك أعطيتهم وأحرمتنا؟ فقال: "إنهم لا هكذا" وشبك بين أصابعه في الجاهلية والإسلام"، فدل أن المراد من النص قرب النصرة لا قرب القرابة، [هاشم وبني يزالوا معي؟] فكان قسمة الخلفاء بمحضر من الصحابة، فكان إجماعاً [ رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٦/١] (مستخلص، فتح) وقدم ذوو القربى إلخ الضمير في "منهم" يرجع إلى ذوي القربى، وفي "عليهم" إلى الأصناف الثلاثة، أي قدم الفقراء من ذوي قرابة النبي عليه على الأصناف الثلاثة المذكورة، والحاصل أن يتامى ذوي القربى يدخلون في سهم اليتامى، ومساكين ذوي القربي يدخلون في سهم المساكين، وأبناء السبيل يدخلون في سهم أبناء السبيل، ولكن فقراء ذوي القربى من كل صنف يقدمون على الذين يدخلون فيهم، وهو الأصح، وهو اختيار الكرخي، وقال الطحاوي: الفقير منهم؛ سهم قبيل الصدقة، فلا يحل لهم كأغنيائهم، وقوله: "ولا حق لأغنيائهم" أي أغنياء ذوي القربى، خلافاً للشافعي كما تقدم، ومثل قوله قال مالك وأحمد. (مستخلص، فتح، عيني) سقط لأنه من بني هاشم أي أغنياء ذوي القربي فقراء بالرفع بدل من قوله: ذوو القربى (عيني)] أي فقراءهم على الأصناف الثلاثة (ط، عيني) منهم عليهم: أي على الأصناف الثلاثة وهم اليتامى والمساكين وابن السبيل. (عيني) وذكر الله تعالى للتبرك: أي ذكر الله تعالى في قوله: ﴿وَاعْلَمُوا أَنَّمَا غَنِمْتُمْ مِنْ شَيْءٍ فَأَنَّ لله حُمُسَه اله للتبرك، يعني لإخراج الكلام تبركاً بذكره، وهذا احتراز عن قول أبي العالية، فإنه قال: يقسم الخمس على ستة سهام، وسهم الله تعالى يصرف إلى عمارة الكعبة إن كانت القسمة بقربها، وإلى عمارة الجامع في كل بلدة تقرب من موضع القسمة؛ لأن هذه البقاع مضافة إلى الله تعالى، فهذا السهم يصرف إليها، وعن قول أحمد فإنه قال: يقسم على خمسة أسهم الرسول يصرف في سد الثغور وأرزاق الغزاة والقضاة وكري الأنهار وبناء الجسور. (مستخلص، معدن) قول الشافعي أن الله وسهم وسهم "وسهم النبي عليه إلخ لأنه حكم علق بمشتق، يعني الحكم في قوله تعالى: وَلِرَسُولِهِ (المنافقون:۸) مرتب على المشتق، فيدل على علة مأخذ الاشتقاق وهو الرسالة كما هو المشهور ولا رسول بعده وبه خرج الجواب عن سهم الرسول عليه للخليفة حيث ينتهي الرسالة، وقوله: "كالصفي بفتح الصاد وكسر الفاء والياء هو ما كان للنبي عليه يصطفيه لنفسه من الغنيمة من درع أو سيف أو جارية، كما اصطفى صفية بنت حيي بن أخطب من غنائم خيبر ثم أعتقها وتزوجها، واصطفى ذا الفقار من غنائم البدر، أي كما سقط الصفي فلا يصطفى الإمام لنفسه شيئاً من الغنيمة، وهذا مجمع عليه رمز الحقائق: ٤٢٧/١] (مستخلص، فتح) بموته لأنه علي كان يستحقه بالرسالة ولا رسول بعده. (عيني) كالصفي: أي كما سقط الصفي الذي كان عليها يصطفيه لنفسه من الغنيمة. (ط، عيني)
And the khums is for orphans etc. [i.e., the khums set aside after dividing the four-fifths is divided into three shares: a share for orphans, a share for the poor, and a share for the wayfarer. It is permissible to give it all to one category, and if he gives it to the combatants due to need, it is permissible. (Tahtawi, Ayni)] Beginning of the explanation of the ruling of the khums that the Imam set aside initially. He divides the khums into three shares: a share for orphans, a share for the poor, and a share for the wayfarer. Each is given a share due to the verse: 'And know that whatever you take as spoils, indeed for Allah is one fifth of it and for the Messenger and for the near relative and the orphans and the poor and the wayfarer.' (Anfal 8:41). According to al-Shafi'i: He divides it into five shares: a share for the near relatives, a share for the Prophet (which the Imam takes over and spends for the general welfare of Muslims), and the rest is for the three categories. He treats the rich and poor among the near relatives equally, divides it among them with males receiving twice the share of females, and it belongs solely to Banu al-Muttalib, not others, because no distinction is made in the Book or Sunnah between rich and poor. Our proof: The four caliphs divided the khums into three shares as we say, and they are sufficient as role models. And he (the Prophet) said: 'O Banu Hashim, indeed Allah dislikes for you the washings and impurities of people, and has compensated you with the khums of the khums.' Compensation is only for those who are entitled to what is compensated, and they are the poor. And it is narrated that Ali and Uthman (may Allah be pleased with them) from Banu Abd Shams, and Jubayr ibn Mut'im from Banu Nawfal came to him and said: 'We do not deny the virtue of Banu Hashim, but we and Banu al-Muttalib are equal in relation to you in kinship. Why have you given them and deprived us?' He said: 'They [Hashim and Muttalib] are not like that' and he interlocked his fingers, meaning they remained together in Jahiliyyah and Islam. So it indicated that what is meant by the text is nearness in support, not nearness in kinship. [...] So the division of the caliphs was in the presence of the Companions, thus it was consensus. [...] And he gave precedence to the poor among the relatives: the pronoun refers to the near relatives, and 'upon them' refers to the three categories. So the poor among the Prophet's relatives are given precedence over the three mentioned categories. The conclusion is that orphans among the relatives are included in the share of orphans, poor among relatives in the share of the poor, and wayfarers among relatives in the share of wayfarers. But the poor of the relatives from each category are given precedence over those who enter into them. This is the most correct view, the choice of al-Karkhi. Al-Tahawi said: The poor among them: the share is akin to charity, so it is not permissible for their rich. And his saying: 'And no right for their rich' meaning the rich among the relatives, contrary to al-Shafi'i as mentioned. Malik and Ahmad said the same. [...] And he mentioned 'and for Allah' for blessing: meaning mentioning Allah in His statement 'for Allah is one fifth' is for blessing, to bring out the speech with His mention. This is a refutation of the statement of Abu al-Aliyah who said: the khums is divided into six shares, and the share of Allah is spent on the construction of the Ka'bah if the division is near it, or on the construction of the congregational mosque in every town near the place of division, because these places are attributed to Allah. And a refutation of Ahmad who said it is divided into five shares, the Prophet's share is spent on guarding borders, salaries of warriors and judges, digging rivers, and building bridges. [...] The statement 'and the share of the Prophet etc.' because it is a ruling attached to an derived term, meaning the ruling in the verse 'and for His Messenger' (Munaafiqun 63:8) is attached to the derived term, indicating that the cause of the derivation is the message, as is well known, and there is no messenger after him. With this, the answer about the share of the Prophet being given to the caliph is derived, because the message ends. And his statement: 'like the safi' (with fatha on saad, kasra on fa, ya) is what the Prophet used to select for himself from the spoils, such as a coat of armor, a sword, or a slave girl, as he selected Safiyyah bint Huyayy ibn Akhtab from the spoils of Khaybar, then freed her and married her, and selected Dhu al-Fiqar from the spoils of Badr. So just as the safi lapsed, the Imam does not select anything for himself from the spoils. This is agreed upon. [...] 'like the safi: i.e., just as the safi which the Prophet used to select for himself from the spoils lapsed.'
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This is a detailed ruling on the division of khums (the fifth of the spoils), including an inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi position, following the practice of the four caliphs, is that the khums is divided into three shares: one each for orphans, the poor, and wayfarers. Al-Shafi'i divides it into five shares: a share for the Prophet's relatives (Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib, specifically), a share for the Prophet (which the Imam uses for general Islamic benefits), and the remaining three for the same three categories. The Hanafis provide several proofs: (1) The practice of the rightly-guided caliphs, which amounts to consensus. (2) A hadith where the Prophet said to Banu Hashim that Allah compensated them with the khums of the khums, which implies compensation only for the poor, not the rich. (3) A hadith where the Prophet distinguished between Banu Hashim and Banu al-Muttalib from other Quraysh, showing that the relevant criterion is support, not kinship. The text then explains the priority of the poor among the Prophet's relatives over other recipients in each category. This is the preferred view of al-Karkhi, although al-Tahawi held that the share for the poor relatives is like charity and thus forbidden for their rich. The discussion also clarifies that the mention of 'Allah' in the verse is for blessing and does not imply a separate share for Him, refuting Abu al-Aliyah and Ahmad ibn Hanbal who proposed additional shares. Finally, it addresses the fate of the Prophet's share after his death: it lapses (as does the safi, the Prophet's special selection), because the share was attached to the office of prophethood, which ceased. The Imam cannot take that share.

Key Terms
الخمس (al-khums): One fifth of the spoils of war set aside for specific recipients.
سهم الرسول (sahm al-rasul): The share of the Prophet in the khums. After his death, it lapses.
الصفي (al-safi): The item the Prophet used to select for himself from the spoils before division. Also lapsed after him.
ذي القربى (dhu al-qurba): Near relatives of the Prophet. In Hanafi law, they receive khums only if poor, and are given priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the fate of the Prophet's share (sahm al-rasul) from the khums after his death?
A) It continues and is given to the Imam for public welfare.
B) It lapses and is no longer a separate share; the khums is divided into three shares.
C) It is given to the Prophet's relatives (Banu Hashim).
D) It is added to the share of the poor.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school divides khums into three shares (orphans, poor, wayfarers), with the Prophet's share and safi lapsed, and poor relatives receiving priority.

Spoils Taken Without Permission: The Role of Group Strength (Man`ah)
Ruling on khums for spoils taken without Imam's permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khums for spoils taken without Imam's permission and apply the condition of man`ah (group strength) to determine whether the spoils are ghanimah.

Core Idea
Spoils taken without permission become ghanimah only if the group has strength to overpower; otherwise it is theft.

When Muslims enter enemy territory without the Imam's permission, the spoils they take are not automatically ghanimah. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the group's strength (man`ah). If the group is strong enough to resemble a formal military expedition, the spoils are subject to khums; otherwise they are considered theft. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the evidence, and the disagreement with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Disputed Element
The act is a group of Muslims entering dar al-harb without the Imam's permission and taking property. The disputed element is whether this property is ghanimah (subject to khums) or theft.
The Arabic phrase 'ذو منعة' is key: a group possessing strength.
2
State the Hanafi Position
According to the Hanafis, if the group has man`ah (strength, e.g., ten or more per Abu Yusuf), the spoils are ghanimah and khums is wajib. The permission of the Imam is not required because the Imam is obliged to support them and their strength makes their action akin to a formal expedition.
Man`ah is defined as power to overcome the enemy; Abu Yusuf set the minimum at ten.
3
Analyse the Proof (Dalil Aqli)
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to a regular military expedition. The effective cause (illah) is taking by force and conquest (qahr and ghalaba). Since the group has strength, their taking is by force, so the spoils are ghanimah. The Imam's permission is not part of the illah.
This is a qiyas jali: the asl is a formal expedition, the far' is this group.
4
Contrast with the Opposing School
The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools require the Imam's permission for khums. Their reasoning is that the Imam's authorization legitimises the expedition. The Hanafi response is that the obligation to support Muslims (nusrah) overrides the permission requirement when the group has strength.
Practical difference: under non-Hanafi schools, khums is not due without permission regardless of strength.
5
Apply the Ruling to Variations
If the group lacks strength (e.g., one or two individuals), the spoils are not ghanimah and no khums is due. However, if they had permission, the well-known Hanafi position imposes khums even for one or two, because permission implies commitment to support.
This shows the interaction between permission and strength: permission makes even a weak group equivalent to a strong one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن دخل جمع ذو منعة دارهم بلا إذن، خُمس ما أخذوا، أي جماعة من المسلمين أهل الحرب أي بلا إذن الإمام وإلا لا، الفقراء بالرفع بدل من قوله: ذوو القربى (عيني)] أي فقراءهم على الأصناف الثلاثة (ط، عيني) منهم عليهم: أي على الأصناف الثلاثة وهم اليتامى والمساكين وابن السبيل. (عيني) وذكر الله تعالى للتبرك... (مستخلص، معدن) قول الشافعي أن المشددة، الله وسهم وسهم "وسهم النبي عليه إلخ... (مستخلص، فتح) بموته لأنه علي كان يستحقه بالرسالة ولا رسول بعده. (عيني) كالصفي: أي كما سقط الصفي الذي كان عليها يصطفيه لنفسه من الغنيمة. (ط، عيني) وإن دخل جمع إلخ: عن أبي يوسف أنه قدر الجماعة التي لا قوة لها بسبعة، والتي لها قوة بعشرة. ذو منعة: بفتحتين وقد تسكن النون أي قوة، وقيد بقوله: "بلا إذن؛ لأنهم لو دخلوا بإذن الإمام، فالمشهور أنه يخمس؛ لأنه لما أذن لهم الإمام فقد التزم نصرتهم بالإمداد، والمعنى إذا دخل جماعة لها منعة، فأخذوا شيئاً، خمس وإن لم يأذن لهم الإمام؛ لأنه مأخوذ قهراً وغلبة، فكانت غنيمة، ولأنه يجب على الإمام نصرتهم؛ إذ لو خذلهم كان فيه وهنا بالمسلمين، وعند الثلاثة لا يخمس إلا بالإذن فقط. وإلا لا: أي وإن لم يكن الجمع صاحب منعة، كما إذا دخل الواحد أو الاثنان دار الحرب مغيرين بغير إذن الإمام لم يخمس؛ لأنه اختلاس وسرقة، وليس بغنيمة؛ لأنها المال المأخوذ قهراً، ولو دخل واحد واثنان بإذن، وأخذوا شيئاً، لا يخمس في رواية، وعلى المشهور يخمس؛ لأنه بالإذن التزم نصرتهم بالإمداد، فصار كالمنعة. (عيني، مستخلص)
And if a group possessing strength enters their [the enemy's] abode without permission, one fifth is due on what they took. That is, a group of Muslims, people of war, i.e., without the Imam's permission. Otherwise, no. [...] And if a group possessing strength enters: from Abu Yusuf that he estimated the group that has no strength as seven, and the one with strength as ten. 'Possessing strength' (dhu man`ah) means power. It is conditioned by 'without permission' because if they entered with the Imam's permission, the well-known ruling is that it is subject to khums, because when the Imam permitted them, he committed to their support by sending reinforcements. The meaning is: if a group with strength enters and takes something, it is subject to khums even if the Imam did not permit them, because it is taken by force and conquest (qahr and ghalaba), so it is ghanimah. And because the Imam is obligated to support them, for if he were to abandon them, that would cause weakness among the Muslims. According to the three (other schools), it is only subject to khums if there is permission. Otherwise, no: meaning if the group is not one of strength, such as if one or two individuals enter the dar al-harb to raid without the Imam's permission, it is not subject to khums, because it is snatching and theft, not ghanimah, because ghanimah is property taken by force overwhelming. But if one or two enter with permission and take something, there is a narration that it is not subject to khums, but the well-known ruling is that it is subject to khums, because with permission he committed to their support, so it becomes like a group of strength. (Ayni, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This is a ruling on the status of spoils taken by a group of Muslims who enter enemy territory without the Imam's permission. The key factor is the strength (man`ah) of the group. If the group is strong enough to overpower (Abu Yusuf suggests ten people or more), then what they take is considered ghanimah (spoils of war) and must be treated as such, i.e., one fifth is taken as khums before distribution to the fighters. This applies even if the Imam did not give permission, because the Imam is obligated to support them in any case. If the group lacks such strength (e.g., one or two individuals), their action is considered theft, not a legitimate military expedition, and the property is not ghanimah; thus no khums is due. The passage also notes a dispute: according to the three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali), khums is only due if the Imam had granted permission. The Hanafi position is that necessity and the obligation to support Muslims overrides the permission requirement. If the Imam does give permission, even one or two individuals' action becomes ghanimah, and khums is due (the well-known view).

Key Terms
منعة (man`ah): Strength or power; a group possessing man`ah is one that can potentially overpower the enemy.
غلبة (ghalaba): Conquest or overwhelming force; the characteristic of ghanimah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum number that indicates a group has strength (man`ah) according to Abu Yusuf?
A) 7
B) 10
C) 14
D) 5

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the key to khums on spoils taken without permission is the group's strength (man`ah), not the Imam's permission.

Khums for a Group Entering Enemy Territory Without Permission
Ruling on whether a group of Muslims entering enemy territory without the Imam's permission must pay khums.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khums for a group entering enemy territory without the Imam's permission and explain the distinction between spoils and plunder.

Core Idea
Khums applies to spoils taken by force; without permission, only a group with strength is liable.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a nuanced question in the laws of jihad: when must Muslims pay khums (one-fifth) on goods taken from enemy territory? The author distinguishes between a group with strength (man'ah) and individuals or small parties. The ruling hinges on whether the wealth is considered legitimate spoils (ghanimah) or mere plunder (ikhtilas).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (entering enemy territory without permission) and the specific ruling on khums (obligatory or not).
The passage distinguishes between groups with strength and those without.
2
Distinguish between spoils and plunder
Understand the definition of ghanimah (spoils) as wealth taken by force (qahran). Plunder (ikhtilas) is not considered spoils and is not subject to khums.
This distinction is key to the ruling.
3
Analyze the condition of strength (man'ah)
Examine why strength matters: a group with man'ah indicates a military operation, making the taken property ghanimah even without permission.
Abu Yusuf defined man'ah as ten or more individuals.
4
Compare the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on permission
Note the difference of narration regarding one or two individuals entering with permission: one narration says no khums, but the mashhur (famous) ruling imposes khums because permission implies the Imam's commitment to support them, making them analogous to a strong group.
The mashhur is the relied-upon position.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab dispute
Contrast the Hanafi position with the other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) which require the Imam's permission for khums to be applicable. Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with valid proofs on both sides.

وإلا لا: أي وإن لم يكن الجمع صاحب منعة، كما إذا دخل الواحد أو الاثنان دار الحرب مغيرين بغير إذن الإمام لم يخمس؛ لأنه اختلاس وسرقة، وليس بغنيمة؛ لأنها المال المأخوذ قهراً، ولو دخل واحد واثنان بإذن، وأخذوا شيئاً، لا يخمس في رواية، وعلى المشهور يخمس؛ لأنه بالإذن التزم نصرتهم بالإمداد، فصار كالمنعة. (عيني، مستخلص)
Otherwise no: meaning if the group is not one of strength, like when one or two enter the abode of war as raiders without the Imam's permission, they do not pay khums because it is plunder and theft, and it is not spoils because spoils are wealth taken by force. And if one or two enter with permission and take something, they do not pay khums in one narration, but according to the famous ruling they do pay khums because by the permission he committed to support them by reinforcements, so it becomes like [a group of] strength. (Ayni, Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on entering enemy territory. The author clarifies the condition of 'without permission' from the previous ruling. If the group does not have strength (e.g., one or two individuals) and enters without permission, they do not pay khums because it is considered plunder, not spoils. However, if they enter with the Imam's permission, there is a difference of narration: in one, they do not pay khums; in the famous ruling (al-mashhur), they do, because the Imam's permission implies a commitment to support them, making their action analogous to a group with strength. The ruling emphasizes that khums applies to wealth taken by force in a military context.

Key Terms
منعة (man'ah): Strength or power of a group; here defined by Abu Yusuf as ten or more.
اختلاس (ikhtilas): Plunder or theft, as opposed to legitimate spoils taken by force.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a single Muslim enters enemy territory without the Imam's permission and takes property?
A) He must pay khums
B) He does not pay khums because it is considered plunder
C) He must give the property to the Imam
D) It depends on the value

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, khums on enemy spoils depends on whether the property was taken by force by a group with strength, not simply on the presence of the Imam's permission.

Rulings on Nafal (Extra Grants) in Jihad
Rulings on the Imam's permission to grant extra incentives (nafal) to soldiers during battle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for nafal and explain the Hanafi istihsan ruling regarding the Imam's inclusion in the reward.

Core Idea
The Imam may grant extra spoils before ihraz, and by istihsan he may include himself in the offer.

This passage establishes the rules for nafal—extra incentives the Imam can offer to soldiers to encourage bravery in battle. The Imam may promise a slain enemy's weapons to the killer or grant a share of spoils to a detachment. The key legal issues are the timing of the offer (before spoils are secured) and whether the Imam can benefit from his own announcement. Through prophetic practice and juristic reasoning, the Hanafi school resolves the latter via istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognise that nafal is an extra grant beyond the regular share. The ruling is recommended (mandub) to motivate fighters.
It is not obligatory; the Imam has discretion.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof includes the hadith 'Whoever kills a slain person and has proof, he gets his weapon' and the Prophet's practice of granting nafal.
These are hadith ahad with zanni thubut.
3
Understand the condition of ihraz
Nafal is valid only before spoils are brought to safety (ihraz). After ihraz, the Imam can only grant from the khums because participants' rights are fixed.
Before ihraz, the Imam may give from the entire spoils.
4
Analyse the qiyas vs istihsan on the Imam's inclusion
By qiyas, the Imam would not deserve the salab because his statement would exclude him. By istihsan, he does deserve it because it is a matter of spoils entitlement, not a judicial declaration.
The text prefers istihsan over qiyas.
5
Note exceptions for who qualifies for salab
The killed person must be a legitimate target (not women, children, insane). Special categories like patients, hired soldiers, and treaty-breakers are valid targets.
The killer's entitlement is conditional upon the legitimacy of the kill.
